Though the company had helped popularize the bike water bottle and developed a huge market share, competitors were starting to nip at Specialized's heels.

To begin our search for new ideas, we watched bike racers and serious bikers using water bottles. This was particularly easy for us, since for years IDEO has had a pretty serious Wednesday night bike ride into the foothills above Stanford. Soon it was clear that there was a problem with water bottles, a problem similar to what we'd faced with the Danish phone. Eyes on the road, bikers often looked like relay racers missing the hand-off as they struggled to stick their bottles back into their bottle cage. That insight helped us to design an improved bottle with a tapered bottom—and a rubber friction ring to make the bottle easier to grip.

A second issue was trickicr. Watching riders, we saw that taking a drink from a water bottle is a clumsy two-step dance—you have to draw the nozzle out with your teeth beforc you can squirt the water. This process is even more awkward when the bottle gets covered with dirt or mud from the trail.

The solution, as is so often the casc, came from looking at products

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/daad5e5ba720a7d6507138be96682f58b0df5c22704716206f95215880fa4a5f.jpg)

Watching mountain bikers inspired a water bottle that keeps mud out of their mouths.

used for entirely different purposes. Our creative team members perused a range of innovations, including one of nature's clever designs, the tricuspid heart valve. Three triangular patches of tissue open and close the valves of the heart. Could a heart valve inspire a sports bottle? we wondered. What if you sealed the top of the bottle with a rubber septum that opens like a heart valve? Asking that question led the team to design a simple self-sealing valve at the mouth of the bottle. A rubber septum cut with an "X" let the water flow more rapidly than in previous bottles. When you stopped squeezing, the septum would reseal, not letting anything in. The bottle was always ready for a quick drink, but its contents would not spill. Or get overly dirty. All you' need to do to get a drink wvas grab the bottle and squeeze—a simple one-step dance. And you'd never have to stick your mouth around the muddy spout.

I think the Racer's Edge water bottle is a great example of how even what seems like a static product can be improved if you see it in motion. Remember the idea of thinking in terms of verbs rather than nouns? One way to think of the improvements in the new Specialized bike bottle is through verbs-opens simpler, pours faster, and stores smoothly. Try it yourself. Watch your products and services on the run. You might find a problem or opportunity you didn't expect.

## CROSS-POLLINATE

As you can see, it's critical to watch people in motion to fully understand a problem. It's a great way to look for ideas that leap traditional boundaries. We think this "cross-pollination" approach is a kind of alchemy of innovation. I'll be talking about it in depth a little later in this book, but I'd like to briefly give you a sense of how it often works at IDEO. Think back to that heavy medical device we talked about earlier. Before it was set to go to manufacturing, we spent some time observing nurses. They'd control the electronic device with a computer stylus tethered to the machine. When a nurse was ready to change into a monitoring mode, she'd gather up the cord and snap the stylus back inside an enclosure. But sometimes after the nurses used the stylus, they'd look for somewhere convenient to temporarily store it, rather than put it back properly inside the enclosure.

We suddenly realized the stylus was like an old fountain pen looking for a holder. A team member wondered aloud whether the rubber septum we used in the Specialized water bottlc might offer a better answer for temporarily storing the stylus as well. We had only a fewv days left before thc final design was to be sent to manufacturing, so we tried a quick fix. The tcam punched a small hole in the screen's lower border and inserted a rubber septum. Sure cnough, the septum made an ideal pen holder. So we ran with it. A minor improvement, you might think, but the kind of improvement that can only come about from watching people in action.

So many products and services have flaws that we start to assume that's just the way things are. But if people are frustrated or confused by a product or service you offer, eventually they may be wooed away when a better product or service becomes available. At the very least, rather than creating positive buzz about your product, they will say nothing at all or complain about it. That's why it's critical that you continue to innovate and improve. And the best way to do that is to watch people use your products.

Think about how frustrating and embarrassing the experience of buying a new or used car can be. For decades we—and the car companies—assumed there was no alternative. No one imagined you could buy a car without creeping anxiety that you were being cheated by fast-talking salespeople. When the Saturn came along with fixed pricing and a low-stress no-haggling approach, GM was obviously offering a completely different buying experience. People liked it. And with the advent of the Internet, the way cars are bought ischanging once again.

The same is true of rental cars. Returning a rental car used to be an unpleasant and time-consuming task. Racing to get to the airport, you often worried whether a line at the rental counter would cause you to miss your plane. Now the process takes seconds. The mobile agent waves you down, enters your mileage into a handheld portable, and away you go. You might say that this innovation wasn't possible until portable computing devices reached a "wcarable" size, but that's not copletely true. The rental car companis could have et p portable stations in the parking lot decades ago. The fact that it was easier to do as technology got smallcr and more portable is only part of the story. What it really took was a change in mind-set. Rental companies had to recognize that they'd save money and build loyalty if thcy moved their services closer to their customers.

## MAKING HEROES

Pay attention to how your customers might like to interact with your products or services, and a remarkable change takes place. You can do more than simply satisfy their immediate needs. You might actually make your customers feel like heroes.

Breakthrough products generally don't come to you in a flash of lig. Usually, it's a gray day with no forecast of sun. Berkley Outdoor Products, for example, came to us with what seemed a waning ritual. Kids weren't fishing as much as they used to, and sales were down.

We went to stores and were struck with the frustrating complexity of fishing. Everything was sold separately: rods, reels, tackle, and line. Next, we went out to the fishing piers and suburban lakes to see the state of kid fishing, and found plenty of struggling dads.

In the old days, Dad could ask the old-timer behind the counter in the fishing storc for help or information, but that won't work today at Wal-Mart. Dads often don't remember much about fishing and don't want to look stupid. So we decided to change gears. Make it all-in-one, playful, and toylike. Rod and reel incorporated in one piece, with all the essential gear in its own compartment—lures here and bobbers there. A complete set of instructions, including how to tie a hook on the line, laminated on the back of the plastic case. My favorite touch was the smallest, but it fit with the all-in-one concept: Even bait was included, in the form of a magical worm-shaped mixture Berkley calls Power Bait.

As a father, I especially like the way Berkley's new approach redesigned the fishing experience from Dad's point of view. Dad went from looking a little foolish in front of the kids to becoming thc knowledgeable hero. And this wonderful ritual pastime became easier to learn and pass along to the next generation.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/34ea71f89da4c808f2d78e2ba47505152f523af555ef8cffeeef49e9dabc8eb9.jpg)  
By watching how dads struggled with their kids' fishing gear, we came up with an all-in-one fishing kit.

If something's broken, it can be fixed. Just keep your eyes open for inspiration.

Everybody wants to be a hero.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/87238dedfe0d483b0aaad3eb448f178c64239653e278dde3d5f8b8302fdb0e4f.jpg)

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/9b36ca42b119b97512b994120cac565023b2bba2785ea3bc2a01dbe771ff7658.jpg)

The best way to get a good idea is to get a lot of ideas. —LINUS PAULING

he problem with brainstorming is that everyone thinks they already do it. When I raise the topic of brainstorming in presentations and conversations with business executives, I see some eyes start to glaze over with the smug "been there, done that" look of someone who has already answered "yes" to the question "Do you brainstorm?" In fact, more than 70 percent of the businesspeople in a recent Arthur Andersen survey say they use brainstorming in their organizations.

Many businesspeople treat brainstorming as a check box, a threshold variable, like "Can you ride a bicycle?" or "Do you know how to tie your shoes?" They overlook the possibility that brainstorming can be a skill, an art, more like playing the piano than tying your shoes. You're alvays learning and can get continuously better. You can become a brainstorming virtuoso. You can have heads of giant corporations and heads of government fly in to ask for your brainstorming help. (Don't laugh. I've seen it with my own eyes.)

So if you say you already do brainstorming in your organization, great, you're on your way. But I believe you can deliver more value, create more energy, and foster more innovation through better brainstorming. For one thing, you could brainstorm more often, weaving it into the cultural fabric of your organization. In that same Arthur Andersen survey of people who said they brainstorm, 76 percent admitted they brainstorm less than once a month. Less than once a month. I consider myself a movie fan, usually seeing thirty to forty films a year on the big screen (plus again as many on video), but if I dropped below one a month, I'd have to say I was a former fan. If you want to keep in shape, you have to exercise your brainstorming muscles more than once a month. So find a suitable space, order some supplies (and some chocolate chip cookies), get a good group together, and brainstorm up several dozen possible solutions to a problem that's bugging you right now.

Brainstorming is practically a religion at IDEO, one we practice nearly every day. Though brainstorms themselves are often playful, brainstorming as a tool—as a skill—is taken quite seriously. And in a company without many rules, we have a very firm idea about what constitutes a brainstorm and how it should be organized. First, a brainstorm is not a regular meeting. It's not something you take notes at. You don't take turns speaking in any orderly way. It shouldn't consume a morning or an afternoon. Sixty minutes seems to be the optimum length, in our experience, though occasionally a brainstorm can productively stretch to an hour and a half. The level of physical and mental energy required for a brainstorm is hard to sustain much longer than that. Brainstorming sessions aren't presentations or opportunities for the boss to poll the troops for hot ideas. Nor should they feel like work. And brainstorming is most definitely not about spending thousands of dollars at some glamorous off-site location.

Brainstorming is the idea engine of IDEO's culture. It's an opportunity for teams to "blue sky" ideas early in a project or to solve a tricky problem that's cropped up later on. The more productive the group, the more it brainstorms regularly and effectively. We call the sessions "brainstormers," which to us sounds more like an active, engaging event. The buzz of a good brainstormer can infect a team with optimism and a sense of opportunity that can carry it through the darkest and most pressure-tinged stages of a project.

So what makes a brainstormer sing? Most people are familiar with the fundamentals—like sticking to one conversation at a time and building on the ideas of others—but it takes extra effort if you want a great brainstormer with valuable results.

## SEVEN SECRETS FOR BETTER BRAINSTORMING

## 1. SHARPEN THE FOCUS

Good brainstormers start with a well-honed statement of the problem. This can be s iple s a qestio. Ey i betehan fuzz. The session will get off to a bettcr start—and you can bring people back into the main topic more easily—if you have a well-articulated description of the problem at just the right level of specificity. A brainstormer without a clear problem statemcnt is likc a company without a clear strategy: You'll wander aimlessly and need a lot of extra luck or talent to succeed. If you find yourself leading a session that lacks direction, spend a few minutes developing a good problem statement. It will be time well spent. For example, "spill-proof coffee cup lids" would be a bad brainstorming topic because it's too narrow and already presumes you know the answer. Another approach, "bicycle cup holders," is too dry and product-focused. Maybe bicyclists shouldn't use cups at all, in which case they certainly don't need cup holders. A better, more open-ended topic would be "helping bike commuters to drink coffee without spilling it or burning their tongues." Go for something tangible that participants can sink their teeth into, without limiting the possible solutions.

We've also found that the best topic statements focus outward on a specific customer need or service enhancement rather than focusing invard on some organizational goal. For example, topics like "How can wve regain market share from Company X?" or "How can we build a better search engine than Company Y?" may not lend themselves to innovations that will be embraced by the market. On the other hand, a series of more specific, customer-focused brainstorms on topics like "How can we accelerate the time-to-first-result for customers searching via dial-up modems" could uncover innovations that might ultimately yield the competitive edge you are looking for.

## 2. PLAYFUL RULES

Don't start to critique or debate ideas. It can sap the energy of the session pretty quickly. You need a way to turn asidc critiques without turning off the critiquers completely. At IDEO many of our conference rooms have brainstorming rules stenciled in six-inch-high letters on the walls, for example, "Go for quantity," "Encouragc wild ideas," or "Be visual." Not willing to mark up your walls? Perhaps the facilitator could ring a bell when participants try to turn a brainstormer into a normal meeting. Or you could print the brainstorming rules on a giant Post-it or write them prominently on a whiteboard.

## 3. NUMBER YOUR IDEAS

Numbering each idea is pretty obvious, right? So obvious that it took us almost ten years to figure it out. Numbering the ideas that bubble up in a brainstorm helps in two ways. First, it's a tool to motivate the participants before and during the session ("Let's try to get a hundred ideas before we leave the room") or to gauge the fluency of a completed brainstorm. Second, it's a great way to jump back and forth from idea to idea without losing track of where you are. We've found that a hundred ideas per hour usually indicates a good, fluid brainstorming session. One hundred and fifty per hour is pushing the speed limit. Of course, it's quality that ultimately matters, but we've heeded Linus Pauling's advice to get a lot of ideas.

## 4. BUILD AND JUMP

Watch for chances to "build" and "jump." High-energy brainstormers tend to follow a series of steep "power" curves, in which momentum builds slowly, then intensely, then starts to plateau. The best facilitators can nurture an emerging conversation with a light touch in the first phase and know enough to let ideas flow during the steep part of the ideation curve. It's when energy fades on a line of discussion that the acitator eally arns his rhe kee.

In the coffee-while-bicycling brainstorm example above, a good "building" suggestion to keep up momentum might be: "Shock absorbers are a great idea; now, what are some other ways to reduce spillage when the bicycle hits a bump?" By contrast, when discussion tapers off, a good "jump"transition statement might be something like this: "OK, let's switch gears and consider some totally hands-free'solutns thatalow tecc tokee bothan  thenebr t all times. What might those solutions look like?"

Try building on an idea. Encourage another push or introduce a small variation. Or take a jump, either back to an earlier path you skipped by too quickly or frvard to a completely new approach. Whatever you do, try to get into the next power curve and keep the energy up.

## 5. THE SPACE REMEMBERS

Great brainstorm leaders understand the power of spatial memory. Write the fow of ideas down in a medium visible to the whole group. There are many emerging digital technologies for group work, but we have had great success with extremely low-tech tools like Sharpie markers, giant Post-its for the walls, and rolls of old-fashioned butchershop paper on the tables. Brainstorming is an intensely group-oriented process, and the facilitator's rapid scribing is one of the focal points that hold the group together. We're not talking about taking personal meeting notes here, but capturing ideas so that the group can see their progression and return to those that seem worthy of more attention.

Cover virtually every wall and flat surface with paper before the session starts. That way you won't find yourself in the awkward position of having to erase ideas to make room for more. And you may find there's a certain synergy in physically moving around the room writing down and sketching the ideas. As you rapidly capture the team's ideas, make a mental note of ones that are worth coming back to during a build or a jump. When you return to the spot on the wall where that idea was captured, spatial memory will help people recapture the mind-set they had when the idea first emerged.

## 6. STRETCH YOUR MENTAL MUSCLES

People are busy. Time is short. Is it worthwhile to "burn" some time at the beginning of a brainstorm doing some form of group warm-up?

Maybe. But that "maybe" rapidly becomes a "yes" in certain circumstances:

When the group has not worked together before

When most of the group doesn't brainstorm frequently

When the group seems distracted by pressing but unrelated issues

One type of warm-up we practice is a fast-paced word game simply to clear the mind (Zen practitioners call it "beginner's mind") and to get the team into a more outgoing mode. While these group exercises done in a circle may seem like you've been transported to your toddler's Gymboree class, they help ground you in the group and temporarily blot out worries about the latest operational glitch or the unread e-mails waiting back at your computer.

Another warm-up is to do some content-related homework. At IDEO we did a systematic study a few years ago to measure the value of different brainstorming warm-up techniques. We divided our pool of participants into three groups and had them brainstorm on applications of specific new technologies to the toy industry.

First we gave two of the groups homework. One group did some background reading and listened to an expert lecture on the technologies. Another group spent half an hour in a local toy store looking at the state of the art in all its variations. The third group came into the brainstorming session cold. Guess what? The group whose homework was a visit to a toy store significantly outperformed the other two, both in the quantity of ideas produced and in perceived quality. Not scientific enough to submit to the New England Journal of Medicine, but we were convinced. We now frequently bring "show-and-tell" to a brainstormer to help us visualize the wide variety of options and materials that could be applied to the session's topic.

One of the best brainstormers I ever attended at IDEO was an exploration of alternative wine beverage containers. Before the brainstormer, we covered a conference table with bottles, closures, materials, and mechanisms ranging from the retro porcelain Grolsch beer bottle stopper to an elegant black Japanese sake flask. By the time we'd examined everything on the table, the session was already in full swing. We had hundreds of ideas before we were done, and a point of view on how to help our client innovate.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/56fa44efbf76650bb4a278e2801eeddad175657803c5c35eae5ab57453ec8df1.jpg)  
Printing brainstorming rules on the wall can help keep sessions on track.

## 7. GET PHYSICAL

Good brainstorms are extremely visual. They include sketching, mind mapping, diagrams, and stick figures. You don't have to be an artist to get your point across with a sketch or diagram. Leave your performance anxieties at the door and jump in with whatevcr visual tools you have available.

But the best brainstormers often get physical. We move beyond two dimensions and push for three. The first way we do this is to bring in everything but the kitchen sink (and we've brought the sink, too, when it was relevant). That means competitive products, elegant solutions from other fields, and promising technologies that could be applied to the problem. The second way we get physical is to have materials on hand to build crude models of a concept: blocks, foam corc, tubing, duct tape, whatever might be useful. The third physical approach is "bodystorming," where we act out current behavior/usage patterns and see how they might be altered. We've bodystormed on products ranging from vending machines to car seats, where our skits and scenarios pointed to all kinds of opportunities for improvement.

## THE BRAINSTORMER EFFECT

Hot brainstormers may generate a hundred or more ideas, ten of which may be solid leads. They can help put a team on course, and the rush oalne an kee tem ebr uzzig r ays. The ple effect. People talk after brainstormers, sharing wild or practical ideas that may have come out of a particularly vibrant session. A great brainstormer gives you a fantastic feeling of possibility, and an hour later you walk out of the room a little richer for the experience. I think that sense of spontaneous team combustion is why we've been able to find so many unusual solutions toseeingly intractable problems. For example, in designing the slim Dynabook laptop we discovered there wasn't enough room for traditional fasteners. A brainstormer generated the idea of "knitting" the two halves together—like a piano hingewhic sent us ut loking for  srg, thin o treaded nne end.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/4a8a28ebcab01f2fd473b884a5e41e3c2f5474b2b1bc8ac889fb4d80999ec7f7.jpg)  
This surprising but effective use of a bicycle spoke was inspired by a brainstorm.

We eventually reached a solution by getting our favorite Palo Alto bike shop to supply custom spokes to Dynabook that, amazingly enough, did the trick.

You can't quantify the value of letting people's minds run wild. At one brainstormer a few designers and engineers got the idea to motorize a swirling pencil--and made one on the spot. "We've struck gold!" somebody yelled out. We didn't follow up successfully on the whimsical toy. But a few years later, with or without our help, the Wigglewriter came to market and sold like hotcakes. A waste of time? Not in my book. It's the sort of incorrigible spirit of play that keeps team members in tune and on edge.

I'm convinced that if you participate regularly in brainstormers, it's like stretching exercises for your mind. You approach problems differently than when you're at your desk or strolling the halls. When a client rejects a course of action, the two or three team members working on that specific aspect of the project don't slink off to their desks to mope. They'll put up chairs to the nearest table and hold a mini-brainstormer, maybe just fifteen or thirty minutes to find a new track.

Brainstormers can do something else for your group. They offer the team members a chance to shine. It's friendly competition. Who doesn't want to demonstrate their cleverness and wittiness in front of their peers? And when a brainstormer thunders, it's a multiple win, a rising tide that lifts everyone up on a wave of excitement.

Some people, of course, are better at brainstorming than others. And truth be told, your performance does matter. Pipe up in brainstormers, bang out a quick prototype, or solve a tricky technical question, and you'll rise in the eyes of your peers. If you've got an open style of innovation—with brainstormers, prototyping, and problem solving—you'll naturally favor those who take action. It's true, "hot groups" aren't a place for shrinking violets. When a newcomer starts peppering a brainstormer with cool ideas, we take notice. You earn respect and authority in that week's brainstormer or on today's prototype team.

Unfortunately, we all occasionally fall victim to negativity. The coach who hammers too hard on the fumbling player, the teacher who corrects the flub and fails to praise the insight. Without a comfort zone, people don't take chances. If you're going to do a lot of brainstorming, there are several other pitfalls to watch out for.

## SIX WAYS TO KILL A BRAINSTORMER

A brainstormer is a terrible thing to waste. It's easy to get it wrong.   
Here are ix surefire ways to kill a brainstormer.

## 1. THE BOSS GETS TO SPEAK FIRST

If the boss gets first crack, then he's going to sct the agenda and the boundaries, and your brainstormer is immediatcly limited. I know of one Silicon Valley boss who eagerly launched a brainstorm saying he was looking for some great new ideas. But unfortunately, he followed that enthusiasm with a room-silencing caveat: "Oh, and every new idea has to be patentable. And something we can manufacture." In that setting, nobody is going to suggest anything even remotely "wild." Try sending the boss out for coffee. Or doughnuts.

## 2. EVERYBODY GETS A TURN

I once sat in on a meeting a client thought was brainstorming. Sixteen people were packed into the room. We went clockwise around the table, and each person was given two minutes to speak. It was democratic. It was painful. It was pointless. It definitely wasn't a brainstormer.

## 3. EXPERTS ONLY PLEASE

Let's see, do I have a materials expert, an engineer, a software guru, and the V.P. of marketing? In brainstorming, don't be an "expert" snob. Bring in someone from manufacturing, who knows how to build things. Invite a customer service rep with lots of field experience. Find someone who reads a lot of science fiction. They may not have the "right" degrees, but they just might have the insight you need.

## 4. DO IT OFF-SITE

Brainstorming at ski lodges and beach resorts can be counterproductive. Do you want your team members to think that crcativity and inspiration only happen at high altitude or within walking distance of an ocean? Don't get me wrong: Off-sites arc fine. But remember, you want the buzz of creativity to blow through your offices as regularly as a breeze at the beach.

## 5. NO SILLY STUFF

Sports utility shopping carts. Velcro diapers. A privacy curtain to hide those embarrassing purchases. These are just a few of the wacky ideas hatched during our Nightline shopping cart brainstormer. I can't emphasize enough what these flights of fancy do for the team. They remind everybody that this isn't like work, that anything goes, and that we're going to have a lot of fun while we solve our problems.

## 6. WRITE DOWN EVERYTHING

I recently encountered a senior manager who's very good at a lot of things, but very bad at brainstorming. What's his main fault? He writes everything down, and I mean everything. He seldom contributes anything useful. We've seen this scenario before, especially among those who are new to brainstormers. Taking notes shifts your focus to the wrong side of your brain. It's like trying to dance and type on your laptop at the same time. Sketch all you want, doodle to your heart's delight. But don't act as if you're in History 101.

Everybody brainstorms. And everyone could get better at it, including IDEO—which was why we recently launched a research project to look for new approaches and variations. We believe it's worth the energy to try harder. A brainstorm can feel like it just another meetng, or it can be a fun, invigorating experience that can take a project or a team to a new level.

## A COOL COMPANY NEEDS HOT GROUPS

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/b4d9ced23496819efeaafe4dfcf04403f8befeff90a2a524a5003f770cfb7e52.jpg)  
rossem aon

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/2778f7d7275d355ae3746656c6570af7bebcf1a83e2860e83e1b357542039565.jpg)

eams are the heart of the IDEO method. It's no accident. We believe it's how innovation and much of business take place in the world. Quite simply, great projects are achieved by great teams.

Of course, we aren't talking about just any old teams. Though companies are naturally about groups and teamwork, too often these groups are simply the result of inertia. Years ago an "operations review committee" may have been formed, with broad and vaguely defined objectives. The group stil lumbers on, meeting once a month, with no one feeling brave enough to suggest disbanding it. That sort of staid, unfocused group bears little or no resemblance to the hot groups we're talking about.

Hot project teams start with a clear goal and a serious deadline. The hot group knows that it might disband after the goal is reached and reform the next week to slay another dragon. How do you distinguish between dull and hot groups? It's the difference between administering a trust fund and making an MTV video. A hot group is infused with purpose and personality.

What's a bad group? A place where defending and preserving the group's status is a key goal. A place where everyone has a passion to retire. At IDEO we put that passion into the present. Our passion is about doing great projects. Today.

## THE MYTH OF THE LONE GENIUS

If you distrust the power of teamwork, consider this fact. Even the most legendary individual inventor is often a team in disguise. In six scant years, for example, Thomas Edison generated an astounding four hundred patents, producing innovations in the telegraph, telephone, phonograph, and lightbulb—-with the help of a fourteen-man team. As Francis Jehl, Edison's longtime assistant, explained, "Edison is in reality a collective noun and means the work of many men." Even Michelangelo couldn't have painted the Sistine Chapel without the help of a gang of artisans. Prolific modern artist Keith Haring not only enlisted help on many of his own large projects but also started creating works of art in collaboration with literally hundreds of schoolchildren.

At IDEO we believe that the myth of the lone genius can actually hamper a company's efforts in innovation and creativity. After close encounters with dozens of real-life inventors, I have to report that most of them don't have a lot to teach us about applying the creative process to business. Too many of the inventors I have met suffer from a selflimiting form of paranoia. They want help with their inventions but aren't quite ready to reveal them. They aren't quite sure they can trust us with their precious secret and are worried that any potential partners will take advantage of them. So they return to the safety of their garages and basements and nothing ever happens.

The same is often true of the lone genius within a company. We've found that loners are so caught up in their idea that they are reluctant to let it go, much less allow it to be experimented with and improved upon. They think more like individuals than team members. And unfortunately, their projects suffer.

## THE MAKINGS OF A HOT GROUP

History demonstrates that great projects and products are often the result of great teams. Products and projects as diverse as the Macintosh, the new Volkswagen Beetle, and the Lockheed Skunkworks were all spawned by charismatic teams. How do they do it? In a microcosm, the IDEO Nightline shopping cart team—though they were only together for a week—displayed many of the characteristics of hot groups. First, they were totally dedicated to achieving the end result. No one doubted that shopping carts could use some improvement, and everyone was enthusiastic. Second, they faced down a slightly ridiculous deadline. When the hurdle is high, there's a tremendous sense of achievement in getting anything done by the deadline. Third, the group was irreverent and nonhierarchical. Despite the deadline, they joked and played around—like brainstorming up a sports utility shopping cart—to let off steam. Fourth, the team was well rounded and respectful of its diversity. Though the team was drawn from widely divergent disciplines, they had tremendous respect for their fellow members. You knew you were selected for your ability, not seniority or political skills. Fifth, they worked in an open, eclectic space optimal for flexibility, group work, and brainstorming. There were high ceilings with no internal walls, no sense that you often have in a typical corporate setting that "the company" wants it a certain way.

Finally, the group felt empowered to go get whatever else it needed. Hot teams connect to the outside world. They know that answers don't lie within. To design a better shopping cart, they sought out grocery stores and shoppers and industry experts. No one on the team thought for a second that a dozen people within IDEO had all the answers.

We've found that this model can help inspire all kinds of businesses, even those that at first glance don't seem conducive to teams.

## HOT STUDIOS

At IDEO we've created a Hollywood-like system for quickly building teams around projects and disciplines. Successful film development companies have honed the art of quickly assembling teams—taking a prototype (a script) and building around it the actors, directors, producers, and countless other individuals and contractors it takes to make a movie. In Hollywood there are no standing committees and no reliance on inertia. Every film production is an intense project where you field the best team you can, and race for the deadline. It's a model that works.

We've developed our own version of the studio system—and I think it's one of our biggest competitive edges. Design firms seldom exceed a hundred people. The reason is simple. When talent can't find enough places to blossom, it generally splits off in new directions.

David learned the importance of forming teams from his days at Stanford. It mattered to him who he worked with. But as the company matured, he realized he couldn't have a personal relationship with each person at IDEO; he couldn't be mindful of everyone's individual needs.

So as we approached the one-hundred-person mark at our Palo Alto offices in the mid-1990s, we took a chance and threw things open, designating some of our best people as studio leaders and giving them an opportunity to make their pitches to prospective members. No one at IDEO was going to be assigned to a studio.

Instead, at our Monday morning "all hands" meeting in Palo Alto, each leader described the type of work they favored, and what was exciting and challenging about their approach to product innovation. We reversed that horrible childhood sandlot routine where players wait to be selected. IDEOers got to pick their team leader, not vice versa. And you got to work where you wanted to: The studio heads each chose a location for their teams, among the various buildings in our Palo Alto campus.

Everyone gave David a "secret ballot" with their first and second studio choices, and amazingly, we were able to juggle the studio sizes so that everyone got their first choice. Each studio group ranged from ten to twenty people. Project teams, which operate within or across studios, depending on the nature of the work, could be as small as three people or as large as a dozen. After a couple of years, we opened it all up again and gave everyone the chance to reshuffle studio membership if they wanted. It's worked. We've not only grown but also developed hot teams with very unique capabilities.

## GENERATING YOUR OWN ENERGY

Creating a hot team is partly about mind-set. Hot teams don't necessarily have to start with the most exciting project. The right team and leader can create their own energy around the task at hand. My very first LP record (remember vinyl?) was the sound track of Mary Poppins and I can still hear her saying that in every job that must be done, there is anlemet  "You find the un nd—sap!—he jo' me. I am not seriously suggesting you quote Mary Poppins to the team, but the idea of making your own fun still applies.

People who work with kids already understand this principle, of course. Think of kindergarten (my son just graduated). The team—or class—spends nine long months slogging through some pretty dense material (like learning a new alphabet). A hundred and eighty days of school sounds like endless tedium. But the teacher (team leader) divides the time into "stories." Every month is something special. There's fall colors month, pioneer spirit month, dinosaur month, and so on. Instead of thirty-six undifferentiated weeks, every week or month has a meaning. Kindergarten is a series of projects, interspersed with games, challenges, and plenty of fun.

At IDEO we've developed lots of ways to build teamwork into even the most seemingly ordinary tasks, many of which involve competition and playful rewards. Dividing tasks into the acts of a play can give them more drama. Most accounting departments, for instance, appear at first glance to be unlikely places for hot teams to sprout. What's there to get excited about in all those rows of numbers? But think of the intensity of the office when they're closing books for the fiscal year or quarter. What if the accounting team could tap into those rhythms and that energy more regularly? The accounting department is critical—and exciting—at those pivotal points. Build that enthusiasm into every workweek, and you've got the makings of a very hot team.

Finally, if you're hard-pressed to find inspiration, Tom Peters has some pretty good advice. He'd argue we can all benefit by thinking of our group or department as a professional service firm, or PSF, in its own right, with clients and projects and a seriously qualified professional staff. As Tom says, "Life in a PSF is as far a cry from Dilbert-Drones in DrearyVille (a.k.a. Departments) as one can imagine." No matter what you do, you can restructure the team's work practices to deliver better quality, faster results, or more fun. Peters cheers us all on to turn our work into "wow projects." Something you're proud of. Something that makes a difference. Something you can tell your kids about.

Easier said than done, you say. But focus on what's appealing in your work or project, and you'll be surprised at how teams will jell out of thin air. The attraction might be a challenging technical task. Or something with a positive social impact, say coming up with a medical product that can reduce painor savelivs. The possibility  publicvisibiliy can also inspire a team. But your project or team's contribution doesn't have to be trumpeted in the media. If it's bound to impress competitors, if it's something you feel proud to mention at a party, then it's hot.

## THE PASSION FACTOR

What motivates a creative team? Passion plays a huge role. We can all learn from recent teams inspired by a cause or a search for excellence. Developers were dying to work on Linux because it was a chance to be part of—and get some credit for—crcating the world's best free operating system. Artists and technical staff at Industrial Light and Magic pride themselves on fashioning the world's best special effects in the latest hot movie. Paul Newman and Ben & Jerry's built an amazing amount of good will and customer loyalty out of connecting with worthy social causes.

Teams don't nced to have higher causes to perform, but they do need tangible goals—a technical first, a challenging sales target, outshining a tough competitor. In short, something to shoot for and be proud of.

We believe the strongcst teams take root when individuals are given the chance of picking what groups they work with and even occasionally what projects they work on. That way, passion fuels the fire. For example, we were recently asked to develop a kid's car seat. We asked for volunteers, and dozens of employees stepped forward. One of the dads who volunteered was so concerned about safety that he'd already bought ten different car seats for his three kids. He was picked, of course. To me, that's the kind of enthusiasm you need to boost a project to the next level.

Sports and recreation companies understand this kind of passion for the work. They live the lifestyle they're selling. That's why so many premier mountain climbers, kayakers, skiers, and windsurfers work for the Seattle-based sports retailer REI. For them, there's little dividing work and play. But your inspiration doesn't have to be physical, or even a conscious passion. It can start with a single emotional or intellectual chord. IDEO's car seat dad was helping to ensure the safety of his own children, as well as that of thousands of others. Not a bad reason to get out of bed in the morning.

How do inspired individuals become hot teams? Crazy deadlines and seemingly unreachable goals are often the sparks. In 1961 John F. Kennedy inspired a nation—and a lot of hot teams at NASA—by challenging them to put a man on the moon by the end of the decade. At the time, it seemed an impossible task. Yet America rose to the occasion. In business, Steve Jobs is the master of setting the bar impossibly high, continuously challenging his teams to do something "insanely great." First he pampered and drove the original Macintosh team. Fifteen years later he resurrected Apple with the bold iMac when the company was almost written off as irrelevant (more than one critic prematurely penned Apple's epitaph) and in truth probably not far from extinction.

But opportunity can also inspire more humble business goals. Remember our Nightline challenge? We knew that if we could build a better shopping cart by Friday, we'd send a great message to millions of people. I think every hot team faces similarly challenging deadlines and goals. It's no secret. Pressure can help you get it out the door.

## OPPORTUNITIES IN OBSTACLES

Adversity can prove the glue that binds your team together, making it emerge stronger and more tightly knit. That's what certainly happened with the hard case we designed and had manufactured for the Palm V. After making nearly ten thousand of the cases, our manufacturing liaison team discovered a slight quality flaw. Though the mistake was fixed on the assembly line, there seemed to be just two ways to deal with the existing inventory: throw out ten thousand cases, or take a chance that only a few dozen were defective and that customer returns would be limited. Fortunately, our team came up with a third alternative: After normal working hours, a dozen or so white-gloved IDEOers carefully pried open those ten thousand boxes and inspected each case, placing the into "reject" or "ccpt" pil. I did a shit, and it was a gret xperience. Rock music blared and we had a raucous time, cracking jokes until midnight. Team members would rotate through the different inspection jobs and then take a break for Pepsi and pizza (without the white gloves on).

It turned out that far fewer cases were defective than we feared, but it was the right thing to do for lots of reasons, not the least of which being that we would have been putting our reputation and relationship with Palm in jeopardy if we'd shipped flawed products. I think that little obstacle brought the team closer than ever before and reinforced its pride in the quality of products shipped.

Hot teams frequently take chances, though not always intentionally. A team in our Grand Rapids office was working on ways to improve airflow through an office space and give workers more control over their immediate surroundings. Suddenly, they hit on a new metaphor. Could the air vents in a car's dashboard provide some inspiration? Three team members drove to the nearest junkyard, paid the one-dollar cover charge, and set upon the first vehicle they came to. They pulled out their heavy netal—assorted saws, screwdrivers, and crowbars—and started hacking away. Suddenly, they heard the junkyard guy yelling that they were on the wrong side of the fence, just as they noticed the keys in the ignition. The team nearly panicked, as they all thought: "Jeez, I hope this isn't his car!" Fortunately, the car was just a junker for sale, and the team quickly set upon another carcass on the right side of the fence. They sliced out virtually an entire dashboard (at a total cost of \$7.50) and got a leg up on their airflow question. The junkyard trip not only provided a breakthrough for the project but also became a memorable shared experience for the team.

## ON A MISSION

Hot teams love a challenge. IDEO has designed and engineered countless products. But we've seldom manufactured and sold our own consumer electronics, a role we usually leave to our clients. Dennis Boyle's hot group decided it was time to challenge that status quo. In the late summer of 1999 as the team's intensive work on the Handspring Visor neared completion, they began itching for a new challenge.

The Visor was an "open architecture" personal digital assistant, a Palm-like device designed to accept a wide variety of modules via its Springboard connector. By definition, its success, like the personal computer's success a generation before, would depend on the hardware and software of third-party, independent developers.

Dennis's team aspired to be one of those independent developersto come up with the killer add-on. A product that would both be a hit in its own right and contribute to the overall success of the Visor. The IDEO team brainstormed up a few dozen alternatives and settled on the idea of making a small add-on digital camera for the Visor dubbed the "Eyemodule." This was a bold move, as the team would actually have to create and manufacture a highly technical product from scratch, something they'd never done. And to top it off, they'd never made a camera before.

By late September they'd assembled an exceptionally diverse inhouse team, IDEO experts in everything from interaction design to high-volume manufacturing, along with some capable outside contributors. Boyle set what seemed an impossible goal of snapping digital images of Handspring's founders, Jeff Hawkins and Donna Dubinsky, by December 2.

The goal lit a fire under the team, and Boyle personally made sure they kept moving forward. Every few hours he circulated among the different disciplines to make certain everyone was communicating. When a designer complained that an electronic component didn't fit, Dennis skipped over to an electrical engineer and asked him to resize it. There was a constant battle to make sure the disciplines kept up with each other. More than once, Dennis made the interaction designer complete a design so the software programmer could continue writing the code and have a prototype ready for the circuit board due the following week.

Evey week, Dehel  t etig, qicklhutig the different disciplines, each subgroup covering only what was relevant to its task. At key points, outside consultants were conferenced in so they could query the team members.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/86c6d22e01903f09ffabb229c16b7499b5f55ac65493100b1d00f41d340337c0.jpg)  
A large group helped create this small digital camera for the Handspring Visor.

Sixty days after assembling the team, they got the prototype working—a week before the deadline. Dubinsky was impressed but wondered how wc could follow through on the myriad logistics and support issues. The team stcpped up its deadlines—and made almost all of them. A dozen alpha units by the middle of January; three dozen by February, in time for Hawkins to demonstrate the Eyemodule at Demo 2000, the same place hc'd held up the Palm Pilot four years earlier.

But the manufacturer didn't have the same sense of urgency, since we were still a small customer in their eyes. We expcrienced more than our share of the usual manufacturing start-up issues, and the project team painstakingly fixed many of the early prototype boards by hand.

Throughout the process, the dedication and intensity of the hot team kept the project on track. When the purchasing agent went on vacation unexpectedly, IDEO team members stepped in, found the components, and ran them down to thc factory themselves. When a bad batch of plastic parts didn't fit properly, half a dozen team members spent a couple of days at the factory, outfitting the Eyemodules with new cases.

As production ramped up, another problem arose. When the manufacturer couldn't automatically set the focus of each lens, team members rushed back onto the factory floor, focusing thousands of lenses by hand. IDEO learned a new technique it was able to pass on to the manufacturer, who ultimately was able to automate the process.

Despite all the obstacles, by mid-April the Eyemodule was shipping. Incredibly, we already had a customer service call center up and running in Salt Lake City and developers making software for the unit. Manufacturing has since transferred to a plant in Malaysia, where it is now sailing along with hardly a problem. Today the Eyemodule is the best-selling add-on for the Handspring Visor, which is challenging the Palm line in the hugely popular handheld market.

I think that's pretty amazing: six and a half months from founding a team to getting a highly technical hardware product on the market. Not bad r  oup that was a epec h went along.

## TEAM PROBLEM SOLVING

Teams find ways to leap barriers. Take the experience one of our team members had at a Silicon Valley company. Upper management wa'givinhis team thefnanal suppor it eedeSevral subontractors refused to do any more work on the project until they were paid. So the team leader drove to the bank, withdrew \$18,000 from his personal account, and paid them. Then he filed an expense report for \$18,001.54 (he billed for the mileage to the bank). And the project got done. That kind of grit and determination, buoyed by a willingness to bend the rules when you have to, is one of the reasons hot teams are so effective.

Indeed, it's often the last-minute snafu that brings a team together. One IDEO team working on a project to reduce noise in office environments arrived at the conference room where they were scheduled to give a demonstration the following morning, only to discover that the room was acoustically isolated—virtually soundproof. There was no background noise to cancel out. It was the opposite of the hectic, noisy office environments that the team was developing solutions for, and would be disastrous for a demonstration. This unexpected hurdle set the team members to drowning their sorrows over beer at a local bar, where they realized they could barely hear one another. Suddenly, a lightbulb went on! One of the members ran out to buy a tape recorder. They taped the bar noise, and the next morning used that background noise in their demonstration, which was a hit.

## TEAMS IN CLOSE QUARTERS

We've all had that heady feeling at one time or another. I'll bet you have been part of a hot group. One of my personal hot-group experiences took place while working as a management consultant in southeast Asia. The focus of the project was a bit fuzzy. Singapore Airlines hired us to do a review of their already top-notch engineering and maintenance operations. Our three-man project team divided this complex problem into manageable parts. Each senior consultant tackled an area—operations strategy, inventory management, and maintenance scheduling.

The team did everything together for more than six months. We worked all day together, lived in the same hotel, ate practically every meal together, worked out at the same health club-even drove together to Malaysia twice a month to get our passports stamped. Squeezed into one small office, we had the chance to bounce ideas off of each other several times a day. And we often worked late into the night helping each other analyze and interpret the mountains of data.

Ultimately, we found the proverbial needle in the haystack, a small but costly flaw in this otherwise sterling airline. A miscommunication between the maintenance and marketing groups had resulted in a miscalculation about how much downtime a new fleet of aircraft needed. We showed how they could dramatically (and safely) reduce that downtime, increasing the number of hours per week the fleet was available for revenue flying. All those late nights the team spent poring over the data together had finally paid off, saving the airline tens of millions of dollars. Without the intense teamwork, I doubt if we could have made such a breakthrough.

Close quarters can charge the dynamics of a hot group. One of our hottest studio teams meets for its biweekly lunch in a ridiculously cramped place, and I doubt the intensity would be quite the same if the room weren't so crowded. Too much square footage, like too large a budget, can dissipate energy and discourage a more immediate and emotional connection between team members. One IDEOer had been a principal in a start-up and toiled with his two partners in a tiny, windowless space for a year and a half. Though on paper it sounds pretty bleak in ac thee was a eat egyo thetea. Laterhey o ing and their office space leaped from a thousand square feet to ten thousand. Suddenly, they had private, noncontiguous offices. Communication deteriorated and relationships soured almost overnight. The company eventually grew to fifteen people, but they'd lost momentum and ultimately never achieved success.

O cr c peoent h pla ' the y  o build hot teams. An impromptu sctting can help spark team chemistry. When the Danish hearing-aid company Oticon discovered that spontaneous interactions between cmployees locatcd on different floors were taking place on stairvells, the firm wisely broadened the stairs to encouragc thc multidisciplinary interchange.

The hottest teams flower like a budding relationship—somebody literally moves their clothes into your closet. While on a furniture project, for example, a handful of IDEOers worked on-site at the client's facilities south of San rancio. They carveout thewn space in terner of the factory and worked and lived with their prototypes and counterparts. Their joint effort had the fervor of a mission. I'm not surprised that four months later they came up with a series of nifty new products for the client, including a rolling T-shaped office divider, capped with a whiteboard that's handy for smallimpromptu meetings r writing quick notes.

## THE RIGHT MIX

There's an art to putting teams together. Personality plays a part. Sometimes it makes sense to stick an extrovert with an otherwise contemplative team, and vice versa. Sameness is not the goal. There are many facets to team dynamics. Where a team member sits, for example, can make a difference. Putting a particular engineer in the right intersection can mean the difference between success and failure. It's a puzzle we juggle all the time, as teams for projects form and disband. IDEO studio head Dave Blakely constantly finds himself creating little neighborhood teams of engineers culled from across the spectrum—from mechanical to electrical, software to manufacturing. The idea of establishing "neighborhoods" is to get the team as close to the hardware they're working on as possible—and make it easy to hold quick, spontaneous local meetings.

If you're working on a series of electromechanical products, you don't want to stick all the electrical engineers in one building and the mechanical engineers in another. Colocating the people who will need to regularly interact with one another recognizes the primacy of project teams and announces that the team is the most important unit in the company.

## ABOLISH "THEY"

One of the things that struck me the most about my first days at IDEO—other than the fact that I had no desk or office—was that try as I might, I couldn't find a "they." At my old job as a consultant for a large international firm, there was always a spoken and unspoken "they." If the coffeemaker was broken, "they" should fix it. If you were entangled in bureaucracy, "they" were the problem. "They" do not innovate. "They" leave less elbowroom for you and other individuals to solve problems from the ground up.

Technology has in some ways exacerbated this problem. I'm confounded at how many companies bring "they" in reach of your every keystroke. I think those large corporations who snoop on their workers' e-mail are nuts—and not just for ethical reasons. If you practice monitoring or surveillance, your employees may end up being less productive and certainly less creative. But "they" companies can't help themselves. They're control freaks—even when they imagine they're loosening their grip.

A friend at a major transportation company sent me her employer's memo about its newly enlightened approach to office attire. The company had finally decided it was time to loosen up. "They" suggested there had been a lot of confusion and anxiety about what to wear on "casal Fridays." So "they"formed a task force. "They" issue a memo saying, among other things, that if you're unsure about whether something is appropriate or not, it's probably not. The memo went on to list items of clothing that were definitely not appropriate, including jeans, shorts, and open-toed shoes—basically what you could expect to see at IDEO every day.

## TEAMS AND TRUST

## So what makes a successful team?

A few years ago I wvas on the same speaking agenda with Lou Holtz, the veteran football coach at the University of Notre Dame. Although I'm generally leery of sports/business metaphors, the coach said something that day that stuck vith me. He said that the team changes every year, but each team member's three implicit questions for him remain the same: Do you care about me? Can I trust you? Are you committed to the success of our team?

The best leaders answer those questions with actions instead of words, and the best teammates leave no doubt about where they stand.

When my brother David joined Boeing in the early 1970s, the company assigned him to a mechanical engineering group. When he pointed out the mistake—his degree was in electrical engineering—no one bothered to reassign him. Instead, they stuck him in a massive vindowless room with tivo hundred other (mechanical) engineers. Adding insult to injury was the strict work schedule policy. The workday began at 8:00 A.M. Arrive early and you couldn't get in. Show up twenty minutes late and they treated you like a grade school truant, taking your name down at the gate, giving you a note for your supervisor to sign. Boeing was, and is, a great company, but in that era, it was ansivering a resounding "no" to Lou Holtz's question about caring about its team members.

There was a time when mutual mistrust was the order of the day. Companies used to have office supply managers. More often than not, the prevailing attitude was that workers cared so little for their employer that given the chance they would rampantly steal office supplies. It is an attitude that makes no sense when team members think of the company as their own. That's why so many companies today are generous with tools. We try to make it easy for our teams to buy or use whatever supplies they might nced. Nor can you go wrong by keeping refrigerators well-stocked with drinks and having a wide variety of food in ready supply, as many start-ups do. It's good hospitality and good karma. If your company is generous, workers are more likely to be generous in kind.

There's another reason to eliminate the "Office of They." Suddenly, every litle snafu becomes an opportunity to exercise your collective problem-solving abilities. A take-charge attitude takes over. More than once, we've done a little plumbing or performed a little "first aid" on our buildings when they needed attention and the property management group wasn't handy. One reason an eclectic and self-determined approach to offices and worker neighborhoods is so effective is that there's no "they." There's just a group of people responsible for our team and our work space.

Strive to create an abundance mentality. Pick projects and clients that excite you—or better yet, you pick each other. You'll find yourself facing fewer turf battles. When our Boston office lands a client close to our Chicago office, it's not a cause for friction. There's a sense that every office will find plenty of work and that things will balance out in the end.

There's something terribly liberating about applying an abundance mentality to your ideas, thinking, and work. There's a Zen-like force here at play: The less you strive to control ideas and insist on credit for those that are yours, the more good ideas you're likely to have—and see implemented. It's why we believe in brainstorming. Good things happen when you willingly take your best ideas and pour them into the mix.

The myth that creativity flourishes primarily in solitude is an established one. We've all read over and over again how novelists and screenwriters writing in the seclusion of remote cottages or bungalows churn out best-sellers and blockbusters. But is that the only way to foster innovation? And is it a viable model for business?

Consider Academy Awvard-winning screenwriter Ron Bass. He has a team of six writers and researchers. They work up ideas for scenes. They research all kinds of details and trivia. They brainstorm. The team dives into two- or three-day outlining marathons, even speaking the dialogue out loud. Finally, the team blocks out the basics of scenes and dialogue, "early prototypes" in the IDEO vernacular. Then Bass sketches a scene in pencil, his assistant types it, faxes it to the team, and they call or fax back their responses. Generally, if half the team hates something, it gets changed.

Other screenwvriters complain that Bass & Co. is a "factory"—and maybe they're right. It's an idea factory. Bass has written or cowritten sixteen feature films, including Rain Man, Waiting to Exhale, My Best Friend's Wedding, Stepmom, and Snow Falling on Cedars. And his films have earned over \$2 billion. Clearly, he's doing something right.

Quite simply, Bass's team approach works. Team members scour novels for possible adaptations. A couple of them handle the stream of "notes" from studios demanding changes in plot or characters. Like Edison's Menlo Park laboratory, Bass and his team manage to constantly juggle four or five projects at once. Bass now turns out more scripts than ever before, and feels his team has improved the quality of his work. Momentum and synergy may be his team's greatest weapons.

Variety can inspire teams. By working on more than one project at a time, team members are often able to see the connections between different projects and emerge energized instead of exhausted. Bass can't fathom writers who complete a script and then shut down their creative juices and go on vacation. Ie and his team are so engaged that it "all continues: comedy, drama, buddy film, caper," he says. "It's all one story, really, the story of who we are and how we relate and how we get it wrong." How does Bass keep his creativity machine running? As soon as he writes a script's final lines——"Fade to black"—he pens the first scene of his next movie. It's a wonderful strategy for any team--to end one project by beginning another. That way, when you do take a break, you already have an opening, a beginning to work from, rather than starting from scratch.

## HOT TEAMS PERFORM

The best teams are like a snowball rolling down a hill, gaining in mass and momentum as they go. Very quickly, just a few snowflakes build into an avalanche of energy and enthusiasm.

How do you start building such a team? First, forget what you learned in school. Believe that your team members will be an outrageous success before their first day of work. That's what Benjamin Zander, conductor of the Boston Philharmonic, does. The celebrated music teacher has a unique strategy for getting the best out of his students. On the first day of class, he tells his first year students that they all get an A. There's one condition. Their first assignment is to write a letter to him—dated on the last day of class—explaining why they deserved the grade.

Zander, who wrote a book called The Art of Possibility, believes there are several beneficial aspects of this dramatic role reversal. First, it's a great confidence builder for his students. Second, it eliminates the often counterproductive sniping that people frequently cngage in when they think only a few will win (one reason we believe bell curve grading is so flawed). But most important, students invariably knock themselves out for that A. Zander believes that they do more to earn their own personal A than they would ever do for the traditional A given by a teacher.

We do much the same at IDEO. It starts with the hiring process. The typical jobcandidate will be interviewed by more than a dozen of us before getting the thumbs-up. The process takes time, but it's worth it in the end. You don't et hired at IDEOnless you wow a bunco us. Those that make it receive a tremendous boost, knowing that a lot oothe accomplished people think they'e talented and capable.

We belinvial u  ov one starts out roughly equal and then is given lots of chances to mess up—and to shine.

Newcomers that flourish in our environment are often offered a key role in a new project, or even an opportunity to manage a project. Age and experience aren't factors. You actually get to pick two of the three people who wil review your work, and IDEOers invariably pick team members. And since we live for projects, there's an opportunity to spread the work around.

## HOT TEAMS GIVE

We frequently buy the products we design—Palm Vs and Handsprings are ubiquitous, as is Crest Neat Squeeze in the bathrooms. Over the years, employees have found their virtual Christmas stockings stuffed with the likes of AT&T phones, Smith ski goggles, Nike sunglasses, and Polaroid I-Zone cameras. It's not practical to buy everything we help engincer or design, but we do our best.

Try giving lots of gifts and awards. But not out of routine. The creation of the gift itself can reinforce your team spirit. Even the company's holiday-season card can be an opportunity. We pride ourselves on our unusual creations, and one year even mailed out cards that contained tiny live trees. We used a space-age bonded plastic substrata, originally developed by NASA, to keep the trees alive. IDEOers pitched in after work to place three pinkie-sized redwood seedlings in each of the six hundred holiday cards.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/b3a5664c28b51de42809c1be2c2f4125d1c637eef808d9cc8940d36926d95a09.jpg)  
End of Project Awards celebrate a team member's superhuman efforts.

But gifts given by fellow team members build the strongest bonds. End-of-project awards are a great way to punctuate team accomplishments, celebrate contributions, and, yes, poke fun. What's important is that everyone play some part in creating the award. The best end-ofproject awards make light of a blunder or cleverly capture the heart of an individual's achievement. They can be strikingly simple. Early in his career, for instance, one of our studio heads sent a reversed drawing to the tooler; his awvard was a mirror on a plaque. Team members thought another engineer may have overspecified the metal enclosure on a large medical device. His "bulletproof" avard was a hunk of sheet metal laced with bullet casings. Then there was the engineer working on a photocopier that initially had some paper-handling problemswhich earned him an end-of-project avard that included pieces of paper folded like fans. Or the team leader who spent weeks on a farm in Michigan improving veterinarian tools. His end-of-project award was a dartboard of a cow's derriere.

Sometimes, of course, awards just celebrate achievement. For four days Andre Youse from our shop barely sept while trying to complete the Nightline shopping cart. His fellow shop workers rewarded him with a big Superman doll holding a small cart. And an engineer who helped hurdle a competitor's seemingly insurmountable sheath of patents was given a hatchet symbolically hacking through five hundred pages of printed patents. When an especially large joint team from two IDEO offices designed an information appliance for Italy-based Merloni, everyone gathered together for a team photo, holding up the dozens of early prototypes. The few members who missed the photo shoot were digitally added later.

But don't reserve fun for the end of a project. Some of our teams have designed custom T-shirts for their leader's birthday, and plenty of people have returned from trips to find their cffice spaces "redecorated" by their colleagues.

## HOT TEAMS SHARE

How can teams stay motivated and abreast of the latest developments? One way is to launch a clubhouse, a regular weekly or monthly meeting that functions a lot like a writers' group. The point is not just to share the latest advances and technologies. Clubhouses help get us out of our routine, pull us up above the fray, and remind us of our passion for our chosen fields. They build camaraderie in a supportive, competitive environment and offer a scmistructured method of maintaining a spontaneous, constantly evolving cutting edge. Clubhouses are part of the fabric of Silicon Valley, a culture known for user groups and home to such legendary innovation-crazy groups as the Homebrew Computer Club, which helped spark the personal computer industry.

A clubhouse can give you a sense of belonging to something larger than yourself. That's part of the reason we believe in the importance of giving tours of our workplace. If the place where you spend more than half your waking hours is considered valuable and even exciting to outsiders, think of what this does to the psyche and spirit of your own team members.

Tours can build pride and inspiration. But they're just one way to open your building and staff to the rest of the world. Over the years clients have occasionally asked us at IDEO for more than traditional product development and design. A number of companies have sent teams of engineers and designers to learn from the IDEO experience. In some cases, they've spent months at a time in our Palo Alto facilities, renting homes or apartments nearby, working on specific projects while trying to glean as much as possible about our methods. Others visit for a few weeks and then return periodically. They pick up on work practices that reinforce the culture. They try to absorb the nuances of our open-ended quick prototyping process and to observe the way it enhances the bonds betveen team members from different disciplines.

Several years ago a number of our Fortune 500 clients asked us to package a two-day version of this IDEO experience. That was the genesis of what we now call IDEO University or simply IDEO U. And just as Nightline chronicled our Deep Dive, an intensely compressed exercise in our practiccs and methods, IDEO U. has becomc an opportunity for outsiders to practice our methods firsthand. For us, it's a two-way street. We gct to continuously test and reformulate our methods, learning what works or doesn't work—for differcnt industries and teams. And our IDEO U. teams serve as ambassadors, reporting back to the rest of IDEO in meetings and informal talks.

Bye e  o    - ing, you can help keep your team on the cutting edge of innovation.

## TEAM MORALE

When peoplefeel special, they'll perform beyond your wildest dreams. It's why we throw blowout end-of-year bashes—to celebrate who and what we are. We once managed to take temporary possession of Moffet Field's blimp hangar. Amid the airplanes, a zydeco band jammed, while we rollerbladed through an indoor space the size of seven football fields.

The point isn't how much money you spend but what kind of experience you can create. The main attraction at one of our recent end-ofyear bashes was a gingerbread house design fest. We put dozens of prefab houses on tables and laid out all the supplies you could imagine—baker's tubes of confectioners' sugar, gumdrops galore, Hershey's kisses, red hots, and candy canes. The idea was so popular that more of us were hunkered down making baroque gingerbread houses than kicking up our heels on the dance floor.

Another great way to make people feel special is to let them play hooky. Sometimes the best inspiration for a team can be the Zen-like act of not doing any work at all. Like taking the afternoon off with your team to see a baseball game or watch a hot new movie (we once had a self-appointed "B team" that watched bad science-fiction films and other B movies on a regular basis). Since we've played a role in the making of a number of films, from The Abyss to Free Willy, we've taken in a matinee more than once. When The Abyss was released, we took over an entire local theater for a private screening.

You can't worry about how much time or money the fun costs. You'll get back whatever time the team lost in the next week and quickly move into the black. After all, morale isn't something that can be measured or planned. Encourage unplanned breaks during the day. At IDEO a break might be a bike ride, or blowing off tension by shooting soft foam Finger Blaster rockets at one another, or, if it's winter and you're outside the Boston office, playing hockey.

The best diversions are combined with a prank. For one office retreat, Dennis Boyle's studio sailed around San Francisco Bay. The group knew they'd be sailing by our San Francisco office at Pier 28, so they put together a giant slingshot fashioned out of a big funnel and bungee cords soon dubbed the "funnelator"—and launched a merciless water balloon attack.

Field trips can be a great form of play. Try scooting off to see something that excites or inspires your team. We regularly take in the latest exhibit at the San Jose Tech Museum. Lately, we've begun taking field trips to other companies. Several IDEOers were recently invited to a daylong visit to Industrial Light and Magic and George Lucas's incredible Skywalker Ranch. It was great fun, and who knows, we might just work together someday.

The best field trips merge fun and projects. Before joining IDEO, David Haygood hosted a wild field trip for Hexcel, the maker of high tech composite materials. Teams from Boston and California converged for an inspiring week in Arizona. In the morning they had what they called "oyster sessions"—throwing a grain of sand into the group in the hope that it might turn into a pearl. The sinewy group scheduled a competitive event each afternoon—a 10K run or a long bike ride. They met for dinner and conducted a second work session in the evening. The exercise seemed to take the pressure off and give the morning brainstorms time to jell. Several good product concepts evolved out of the mix.

## HOT TEAMS MEET

Takigfel trisplayighookyand holing gy offsi can elp keep a team motivated. But when you can takc thc energy that most companies only manage to drum up in an off-site meeting, and find it in your routine work, then you're onto something hugc. The secret, I think, is to scuttle rules, provide good food, and encourage lots of play. Here's a glimpse of one such group at IDEO.

Two Thursdays a month, Dennis Boyle's thirty-five-member studio meets "Under the Wing" for lunch, featuring pasta, enchiladas, and salads. The seats around the conference table disappear nearly as fast as the food. Latecomers drag chairs into tiny spaces, and the stragglers squeeze through the crowd to grab a plate and a place to stand.

Theamsphere s a litt  ke the kthen   ut ary. There's not really enough room for everybody, and that's part of the attraction. I try to make the Wing lunches whenever I can. It doesn't matter what's on the agenda because, strictly speaking, there is no agenda. This is playtime, a chance for show-and-tell, and, of course, a few jokes and harmless ribbing. In principle, Boyle runs the show, but his real job is just to keep the ideas flowing.

The studio uses bimonthly lunches to share insights and progress on projects. At one meeting, for instance, one of our senior designers stood up to talk about the sleek Webslate his team had just created, a "concept car" demonstrating the handheld possibilities of the new Transmeta chip. "It's eye-catching, it's for Web surfing, not so thin that it can't happen," he said, proudly passing around drawings. "The Palm concept, maybe taken to another level." Two weeks later Transmeta and Linus Torvalds unveiled the chip and Webslate at a major press conference.

A veteran IDEO team leadcr spokc cnthusiastically about how voice recognition and pen tcchnology wcre quickly coming togethcr for handheld devices. He couldn't get into thc details, but his team was building a "wearablc" computer for a very largc computcr company. Excitcd by the possibilitics, he talked about Microsoft's "ClearTypc"

screen technology and how close it was getting to the quality of typescript on paper.

Another team member showed off a bug-shaped information appliance for Generation Xers. Someone else talked about a defeatured, inexpensive cell phone for kids and the elderly, with a streamlined interface and an alternative to expensive monthly service charges. That led, naturally, to a discussion of the latest ear clip Ericsson phone and the new minuscule Nokia model, which someone had just purchased for seven hundred dollars and had already begun to disassemble.

And there was still room for a birthday celebration and some hilarious recounting of New Year's escapades, as well as nominations for the weirdest gift—an egg cuber—and the latest candidate for our collection of amazing edibles—hologram candy.
