Finally, the team discussed renting a go-cart track in the spring to celebrate the completion of a major project.

We've all endured countless dull meetings over the years, but most of our studios have the team meeting down pat. What makes it work is the enthusiasm, the show-and-tell, the humor, the irreverence.

## HOT TEAMS LOOK THE PART

Whatever your current T-shirt budget is, double it. T-shirts cost pracically nothing yet offer the return of team solidarity. By definition they're about teams. IDEO veterans have a couple dozen T-shirts. The best are edgy, inspired by projects. Once hooked, youll find yourself unable to resist making more T-shirts than you ever imagined. T-shirts are like quick and dirty prototypes, and if your team or company has a number of projects going on, every few months youll have something new to wear. Even if your team doesn't boast a hot profile, it can still sport attitude. Our accounting and marketing departments made a fleece vest that proudly shouted, "IDEO Overhead."

oa wr ur passion  seatshirts, biccemessnger bags,r basball caps. The original Macintosh team members were allowed to etch their signatures into the machine's injection molding tools, thus leaving their literal imprimatur inside the product's caseworks. Programmers working on cool softwvare are notorious for leaving their names and nicknames buried within the source code. Let irreverence sprout when the spirit moves you. Five early Yahooans lobbied for the right to get their cars "hrik-wrapped" with a plastic purple and yellow Yahoo sheet. Grassroots team efforts like these are infectious. It's no surprise that some of the world's strongest brands started with a hot team.

## HOT TEAMS NEED CHARACTERS

Remember that innovation stalls with the status quo. Celebrate your team members' differences. Many years ago one of the most brilliant engineers at Atari was, well, eccentric. He lost his apartment, so he literally moved into the office, taking residence in a makeshift loft that fellow team members built for him. Incredibly, the only entrance was through a conference room. He'd crank away all night, and you'd arrive in the morning to find all the amazing things he'd accomplished. But you couldn't talk to him till the afternoon when he clambered down from his loft. If you think this sounds bizarre, sometimes that's how innovation happens. Amazon's first and most prolific reviewer was practically entombed in books. Every wall of his office was crammed with volumes stretching up to the ceiling. His sleeping bag was tucked under his makeshift desk. He rarely went home.

Hot groups tend to have at least one or two certifiable weirdos. Look around your group or team to get a sense of the different roles people play. Most teams have everything from dramatic leads to character actors, writers, and, yes, directors. Characters round out your company, and we all need a few individuals we're not sure what to do with. We've been lucky to find many of these talented types at IDEO.

## EIGHT CRAZY CHARACTERS FOR HOT GROUPS

## The Visionary

We all occasionally make the mistake of not rising above the fracas long enough to ponder what's next. At IDEO we have an elder statesman who inspires affection and enthusiasm among his flock. Over the years he's recruited key team members with divergent talents and personalities. He coined the term "interaction design," anticipated the importance of computer-aided design, and is the one most likely to figure out what's next.

## The Troubleshooter

It may be politically incorrect to say it, but business bogs down with excessive politeness. There are plenty of times when we all could use someone who doesn't suffer fools gladly and cuts to the chase. At IDEO that's the first person David hired, IDEO's chief problem solver and fix-it man. His clear thinking and quick actions can right a drifting brainstormer or put a wandering project back on course. Devoid of political fears, our troubleshooter has occasionally rubbed a worker or client the wrong way, but his clarity of thought and expression has saved us dozens of times.

## The Iconoclast

Every great organization needs a counterpoint, someone who challenges the status quo. We've got just that person, someone who doesn't buy into all of our ideas about teamwork and group innovation. Frankly, we're happy to have his contrarian view. Our iconoclast is a Silicon Valley design legend who broke the mold. He's got an uncanny ability to spot key business trends and strategic alliances for IDEO. And he's seldom wrong.

## The Pulse Taker

Every company needs a heart. The head of our human-factors group makes sure we make a personal connection. One day she might tg be hind a Japanese salesman to see how he really uses a portable computer. Another day she might invent archetypal characters who form the movielike narrative of how people might use a product or service. She inspires IDEO designers and engineers by unlocking the all-toooften-overlooked insights and patterns of exceptional and ordinary people.

## The Craftsman

Just as we believe multidisciplinary teams are critical, we also believe that it's invaluable to bring certain talents and technologies in-house. In the age of the Internet and virtual corporations it's easy to think that expertise is just a click awvay. But you can't outsource all your core competencies. IDEO is in the business of making stuff, and we wouldn't be the same if our magic elves weren't on staff. IDEO's machine and model shops are our in-house alchemy labs, the place where proverbial napkin sketches take physical shape, where model makers, machinists, and skilled craftsmen interpret and stretch the boundaries of design and engineering. Some of our best work happens when an openminded IDEO engineer or designer leaves enough room for the shop to play.

## The Technologist

Every company needs someone who has read all the latest journals, can unlock Byzantine problems, or, when he doesn't know the answer himself, can point to seemingly obscure sources. In other words, a geek. We've got a resident technologist. His knowledge of complex materials, mechanisms, and seemingly arcane technologies provides a wealth of raw material for IDEO engineers and designers. He can't help but probe the deeper connections between things. Just by his presence, he can change the perspective on a project.

## The Entrepreneur

Bright, innovative, but didn't quite find a niche within the firm. We've all encountered such people in large organizations, and if truth be told, it happens on occasion at IDEO. The solution for our entrepreneur was a spin-off that's since come back into the IDEO fold. It's a quirky, positive influence on our culture. Our entrepreneur constantly experiments with how to quickly brainstorm, prototype, and communicate innovations. Quite by accident, he's become one of our masters of process and a brainstormer extraordinaire.

## The Cross-Dresser

We all know this kind of individual. Enthusiastic, self-educated, and self-motivated, cross-dressers have learned so much since they left school ten or twenty years ago that it might as well have been a past life. Their thirst for their chosen occupation makes them ideal team members. At IDEO the typical cross-dresser might have studied engineering but fallen in love with design. We've got so many crossdressers that it's almost the norm. You know you're open to cross-dressing when team members' formal training doesn't matter, when energy, abilities, and personality are the main qualifications.

Teams may be ground zero for innovation at most companies, but they start with individuals. I'm sure you've got plenty of extraordinary people in your organization. Match them to projects, challenge them, and give them a chance to blossom, and I think you'll be surprised at the results. There's nothing like a hot team to get the job done.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/c57ca2a69673b026bd82ecb9dbc02dc5529a2036033cac18de9bc4cfe30995a5.jpg)

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/595afc5e46f79dbf7725db4d59ed9f6e0a14815fd910955d88586289eed7cca4.jpg)

he problem seemed insurmountable. We'd designcd some new style would stay fog-free in the freezing conditions found on most ski slopes. We needed to test the gogglcs in the wild. But it was the middle of summer in sunny California. Our client at Smith Sports Optics didn't have the time or the budget to fly us down to New Zealand, where it's winter in July.

We couldn't wait five months for winter to come around, but there was another possibility. Tivo blocks south of IDEO's Palo Alto offices stands a classic family-run ice cream factory. The folks at Peninsula Creamery thought us a bit crazy when we floated our wild request for a nontraditional use of their industrial-sized freezers, but as long as we didn't eat all the ice cream, why should they care?

Industrial design studio head Paul Bradley and a handful of team members bundled up in parkas and long johns. The exercise bike and fan (to simulate wind) were found by tapping out an e-mail (the way all sorts of unusual requests get filled at IDEO). Since nearly everyone at IDEO is a bike enthusiast, it wasn't hard finding volunteers to pedal for an hour in an ice cream freezer, as observers snapped photos of their perspiring faces and, fortunately, not-too-foggy prototype goggles.

Prototyping is problem solving. It's a culture and a language. You can prototype just about anything—a new product or service, or a special promotion. What counts is moving the ball forward, achieving some part of your goal.

Not wasting time.

Prototyping is a state of mind. Think of the best moviemakers. We don't idolize them just because they strike us as glamorous players in a profession filled with beautiful people. They're also people who get things done. People who make movies prototype every day, just to get by. Consider the following story. Larry Shubert of IDEO worked for a few months on the set of the underwater thriller The Abyss. If you haven't seen it, all you need to know is that most of the movie happens beneath two thousand feet of water. That critical fact forced the Abyss team, led by Titanic director James Cameron, to seek out a gigantic body of water to film the action. A pool wouldn't do, but what about a half-finished nuclear power plant in South Carolina? The main containment vessel was a tremendous 250 feet in diameter and 50 feet deep. Cameron turned it into the world's largest freshwater tank—almost 7 million gallons. This being a film, there wasn't time to plan. There was also no time to build scaffolding, so parts of the interior set were created literally as the water poured in, with workers in rowboats furiously painting the walls as the water rose.

That's the right metaphor. No matter your business, no matter your experience, odds are the water's rising. You don't have time, and if you don't act soon, you—or your project—will be underwater. There probably isn't time to do things the regular way—or by the book.

When Shubert and a couple of other IDEO folks were told they were flying to South Carolina to work on The Abyss, they suddenly realized they'd be working underwater and needed to be scubacertified. One problem. The scuba course was going to take weeks. "We said, 'There just isn't enough time," Shubert recalls. "Well go through the books on our own." After a little negotiation, they made a deal. Schubert and the others read the books, did the dives, and passed the test—in a couple of days instead of a few weeks.

## KID STUFF

Many of us first learned about prototyping as kids, doing class projects or, to help pass those long summer afternoons, making up games. My brother David, for example, has been building things and then trying to make them better for as long as I can remember. We had a snowy Ohio winter the year I was six years old, and David started a series of increasingly complex snow construction projects in the backyard. He started with the basics—three-tiered snowmen—but soon progressed to whole forts by lining up snowmen shoulder-to-shoulder to form four walls.

Looking for the next revision of his prototype fort, David briefly considered a two-story model, which he thankfully abandoned when he hit upon the idea of using a cardboard box to make snow "bricks." We were industriously building snow fort 2.0 with our adobe-style construction techniques when David hit upon an idea for revision 2.1: adding water to each brick so that it would freeze to a solid (and incredibly heavy) block of ice, which David hoped might help the fort last until Memorial Day.

You're probably coming to the conclusion that my brother was incredibly driven from an early age, which may be true but is not the point. With our little team, we built the mother of all snow forts that year, and we did it by building a bunch of prototypes and then figuring out how to make each one better than the one before. David's gift was his faith in the process. He knew that if he kept trying out new techniques he'd make dramatic improvements.

I think a lot of us understood this intuitively as children and lost it gradually as we matured. David's influence has made this childlike curiosity and enthusiasm second nature at IDEO. A playful, iterative approach to problems is one of the foundations of our culture of prototyping. It can be part of your workplace too.

Veteran IDEO studio head Dennis Boyle approaches client meetings like mini-shows. He often says, "Never go to a meeting without a prototype," which we now jokingly refer to as Boyle's Law. In fact, Dennis often brings several prototypes, as well as interesting materials to spark the muse. It's part of the iterative process. "We kind of shock people by how many things we'll build sometimes," he says. When a project's just getting off the ground, client meetings may be weekly. Dennis wants to make his mistakes—and discoveries—as soon as possible. As he puts it, he looks for any opportunity to make models, sitting down with his team and saying, "OK, what are we going to build for next week and the week after and a couple of weeks out?"

There's always something that wasn't clear at the last meeting that you can clarify by offering distinct choices, such as showing how you might implement a hinge or mount a display or control a specific mechanism. When the project is especially complex, prototyping is a way of making progress when the challenges seem insurmountable. "In engineering, if you have more variables than equations, you normally have an unsolvable situation," explains Dennis. "But on the other hand, if you just take some good guesses and fill in the blanks on some of them, you'll get some answers."

Focused prototyping helps resolve little critical problems one by one. IDEO studio head Sean Corcorran's mantra is "build to learn." When he was working on a chair for Vecta, for example, his team reached a point where a height adjustment lever that tilted with the chair became critical. They didn't build the whole chair. They didn't even build the whole tilt mechanism. They just built the little lever and its interface with thc release mechanism. It only took a couple of hours. The finished prototype fit in Sean's hand and it quickly demonstrated that the principle would work.

A understandable if the process still seems undefined. "All well and good." you may say, "but how does this help me if innovation is't a dailyritual? Andhow o I prototype anew rvice r bsiess?"

Quick prototyping is about acting before you've got the answers, uau sider Jeff Bezos's story of the birth of Amazon.com. The business rookie launched one of the Internet's first multibillion-dollar behemoths literally on the run.

Bez's saga puts wings on the Slicon Vally clicéof starting your company in a garage. His e-commerce juggernaut began with one startling statistic. In the spring of 1994 he happened upon a prediction that annual Web growth would ramp up at an astounding 2,300 percent. Inspired by the market potential of such explosive growth, he madly scratched out a list of things he could sell on-line—everything from music to clothing—before settling on books.

Within weeks he quit his cushy Wall Street job,called a moving van, and "packed and had our things taken before the van knew where they were going." Incredibly, Bezos hadn't yet figured out where to cast his e-commerce seeds. His short list included Boulder, Portland, and Lake Tahoe. Unable to make up his mind, he instructed the moving van to simply head west. The next day, Bezos phoned and told the driver he'd decided on Seattle, a city with plenty of high-tech workers.

Bezos was doing exactly what innovators do every day: breaking a problem down into its parts, making on-the-fly decisions in parallel. The ordinary thing to do would have been to stay put until he had decided upon a city. How could a moving van begin its journey without knowing a state, let alone an address?

But Bezos gained a day of Internet time by launching his ship before he'd charted his New World. As his moving van rolled westward from New York on the interstate, Bezos flew to Texas and picked up a beatup family car. While his wife, MacKenzie, drove, he sat in the passenger seat, pecking out a business plan on his laptop and punching out calls on his cellular. Think of it: nothing on paper, no place to land his imagined company, yet he was already hurtling toward his destiny. He took a detour through Northern California to interview potential vice presidents of development, and he retained a Seattle attorney by phone to incorporate his on-line venture with the unlikely name of "Cadabra." He still had no idea where he was going to live or how he would fund his scheme, but he had no time to waste because "when it's growing 2,300 percent a year, weeks are important.

Bezos's story would make a great movie about how rapid prototyping can give you a business edge. The dynamo entrepreneur was sorting out the pieces of the puzzle as he sailed west, trying to figure out what his Website would do, where he'd be, whom he'd hire—and what he'd call the darn thing.

Bezos could make an early blunder—like the clunky Cadabra—because he was carving himself out so much extra time. Once he'd rented suburban Seattle digs to house his venture in the proverbial garage, he applied the same "time is precious" logic to Amazon.com. Get it up, get it out, was the order of the day. Function preceded style and editorial content. Low on graphics and animation, Amazon.com loaded fast and excelled at the basics—making it easy to find and buy books.

By the time the dust cleared, the bricks-and-mortar booksellers were playing catch-up. The next time you kick off a project, try sparking your engines with a little New World Amazon-style energy. Think about tackling problems when you don't have the answers. Once you get in gear, you'll be surprised at how easily some of the solutions appear.

## MAKE YOUR LUCK

Prototyping doesn't just solve straightforvard problems. Call it serenopen up new possibilities of discovery. It's the same method that's helped scientists unlock some of the greatcst secrets of nature.

"I decided that no harm could come from spcnding a few days building backbone models," said 1962 Nobel Prize wvinner James Watson, referring to the metal prototypcs he and fellow gencticist Francis Crick used to model and test thcir hypotheses. "Perhaps a week of solid fiddling with the molecular models would be necessary to make us absolutely sure we had the right answer."

Although their academic colleagues were skeptical of the approach, Watson and Crick's freethinking style and openness to twodimensional and three-dimensional prototyping helped guide them toward the momentous discovery of the structure of DNA. Even Watson sometimes wondered if he was floundering. "My doodling of the bases on paper at first got nowhere. Not until the middle of the next week, however, did a nontrivial idea emerge. It came while I was drawing the fused rings of adenine on paper."

Doodling, drawving, modeling. Sketch ideas and make things, and you're likely to encourage accidental discoveries. At the most fundamental level, what we're talking about is play, about exploring borders.

Some of the bravest prototypers in the world are the people who build experimental aircraft—and the test pilots who fly them. The Bell X-1 aircraft flown by Chuck Yeager to break the sound barrier in 1947 was sometimes referred to in news stories as "a bullet with wings." What's lesser known, however, is that the first prototype actually was a bullet. When you're creating something new to the world, you can't look over your shoulder to see what your competitors are doing; you have to find another source of inspiration. During development of the historic aircraft, designers at Bell examined a fifty-caliber bullet flying at supersonic speed. The bullet was aerodynamically stable even as it broke the sound barrier, so Bell's engineers created a thirty-onefoot-long fuselage in the shape of that bullet, adding some stubby wings and a powerful rocket engine. Thanks to the brave prototype testing by men like Yeager—there wasn't even an ejcctor seat in the

X-1—we gained knowledge about supersonic flight that led to the creation of production aircraft like the Concorde and modern jet fighters.

Of course, we don't pretend to be Chuck Yeager or Nobel Prize winners, and IDEO makes no claim to discovering anything remotely as important as the structure of DNA. But the process of prototyping can spark little innovations, the sort that can be the difference between a product's success or its failure.

When designing a new video game controller for Logitech, we sent drawings to IDEO's machine shop to have them knock out a prototype steering wheel to be made of rigid ABS plastic with a black rubber coating. But the shop was completely out of black rubber, which, with a deadline looming, seemed like a potential disaster. So they molded the steering wheel out of red rubber, the only kind they could lay their hands on in time. By chance, the client loved the brightly colored wheel, and the little accident spun the product in a new direction, inspiring the game maker to introduce a line of fire-engine red steering wheels.

Brendan Boyle, Dennis's brother, invents toys at IDEO's Skyline group and pitches them to the major toy companies. One of his more successful discoveries came while gluing together the prototype of a toy football. Brendan and his team had hatched the idea of creating a foam-rubber football that had built-in tee-wings that kept the ball propped up so you could automatically tee it up for placekicking. Charlie Brown would have loved the idea.

Brendan fashioned his first prototype the quick and dirty way. He simply took an existing football and set about gluing wings on it. But as he began applying the wings, he saw that it would be easier to follow the existing curved line of the football.

"Oh, this is kind of cool," Brendan remembers thinking, seeing that if his wings traced the curves, it would look like a propeller. He quickly "punted" on the idea of a self-teeing football. Once he started tossing balls around the office, it became clear that the curved wings had a more noble purpose—straightening wobbly throws into perfect spirals. And so the Aerobie football was born, one of Skyline's most successful toys.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/29cb173de618f34fee01e96cd38692e28dd752c1d06e691aedf7a02d83a0180e.jpg)  
Get in the habit of making stuff or airing trial balloon ideas. Odds are you'll bounce your way to the insight that may inspire your next breakthrough.

## PROTOTYPES BEAT PICTURES

Prototypes aren't only about making giant discoveries like DNA or little ones like a hot-selling toy. There's also something wonderfully tangible about a prototype.

It's easy to reject a dry report or a flat drawing. But models often surprise, making it easier to change your mind and accept new ideas. Or make hard choices, such as forgoing costly and complex features. Years of experience have taught us that prototyping is also part performance, that if the act isn't well orchestrated and substantial, the audience gets antsy. And executives, understandably, already have enough on their minds.

Give your management team a report, and it's likely they won't be able to make a crisp decision. But a prototype is almost like a spokesperson for a particular point of view, crystallizing the group's feedback and keeping things moving.

We believe in that great old saying, a picture is worth a thousand words. Only at IDEO, we've found that a good prototype is worth a thousand pictures. Somehow you up the data rate. Give people two or three very concrete choices. Create a situation where the audience can hash out ideas, and you can run with them or show why they might not fly. Bring lots of hardware in that shows the size and shape of things, how parts fit and work together, and people will likely say, "Oh, I never thought of that."

What kind of prototype works best in different situations is itself a moving target. Good prototypes don't just communicate—they persuade. If you're developing a consumer product, you might prototype out of foam or plastic or wood. But there are many other ways to prototype. Sometimes we quickly film what we like to think of as movie trailers, to show the highlights and essence of what a product, service, or business may become. If you're working on a project that has a service or human component, sometimes it helps to have team members—and even clients—express the project through archetypal characters in a little improvisational skit.

Living, moving prototypes can help shape your ideas.

## BIT BY BIT

All too often, we've seen how employees prepare presentations for upper management in large corporations. Someone is given a few months to come up with a new product, service, or marketing campaign. More often than not, they don't solicit enough input or feedback early onthere are no incremental reviews. When the boss finally sees what they've been working on ll these months, it's either a happy surprise o tol s.Ty t  hein  q two-minute warning from the moment you start on a new project. Instead of the long bal try to get a few yards, passing to the sidelines to stop the clock. Just keep the momentum going.

We pitch presentations in stages, show the rough sketch, the cheap foam model, and use them to right the course before it's too late. As Dennis says, if you only have till next Thursday, you actually have to decide on some part of the product. You've got to cut and run and tell the shop to work up a model.

Or then again, you might buy your prototype from a dime store. Tiventy years ago IDEO veteran Jim Yurchenco bought part of a key prototype for the first Apple mouse off the discount shelves of the Walgreens store across the street. A butter dish bought for a couple of bucks was just the right size to hold the mouse's rolling ball. That isn't to say that we didn't make dozens of subsequent prototypes. It just shows that what counts is expressing the idea quickly—and cheaply.

This isn't a concept that only works for start-ups. A few years back, Wells Fargo bank was struggling with a feeling we know all too well. If someone shadows you while you're tapping away at an ATM, you probably get uncomfortable or even fearful, especially if you're withdrawing a couple hundred dollars and the streets are deserted. So when IDEO took on the bank's project, we observed how people behave at ATMs and began brainstorming all kinds of ideas, including expensive addons like video cameras and periscopes. But it turned out that sticking a \$1.80 fish-eye mirror (the kind that truckers stick on their big side mirrors) above the ATM worked just fine. Sure, we could have pushed them toward more high-tech, expensive solutions. But prototyping can also remind you that sometimes the most obvious, simplest solution is the best.

Prototyping is a dance. Sometimes the music doesn't move you or your steps fail. But that's no reason to stop. Just as writer's block happens when writers stop writing, so, too, does innovation grind to a halt when prototypes stop being built. When the muse fails you, don't mope at your desk. Make something.

"I just prototype a bad idea and maybe shoot the hell out of it," says one of our Silicon Valley—based engineers. He'll often know it's not the solution he wants, but if he prototypes it, he can shoot it down faster and then find out what doesn't work or perhaps discover something new. Prototypes can be a source of creation and insurance. When all else fails, prototype till you're silly.

## HOW APPLE DID IT

It's easy to rhapsodize on the value of modeling. But sometimes only a real-life story will show you how the process actually works. And few products better demonstrate the importance of quick prototyping than the Apple Duo Dock. The time was the early nineties. Apple wanted to introduce a portable that could easily dock in a home base with a standard monitor and keyboard. The portable had to be small and thin, and the company initially anticipated some sort of mechanical dock using levers they'd already seen.

It bears remembering that for decades—long before the debut of the iMac—Apple had been a leader in industrial design. In the early eighties, without Apple the profession almost wouldn't have existed in Silicon Valley. PC clones were manufactured, not designed, and we oa debt  atitde tothis colorulcompany whic alvays renized that behind every transaction was a real person.

But with the Duo Dock, Apple had the usual pressures. It's natural and increase risks. It seemed like the laptop would dock mechanically, but we tried not to let that constrain our brainstorm. We were searching for a metaphor. At the time, Dennis Boyle had a three-year-old who'd already figured out how to pop a video into a VCR. If the TV was already on, it would just start up and play his carton. Didn't that kidfriendly experience fit perfectly into the tradition of the Mac graphical interface, the wonderful, playful simplicity that was Apple's trademark?

What if we could do something that direct—and powerful? What if you could just pop the Duo into the dock like a video—and it would sweep in effortlessly and play like a movie? Though the idea sounded promising, Apple at first wvas reluctant to take any chances. The company preferred a traditional mechanical eject system. The VCR approach sounded expensive, like something that might delay the launch date.

But the VCR metaphor stuck with the IDEO team. Dennis and company brainstormed again. They quickly hatched (and later patented) their key innovation, which involved combining a toy motor with a clever gear mechanism. The other option, using traditional scientific motors, was pricey-anywhere from ten to thirty dollars. If they'd used the sort of precision motor people expected them to use, cost alone would have killed the idea. But toy motors are dirt cheapabout \$1.25—and surprisingly reliable.

Dennis gambled. He temporarily suspended work on developing a mechanical lever and told the team to make it with the toy motor. A couple of weeks later he brought in a working model to Apple. They were so excited they took it around to everybody and got instant buyoff. Sure, it cost a little more and would take a few weeks longer. But Apple recognized that the simplicity and smoothness of the VCR-like approach would give it a competitive advantage. And more data protection. One risk with mechanical docks is that users try to eject their computer before files or the operating system software are ready. Since the motorized approach was itself software-controlled, the Duo would only eject when ready.

Still, there were problems to solve. When early models spat the Duo out so forcefully that the expensive laptop looked precariously close to crashing to the ground, we added an oil-filled damper to soften the movement. More than a dozen different prototypes were made. Early models were fashioned of particleboard and ABS plastic, testing levers, and soon the toy motor mechanism. They were often cobbled together in an afternoon.

The working prototype itself became a celebrity, like a popular exhibit model at a science fair. These were the days when Apple CEO John Sculley would stroll through Apple's labs to get a firsthand look at the latest projects. This particular day, he was supposed to review five different projects in about half an hour, but once he spied the Duo Dock prototype, he didn't budge. The other projects had static charts or simulated images on the screen. The Duo Dock was entertaining. You could open it up and see its clever mechanisms working away. Sculley wanted to know all about it, watching, listening, and talking about it.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/ae9ef026dad5f22c253d7745fcf35aec0f555667a104393375fc73a006c0c578.jpg)  
Nearly every prototype sparked improvements during development of Apple's Duo Dock.

Apple ordered a couple dozen copies of the next prototype so it could demonstrate the concept to customers and partners before the real ones rolled out of the factory. In the few weeks before the new prototypes arrived, the original became so popular that it required its own conference room. You had to schedule a meeting if you wanted to see the prototype. The Duo Dock earned great reviews, but we're equally proud of how cheaply Apple was able to manufacture the innovation. Fear of added expenses nip countless innovations in the bud. But the toy-motor-powered mechanism embodying the VCR approach required less than seven dollars in parts—roughly comparable to the cost of some purely mechanical solutions.

To me, the Duo Dock is a classic case study of why an iterative approach works. Prototype with energy and enthusiasm, and you've got a good chance of hitting upon the very feature or product that resonates with your customer.

## FORGET CONGRESS

Okay, so maybe you don't make products and are having a hard time seeing the value of prototyping for your business or service. You don't believe the shoe fits for every industry. What about a national park? If the federal government can prototype on the fly, isn't that a good sign that even the most daunting bureaucratic obstacles are no excuse, that with the right energy and ideas anyone can innovate?

In 1997 the Golden Gate National Recreational Area was struggling like a geriatric multinational corporation, unsure of its identity and future. A patchwork of roughly two dozen sites strewn along seventy miles of rugged Northern California coastline, the parks lacked funding and volunteers, and—despite crown jewels like Muir Woods and Alcatraz—many of its riches were barely known to the public.

Board member Mickey Drexler, chairman of the Gap, knew they had a problem. Nobody, not even fellow board member and advertising guru Rich Silverstein, knew exactly what the GGNRA was—or what it was selling. "We can't even define it ourselves," Drexler declared during a key powwow. "We can't even put it on a T-shirt."

The T-shirt idea set Silverstein (famed for his celebrity-studded "Got Milk?" campaign) to thinking. He brainstormed with his partner Jeff Goodby. They quickly recognized that the "Recreation Area's" runon namc stunk. Why not just change it? Well, for one thing, that kind of change requires an act of Congress.

Unless you break the rules. So they hatched the "Golden Gate National Parks," earned the ire of federal bureaucrats, and forged ahead. Following thc Gap chairman's logic, they hired the gifted illustrator Michael Schwab, who created the marvelously simple " brand look" for the first of many icons—a sweeping image of the Golden Gate Bridge and the parklands on either side (never mind that the bridge itself wasn't technically part of the park). The icons were so hot that vhen they premiered in bus shelters people actually stole them. A barrage of phone calls from consumers made clear that plenty of others were dying to buy them. And so the name—and numerous classic imagesburst forth and multiplied on T-shirts, swveatshirts, coffee mugs, and calendars. In just two years, the park raised \$26 million in charitable contributions, launched a couple of profitable retail stores (you've gotta like a federal agency that invents profit centers), and recruited fifteen thousand new volunteers, helping to put the shine back on a national treasure.

The entrepreneurial, rule-bucking Golden Gate National Parks is now the poster child of the national park service, and the same bureaucrats who bemoaned its shoot-from-the-hip style now come to study the masters, treating the park like a living Harvard Business School case study. The best irony of all is that if they'd waited for Congress, they wouldn't have gotten past Go. The feds still consider the original, cumbersome "Golden Gate National Recreation Area" the official and legal namc.

They can keep it.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/1b568863fca6d83cb96b32abf6549f127adfc8880c3f249b351b012c80856a2c.jpg)

rototyping, brainstorming, and observations. These are the fundamentals, the reading, writing, and arithmetic of innovation. Great teams provide the charge that makes these basic skills flow throughout a company.

But just as brainstorming doesn't exist apart from teams (no, we don't count solo effort like thinking in the shower as brainstorming) the process of innovation doesn't happen in a vacuum. Innovation needs teams. And teams need places to thrive and grow.

Innovation flourishes in greenhouses. What do I mean by a greenhouse? A place where the elements are just right to foster the growth of good ideas. Where there's heat, light, moisture, and plenty of nurturing. The greenhouse we're talking about, of course, is the workplace, the way spaces take shape in offices and teams work together.

Perhaps you consider office space secondary to the nitty-gritty process of innovation. Well, at IDEO we consider it one of our premier assets. A few years ago I was quoted in Fortune magazine in a cover story on "The End of the Job" as saying, "Hire the right people, and everything else will take care of itself." Life is not quite that simple, of course, but hiring talented people is the top job of every business unit manager in the world. And top people tell us they join IDEO not only because we lead our industry but also because of our physical and cultural environment. Our office spaces help make innovation happen.

If that still doesn't sway you, how about a little bottom-line argument? At most companies, office space is the second biggest expense category after salary and benefits. Every competitive company knows it has to pay well for top talent. Why shouldn't you pay the same attention to how you create the spaces in which this talent must perform? Athletes need proper facilities. Why not workers?

Creating great office space may be one of the hardest parts of the innovation puzzle. It doesn't happen simply by hiring forward-thinking architects or leasing cool buildings. Indeed, some of IDEO's best spaces have evolved by eschewving any sort of overriding master plan. We take the opposite of the Dilbert approach. It's pretty simple. When it comes to office space, the fewer rules the better.

That said, we think we're pretty good at creating office space that works—and looks great. Along the way, we've developed some basic tenets that will help you in making your workplace better fitted to innovation. Start by building neighborhoods and thinking in terms of projects. Make building blocks to create playful, flexible foundations for your workers. Throughout the process, remember the spirit of prototyping. Space should evolve with teams and projects in much the same way as do your plans for an innovative new product. Encourage hot teams to find or create a team icon.

Finally, don't forget that your spaces should tell stories-—about your workers and your company.

## BUILDING NEIGHBORHOODS

Our offices at 700 High Street in Palo Alto have perhaps been photographed by more magazines than any other IDEO space. The flights of fancy are great, but the most important feature for companies trying to create spaces that support innovation are closer to the ground: the neighborhoods and personal touches. High above float the colorful umbrellas and national flags; a full-sized jib sails just a wave away from a snowboard with boots but no boarder; and in the corner glares a defant pirate flag.At ground level, visitors are often struck by the plasicbeaded curtains some people use as doors or the Christmas tree lights that shine year-round. The main pathway leads past our murals and project displays, a wall that illustrates a medical project we worked on to more efficiently grow human skin for burn victims, and the GM Hughes charging station still used today for General Motors' EV1 electric vehicles.

We believe in the importance of neighborhoods and community in fostering innovation. Try creating spaces that draw workers in and encourage interaction. Our spaces, for instance, have been laid out so that a central asymmetrical table functions like a park for three or four team members. Prototypes, sketches, or blueprints crowd these tables, and if workers aren't hunkered down, their Palm Vs or cell phones usually signal they're nearby. Everybody who lives by that "park" can see at a glance whether their neighbors are at home, but they can also slide translucent Lexan barn doors closed if they need to buckle down and work privately on something. It's a wonderful mix of community and privacy that seems to offer much of the seclusion of traditional offices without the separation.

Our Palo Alto offices are great examples of how neighborhoods and communities can spill into the streets. We started in a small office in Palo Alto, just a few blocks down the street from Stanford University. Now we've got seven modest offices housing about 160 workers, all within a few blocks of one another. (IDEO also has offices in Europe, Asia, and sprinkled across North America.) There's a reason why we don't cram all our Palo Alto staff into the same building. Smaller offices make it easy for our studio teams to put their own stamp on their space. Each building's character and personality reflect its workers and their particular blend of projects.

Spaced along pleasant, treelined High Street, just off Palo Alto's bustling University Avenue, the offices—and our workers—enjoy the local buzz. We bump into one another when going out for a sandwich or a cappuccino, small clusters forming outside an office. IDEO is a microcosm of a college campus, with ample opportunities for serendipitous encounters, as well as secluded, focused work. Since most of our spaces lack internal halls, High Street has been called the "hallways of IDEO," the places where spontaneous interactions take place.

We aren't alone in looking to turn a walk around the campus into an innovation opportunity. Swedish telephony powerhouse Ericsson has made this idea of communication-on-the-go central to its new London headquarters. An abstract, curvaceous staircase links floors, giving plenty of room for the company's handset-holding workers to stop and meet. Indeed, the company says the building's principal idea is to "increase the natural foundation for meetings between people."

But while grand, architectural gestures like these are important, it's the personal nature of neighborhoods that drives innovation. At IDEO people celebrate their space with prototypes they've worked on, found art, and clever mechanical toys. One of my favorite corners looks like something out of a bike shop. There's a hat rack full of cycling helmet prototypes, a plywood pendulum with a heavy metal bal to test them, a phenomenally light carbon racing wheel, and a clever folding bike lock of hardened steel.

At nearly every corner, mobile steel Metro shelves are brimming with foam prototypes or parts. If someone's got a hobby or interest, you'll find it in his or her space, everything from cell phone collections to the mishmash of horribly designed toys and gizmos. As for the bizarre, IDEOer Roby Stancel favors a Styrofoam dress shop model's head to store and display his electrical components (sort of a cyberpunk hairdo). There are also plenty of surprises: quirky wall cutouts and window slits that afford unexpected views and provide amusing displays for knickknacks.

Look around your office. Do workers have places for impromptu meetings? Is there a comfortable blend of openness and privacy? And equally important, are workers encouraged to celebrate their work and hobbies in their space? If you can't tell when you're moving from one neighborhood to another, you probably don't have neighborhoods.

## THINK PROJECT, THINK PERSONAL

Visitors areoten struck by the anythinggs, kindergarten look u offices. But our innovation edge isn't the color, wild materials, and tys. P y I ay  The reason our offices seem to work is that nearly everyone has a say. We're often design the overall pattern of their space and then let team members mold their own nooks and crannies as they see fit.

While IDEOers are encouraged to sketch out their identity like finger painters, we prefer watercolors to oil paints. Everything from office partitions to files and desks are easily movable. We've designed and built little carts to neatly cluster the web of power and telecommunications wires. IWhen a team member moves, it's as simple as disconnecting and pushing the cart to their new location. We've got a shrink-wrapping technique so that you don't even have to take things off the shelves before you roll them away. Space is the team and the work. If a member wants to jump aboard another project, he or she needs to be able to quickly take off and land in another building or office.

How do you keep space evolving and elastic? One way is not to take your space or yourself too seriously. Just as IDEOers really don't earn a traditional window office by achievement or rank, our culture frowns on the idea that anyone's status or space is sacrosanct. How wild can it get? One studio head returned from vacation to find his office entombed in a plaster wall. Still another returned from Hawaii to find his desk and files sitting on a custom-built redwood deck, complete with beer cooler, patio table, and plastic pink flamingo. And another returned to find his transformed into an "executive washroom," with a linoleum floor, shower curtain, and a toilet where his office chair used to be.

This may sound a bit extreme, but companies that depend on the creativity of their staff give them free reign when it comes to space. At Pixar, one worker's station resembles a tiki hut, complete with bamboo door and roof; another evokes a child's bedroom, with oversized stuffed animals and a bunk bed. Why should businesses encourage such playfulness? Well, Pixar's freewheeling, independent approach to space has helped the company make some pretty darned creative blockbusters. Their first three feature films, Toy Story, A Bug's Life, and Toy Story 2, have collectively topped a billion dollars at the box office, and that doesn't count all those toys and licensing deals.

But I'd be the first to agree that most companies don't see the connection between creative space and innovation. We were recently on a British television show that cut back and forth between David's office and the office of a traditional Fortune 500 CEO. The contrast was dramatic and spoke volumes about the cultural differences between the organizations as embodied in their physical spaces: David's office with a thousand eclectic items, the flotsam and jetsam of twenty years in hands-on product innovation, versus the stately CEO suite with a large couch and blank walls: no computer, no papers, not even neatly filed memos, just one of the company's antique consumer products carefully positioned on an otherwise empty credenza in the background. At IDEO we're never tidy, but all the mess is not madness. When David wants to make a point to a visitor, he's like a well-outfitted puppeteer all the props are within arms' reach.

## BUILDING BLOCKS

Building blocks are fun. They become part f your company's space toolbox. They actually help you innovate.

The cheapest, simplest materials can inspire workers and become a building block that takes shape throughout your offices. Take these milk-crate-sized foam cubes you'll find throughout our offices. By chance, Douglas Dayton, who heads up our Boston office, did some acoustics consulting in aformer lie. To dampen the clatter f nosy heaters and air conditioners in IDEO's brand-new Boston office, he arranged to have a local firm make thirty gray soft-foam cubes about fifteen inches high. He wasn't thinking only of function. The building's icon is a geometric cube sculpture out front that represents IDEO's four-square logo. With a little glue and piano wire, Douglas fashioned his thirty cushiony cubes into an acoustic "cloud" over his desk that mimicked the sculpture.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/664517751e259d2d4dc804e5dde328d7d62778a0952b45ae94b197315ef448b2.jpg)  
Simple foam cubes can contribute to the playfulness and versatility of a work space.

The cubes were contagious. He bought three hundred more, originally intended for creating more "clouds," but they quickly disappeared throughout the office, becoming makeshift chairs, tables, and room dividers. Still another three hundred were instantly absorbed. Soon there were more than eight hundred—in an office with only forty people!

Cubes make great impromptu conference room partitions, amphitheater-like seating (just back them up to walls), and wonderful entertainment for visiting kids. And in a clear example of how creative space can feed your creativity engine, the cubes have even helped us innovate. To prototype an elevator for Otis, the world's largest elevator manufacturer, the Boston office stacked cubes to simulate the interior of an elevator so you could actually walk in. It was perfect for testing how fast it takes to load and unload, and it was great fun.

## INSPIRATION FROM ADVERSITY

Space problems can actually inspire innovation. The high-ceilinged, sunlit offices at 700 High Street, for example, are both a joy and a challenge. The light makes for lots of glare on computer screens. In the old days, most companies would have ignored the problem or imposed a heavy-handed solution. Instead, we held a contest to see who could come up with the best idea for diffusing the light. One designer took a trip to a home-improvement store and cleverly created white arches from PVC tubing, held in place with galvanized plumbing fixtures and covered with window screens. His winning entry earned him a great Italian desk lamp, but the "people's choice" solution was a simpler, far less expensive, arguably more attractive solution—oversize green market umbrellas, which sprouted like psychedelic mushrooms from the ceiling. Space can be both a resource and a place for fun.

Encourage people to solve their own space problems, and you'll likely spur innovation where you didn't know it existed. Years ago Jim Feuhrer, one of our machine shop veterans, found there wasn't room for his bike, so he hung it from some overhead hooks. Faced with higher ceilings in another building, another IDEOer, one with extensive sailing expertise, devised a clever system of hooks and pulleys to hoist up his wheels. No one complained. No one was reprimanded. Today, so many bikes hang from our high ceilings that you sometimes get the feeling you're in a bicycle factory.

Is there a space contest you might hold? Do the limits of your office space offer opportunities to stretch the innovative muscles of your team? Think of the interest people take in their apartments and homes. Give people ership in their workplace, and you'll be uprised at the positive results.

## PROTOTYPE YOUR SPACE

At its best, space inspires and amuses. Our Boston office's foam cubes becane both process and play. Employees felt elated after crowding onto and off the simulated foam elevator. The merging of fun and work ias invigorating.

But our building and testing of a foam elevator wasn't anywhere near our wildest experiment in space and process. Though we're trained to build prototypes we can touch and feel, even we recoize that some things are too big to prototype. Well, in Boston we decided to ignore that logic. We managed to build a nine-foot-high, forty-twofoot-long prototype of an entire Amtrak train car—and get it in the office.

Our Amtrak design team started working in the car. It was where the project lived, where the team members were trying to soak up the experience they were trying to create. You couldn't walk by it without finding three or four people pacing around, sketching, sitting down, asking questions. The space invited experimentation. In a couple of hours, we could install new spotlights or check ambient lighting. Situated next to our café, conference room, and machine shop, the train car became the place to hang out at IDEO. People held meetings in the car or tapped away on laptops. The train became a stage that made possible plays we'd never imagined. We split it into a mock-up of a "bistro" café car and a passenger car.

Amtrak brought a dozen conductors, service attendants, and managers to walk around—and at times upwards of twenty people were tromping around the structure, our own jungle gym. Since nearly everything was built in foam core, we could often model a suggestion that same afternoon. Amtrak could actually get buy-off on the design by walking kcy internal audicnces through the car. The train was prototyping, space, process, furniture, fun, and one heck of a conversation piece. Today you can experience thc real thing in Amtrak's Acela express scrvice from Boston to Washington via New York.

Space projects offer your workcrs great opportunities to expand their notion of office, work, and play. Think hard. Is there a way to integrate prototypes or the prototyping proccss into your company's work environment?

## CREATE A TEAM ICON

The Amtrak train car served for several months as a kind of icon of innovation for the Boston office. But it's not always possible to turn part of a project into inspirational space. The best teams and studios make a practice of seeking out icons that inspire the workplace. The more adventure and camaraderie you can attach to the icon, the better.

One of the greatest IDEO icons began as a treasure hunt. A young IDEO group called thc Spunks came up with the whinsical teambuilding idea of searching out a great centerpiece symbol for their space, akin to the pirate's flag Steve Jobs flew over Bandley One to mark the Macintosh development team's territory. The Spunks rented a bus and drove down en masse to Thompson's Aviation Library in El Monte, California, a graveyard for airplanes in the sands of the desert near Los Angeles. Most of Thompson's business was renting crashed airplanes for Hollywood films, and the IDEO contingent had a wild timc stumbling around the great aviation relics. They were looking for something a little less than twenty fect long and found just what they'd dreamed of: a wing from a vintage DC-3, the same type of aircraft that flew Ronald Colman to Shangri-La in the 1937 movie Lost Horizon.

Back at IDEO, after a lot of weekends spent buffing and polishing, the win made for a pretty wild roo divider, but its most interesing use came later. When Peter Skillman, the ringleader of the treasure hunt, joined Dennis Boyle's studio at 660 High Street, he knew the wing needed to fly over a central spot. He chose the popular conference table adjoining the studio's tiny kitchen, and had our model shop build a full-scale foam-core and wood replica. They had to prototype the wing, you see, hang a lighter replica from the ceiling by cables to test the forces the real wing would likely exert. Space became an exercise in work and play, and the result—a dramatic soaring wing—is now a wonderfully expansive metaphor to inspire the studio.

The team icon doesn't have to be big. It can be a little thing. Or more precisely, lots of little things. OXO International, famous for its oversized Good Grips kitchen gadgets, understandably has an interest in how people of all sizes and shapes hold its products. One day in New York, Alex Lee, the president, noticed several discarded gloves on the sidewvalk. Intrigued by these small pieces of "found art," he asked his staff to start collecting gloves. Today the company has an amazing, eclectic collection. It's not just a good conversation starter for visitors. The gloves represent the range of hands OXO's products must fit, and provide a living, breathing symbol of the firm's mission.

Are you launching a new team? Maybe there's an artifact that might inspire it. Or something you might actually cobble together or adapt for your office. Turn creating your space into a team-building exercise, and you'll be surprised at how much fun and pride people take in their workplace.

## WATCH YOUR BODY LANGUAGE

As your office space starts to take shape, it's a good time to take notice of your "body language." Franklin Becker and Fritz Stecle in their book

Workplace by Design talk about the physical work environment represented by a company's office space as a kind of body language for the organization. As we all know from personal experience, however, verbal and body language don't always agree. What is the body language of your organization saying to employees, clients, and visitors? Does your company send signals of hierarchy, or is it virtually egalitarian? Is it a place of radiating possibilities or faded glory? Ericsson, for instance, believes there is a connection between its cool new London offices and the company's "progressive" attitude.

Is your company sending the right signals, or is its office space contradicting its corporate policies? Becker and Steele claim that an R&D center laid out in rigid ways and using delicate, easily damaged surfaces is like a lover whispering "I love you" in a bored voice while doing a crossword puzzle. Watch your company's body language. It's speaking to you even if you haven't been listening.

As a management consultant, I've visited my share of offices that send out confused signals. Not too long ago the window office, walnut desk, and leather chair were nearly as important as the salary in reflecting executive status. In this carrot-and-stick model, only the champions earned leather and wood. But I know firsthand that lots of folks rebel against that work model. I still remember the day I interviewed at the consulting division of one of the big accounting firms. Great people. Reputable firm. Reasonable salary. It all looked pretty good until I asked to see what my office space would be like as a new associate.

First, my simple question about space flushed out the truth about lifestyle. Suddenly, I heard that you travel so much you don't really need a desk, which was a different story than I had heard earlier. Second, I got a clear signal about how they valued new associates. Not only did I not get a desk, I didn't get a filing cabinet or even a drawer. Where would I store my fles and things? I'd share a gray sheet-metal utility shelf with another associate, my portion marked only by my name scribbled on a piece of masking tape. Maybe it wasn't quite rational that I ture down the joboffr party becauseo a grayy shelf, but the space had communicated a lot about the firm's culture, and I wonder how much talent was fended off over the ycars because the "body language" of the office said, "New associates don't matter."

## SIMPLE TEAM SPACE

When you'e creatingor updating space, remeber n asy but ten elusvegoal Kee isiple. I's a big deal tohange the physical workplace, and companies can get as excited and carried away with new approaches to office space as they d with thelatest management trends. That's why it helps to have a snse of the hisory o offcespace as well a the en e u oni  e o place.

This is one part of the innovation puzzle where you can actually see with your own eyes what works and what doesn't. We've come a long way. Remember the early-twentieth-century photos of endless rows of typists or clerks immortalized in movies like Desk Set and The Apartment? There was a time when control and supervision were the message, the factory ivas the model. Fresh ideas and teamwork weren't part of the program.

At the close of the 1950s, Union Carbide's new headquarters in New York City epitomized the rise of space as status. In the following years, the building became as hierarchical as a feudal fiefdom. Where you worked was what you were. Climb the corporate ladder and you'd be rewarded with more space, wood furniture, broader views, and closer proximity to senior management. Grades and colors of carpet and wallpaper separated the haves and have-nots. The smallest detail could betray you. If your ashtray wasn't made of the right stuff, maybe you weren't either. It's easy to say that those were the old days, but some new ideas in office space can be as trendy-and impractical—as fashion. Several years ago, for example, some companies rushed to join a new fad—the totally open office.

Declaring war on private offices, Chiat/Day, the talented advertising agency, brightened spaces with flashy tables and chairs and mobile workstations dubbed "hives." The New York Times praised it as "a remarkable work of art," but workers felt lost. Nobody had a home or even a neighborhood. Each day, workers were just expected to grab whatever space was vacant. People pined for privacy and often stayed home if they needed to get something done. Confided one worker to the Times, "Creative people need quiet places to think, and that was something they never planned for."

Not surprisingly, in the mid-1990s when the Omnicon Group bought out Jay Chiat, the company chucked the totally open office. New management added some private spaces for writers and art directors, and created more project rooms and a room full of tables and computers for teams to spread out ad campaigns. In other words, the firm listened to its own workers.

It's an invaluable lesson in the critical role space plays in a company's work process and identity. Creating a great work environment may be nearly as important as hiring the right people. Indeed, in my experience they're often inseparable. When people interview at IDEO, you can often see their surprise and delight that our office doesn't look like a workplace. There are as many ways to achieve this as there are to lay out a house. But in the numerous companies I've visited around the world, I've found that the best offices all seem to celebrate teamwork. And sometimes that's about fairness and simplicity more than anything else.

The top high-tech company founders nearly alvays grasp this essential principle. Hewlett-Packard, arguably the first Silicon Valley enterprise launched in a garage, had an egalitarian approach to space for decades-almost nobody had an office. Jeffrey Bezos, founder and CEO of Amazon.com, intuitively understood the importance of frugal and fair office space. Bezos also started in a garage, and by the summr of 1997 when Amazon went public and had a few hundred employees, the company still held true to its spartan ways. Rent was cheap. Across the street stood a pawnshop, a wig store, and the Green Tortoise Hostel. Bezos believed in the power of example, of managing and markcting by metaphor. He bought the best computcrs and hired the best people and scrimped on ncarly cverything else. The only sign announcing Amazon.com's headquarters was a sheet of paper stuck in a plant in the lobby.

Bezos's cramped shoe-box office was no bigger than his assistant's, and his desk, like most in the start-up, was an unfinished door with fourby-fours for legs. That desk was more than just a way to save a few hundred dollars-it was a symbol for workers, Wall Street, and investors, worth millions in marketing and good karma. Amazon wasn't merely an upstart challenging traditional retailers. It was short-circuiting the corporate status quo, and for its quirky mix of grunge workers and liberal literati it mattered that there was no ivalnut or mahogany for the executives—that everybody's PC sat on the same cheap door.

Visit the best start-ups, and you'll see a self-awareness that extends to space. Back in 1998, when Yahoo's market cap was already in the billions, more than a few Yahoo walls carried pictures and information about employee-inspired community efforts for youth and the environment. Even then, with dozens of multimillionaires, Yahoo walked and talked like a company that hadn't lost sight of its roots. One of my favorite touches was its executive offices. On the ground floor stood Amdahl, a dinosaur from the mainframe computer era. You got the sense that Yahoo was growing out of the ashes of the past, that the executives liked the humbling sense of walking into someone else's lobby before they reached their own.

Small or fast-growing companies present unique challenges. Startups, for instance, have revolutionized American business, but overlooked in the stock options and venture capital is that if you survive the first round of financing, you've probably outgrown your first or second building. Companies dripping in financing often just lease a cavernous building, leaving half or more of it empty, figuring that in the proverbial nine months they'll be bursting at the seams. But I've secn too many companies—even IDEO—lose momentum when therc was too much extra elbowroom. The real sccret is to crowd it with peoplc, but it helps a little to crowd it with furniture. When Handspring first moved to its building in Sunnyvale, it had a literal and figurative emptiness, but when I visited a few days later, the previously cavernous space had been filled with furniture, transforming it into more of a buzzing start-up.

I've seen the same "density-yields-buzz" phenomenon in our offices around the country. When the body count per square foot was high, the offices seemed to pulse like hot nightclubs on Friday night. It's a delicate issue to manage. No company wants to cram people into the office like sardines. But too much space, we've found, is like too much time for a deadline—we're all happy to take it, but it doesn't necessarily help get the job done.

## HIERARCHY IS THE ENEMY OF COOL SPACE

As you create or rework space, beware of a natural tendency to reward superiors with superior space. Overly hierarchical corporate rules about space can drive a stake between you and your plans to innovate. Once, I spent all morning consulting with a well-respected Fortune 1000 company that said it wanted to shed its old bureaucracy, inspire hot groups, and learn to collaborate. Then they took me upstairs to the sumptuous—and exclusionary—executive dining room for a steak, and didn't see the irony.

If you've worked for a large company, you've probably witnessed firsthand how company policies toward space and hierarchy can drive people to absurd, petty lengths. Companies that rigidly assign office space based on rank can be the most abusive. IDEO's David Haygood, director of business development, for example, told me of a former employer who sentenced workers to dismal cubicles if they were "grade 19" or lower, while those at grade 20 and above got a hard-walled office with a door. Constant reorganizations created greater tensions. Once word leaked of an impending reorganization, grade 20s flew like vultures to the part of the building where they'd be moving. Eyes on the ceiling, they dashed in and out of offices, counting. What in the world were they doing? By counting the ceiling tiles, workers could quickly ascertain the precise square footage of each office and knew for certain wic ofoetbsont. It's  id' e.

Wise to this depressing company ritual, Haygood had the next company he worked forp out the false ceiling and spray-paint the vhead mechanicals black. Next, since Haygood was in charge of office allocation, he assigned himself the worst office in the building—right next to the restroom.

The usual bickering ensued. Within minutes a few malcontents came to Haygood's dismal office, bitching and moaning. Haygood smiled at them and told them that anyone who didn't like his or her digs could have his. That shut up the complainers fast.

Ridiculous, right? Isolated case? Maybe. But I had an experience early in my career that says Haygood's story was not unique. With my newly minted MBA, I was ready to conquer the world the week I started with a global management consulting firm. What was my first assignment? Marketing strategy for a major airline? Operations analysis? No, actually, I spent the better part of a day making very precise measurements of each partner's office, including deductions for any support pillars or obstructions that decreased the number of available square inches. And why? One of the partners felt he had been slighted in the recent office move, and he needed my data to help argue his case and right this injustice.

Hopefully, such battles are on the way out. Over the last few years, a number of forward-thinking corporate executives are leading the charge against such status symbols. A senior executive with AT&T in Ohio chucked his office door and wood-paneled walls entirely and installed clear glass; not surprisingly, his management team quickly followed the lead. Throughout the nation, many corporate executives have abandoned their plush private offices and ventured into openplan leadership communities. And even more have broken the umbilical cord between window views and rank.

If you'rc not the CEO, sometimes it can be tough to change the status quo. One midwestcrn multinational rewards executives by issuing them a leather chair whcn thcy enter the inner sanctum. One day, a new executive declarcd that he didn't want leather and ordercd an ordinary chair. That tiny act of indepcndence was viewed as a dangerous rebellion. Some executives worried that if "one of their own" rejected the leather privilege, soon other privileges would be snatched awvay from the executives. And so, incredibly, the executives insisted that the maverick sit in a lcather chair.

## GIVE YOUR WORKERS A VIEW

You and your company don't have to be a part of the leather chair set. You can literally give your wvorkers a view. All of them. In the early 1990s, Alcoa realized that the old equation of space equals seniority thickened its corporate lifeblood. A revolution was started at the top, with Chairman Paul O'Neill ordering his executives into open-plan offices. The experiment worked—O'Neill liked how it encouraged his staff to commune in the kitchen and common areas—and so he charged ahead with his plan to hire an architecture firm to design a new headquarters near Pittsburgh's Three Rivers Stadium.

Today Alcoa's remnants of the 1950s—status-separating private offices and executive parking spaces—are gone. Hallvays were chucked because they divide people, and escalators have replaced elevators to encourage motion and interaction. Tables and chairs are made partly of aluminum, and murals and collages give each kitchen a unique look. Best of all, suddenly everyonc has a room with a view. No workstation is more than forty-fivc feet from the nearly twelve-foot-high windows looking out on the Allegheny River and the Pittsburgh skyline. Centrally located coffec areas and cafeterias on each floor are magnets for collaboration. And everyone, including Chairman O'Neill, gets the same nine-by-nine-foot open office.

The result wasn't merely that Alcoa won a BusinessWeek/Architectural Record Award, sponsored by the American Institute of Architects. O'Neill told thc New York Times that the new offices have helped them to "beat the liver out of the competition."

But you don't have to be a Fortunc 500 company to realize that space matters. We have faced similar issues in trying to make the best use of our San Francisco office. The site has natural advantages and possible drawbacks. Located near the foot of the Bay Bridge in an old maritime pier, the office was designed to include a spacious deck and a glorious sixty-foot-long view of San Francisco Bay and the bridge overhead. But one-third of that view was lost because a wall had been built without windows to save a few thousand dollars.

Eventually. we knocked out the windowless wall, opening up that space so that three roll-up Plexiglas garage doors could span the front. Moving the manager's office away from the new window allowed the sunny waterfront area known as the Lookout to be used entirely for shared purposes. Now lunch tables line the front, with a kitchen at one end and a periodicals library at the other; Plexiglas cabinets display dozens of current magazines (with back issues tucked inside). Workers can eat lunch and peruse a magazine on the couch or one of the rollable egg-shaped chairs. Every Monday a different member of the office hosts a lunch in the space. Large white foam-core panels break the space up from the rest of the office, so that the Lookout can be used to prototype projects or talk about company issues.

The space most companies might have doled out to executives is now an ideal place to greet or entertain clients, eat lunch, collaborate or read or research something from the library. Most corporate libraries are an afterthought, a lost opportunity when you consider the wealth of inspiration and information they might provide. The IDEO San Francisco office has made its library an integral part of the work space. The periodicals library is just part of a massive collection that runs the entire length of the building and includes extensive reference materials and books. Laser printers positioned within the space encourage workers to regularly visit and use the library.

I think our San Francisco office is a good example of how you can subtly alter space to support innovation, make resources more readily available, and open views up for everyone. Try looking around your company. Might moving the executive offices make a change? Could making the library a part of the workplace help people find what they need faster? Should that great view go to the CEO or the company lunchroom?

## TELL STORIES

Show me a company going places and I'll show you a story worth telling.

If your company has been doing some neat stuff or has got big plans for the future, it's probably got a place for show-and-tell. Hewlett-Packard, for example, is proud of its museum. The company employs an official, full-time corporate librarian who lets visitors see and sometimes touch the company's wide range of products. The librarian also publishes the oral histories of retired company executives.

Now, I'm glad they're preserving the legacy of this great company, but it's a bit like resting on your laurels. Wouldn't it be better if instead of a museum you displayed current projects that teams could be proud of and look to on a daily basis for inspiration? What about the latest prototypes, materials, and mechanisms? Wouldn't that be a greater influence on future innovation than canonizing the past? How about aspiring to a corporate "Exploratorium" instead of a museum?

We believe in letting the world know we've done some pretty cool stuff—without stifng that ceativity in  useum. I yur cpy has done something impressive that tells a story, try to put it front and center in a place that makes your workers proud—and lets clients and visitors see what you do.

The lobby at 660 High Street is our best storytelling entrance. There's the sleek soapbox car that won the intensely competitive local race called the Sand Hill Challenge. On each side of the broad lobby, shelves overflow with prototypes and final products—everything from the Heartstream defibrillator to the Palm V, Crest Neat Squeeze, Pepsi Tivist 'n Go cup, and the SoftBook electronic book. Nothing is placed behind glass—it's all there for us to handle and show visitors the nuances of innovation and design.

Each of our offices has some version of a storytelling entrance. At some, the story winds through the building and into the individual "neighborhoods." Storytelling is best when it comes from the heart. We encourage studios to display their best work, but we don't bog down the process with corporate rules. There's no official "storytelling" policy. Bureaucracy would just kill the inspiration. The best storytelling happens when the team itself is proud and wants the world to know what it's accomplished.

Our machine shop used to be one of our best secrets. Unlike many firms, we've employed and cultivated top model makers and machinists in-house. It's a competitive advantage that our clients have come to rely on. But for years, the shop never displayed the imaginative solutions they'd helped dream up for what often seemed intractable problems. One day the head of IDEO's shop asked why they weren't on the visitor tour. They, too, wanted to strut their stuff, as well they should. Today the shop has a compelling display that features everything from injection-molding equipment to stereolithography parts. Morale has never been higher.

Storytelling is usually inspired by one or two individuals. Just as too many cooks can spoil the broth, too much technology can kill a good story. I believe in a healthy skepticism toward capturing a company's achievements in formal knowledge bases or databases. There's a cer-

At many companies Dennis Boyle's cluttered corner might be considered a liability. Truth is, Dennis used to wonder if there was something wrong with him. He'd been collecting mechanical parts, odd materials, and other quirky artifacts since he was a boy.

As the head of one of our most celebrated studios, Dennis became known as the keeper of the files, the stuff, the slides, the boxes. Circuits, fasteners, exotic plastics, unusual glass, oddly molded partsyou name it, Dennis had it. As a Stanford lecturer, Dennis often lugged around a cardboard box to show his students. He called it the "magic box" and told his students that engineers should make a practice of collecting interesting things that might one day prove useful. In essence, Dennis was celebrating a physical version of what writers and lots of people do with a diary, writing down interesting events and insights. Often, to spark a creative brainstorm, Dennis would bring a few choice items from his magic box.

When Dennis became a studio head, he searched for similar ideas to inspire his team. What about the magic box? he thought. He knew that plenty of other engineers and designers had impressive collections of gadgets and materials, but unlike his, theirs often weren't accessible. He decided to invent a model to make the gadget collection a part of our process. No corporate dictum was issued, no meetings called to poll the studio heads.
