Dennis simply grabbed a Stanford intern, pointed to his boxes, and asked him to come up with some sort of inexpensive, interactive display.

There was some gentle resistance. My brother David kidded Dennis about his osession when he kept bringng up his project at our firm's

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/91fc998fc2e0fe22ef926164cfd1babd6c1b9b865059642298aebc1f0d6b6d88.jpg)  
The "Tech Box" is a centrally located lending Library of innovation elements.

roundtable meetings. When Dennis encouragcd other celebratcd IDEO collectors to pitch in their masterpieces, he was met with skepticism. Some figured they'd never sec their prized possessions again.

But ultimately the "Tech Box," as it became known, was a runaway success. With a little proselytizing, ncarly everyone started gencrously offering up their valuables. A five-drawer flat-file cabinet, dcsigned for blueprints and interlaced with bento-box-style partitions, displayed the eclectic collection. Each item was neatly taggcd. Set smack in thc middle of our 660 studio office, next to the printers and copiers and DC-3 wing meeting space, the Tech Box soon became our process centcr, our living version of the lobby shrine and museum.

This was the Montessori approach, and just as kids learn to count by moving colored beads, engineers and designers came to this physical and mental wellspring to touch and feel and see. The Tcch Box became our corporate spark plug. Stumped on a project? Rifle through some drawers at the Tech Box. Need inspiration for a project or thorny design issue? Grab something from the box to toss on the table and spark new ideas. The box holds tiny switches and minuscule batteries and plenty of exotic materials—everything from Aerogel, an amazingly light translucent substance that feels like a solid form of smoke, to Mylar, the golden satellite insulator, to Kevlar, the material that stops bullets cold.

Today clients and visitors flock to the Tech Box to see—and more important, hold—the miracles within each drawer. Stir a cup of boiling water with a thin, long copper heat pipe, and the end of thc tubc instantly burns your finger. Drop the Nitinol "memory mctal" wire in the cup, and it reassumes its natural coiled shape. Twist a packct containing supersaturated liquid, and it quickly turns to solid, giving off so much heat that it nearly burns your hands. Rubber balls that don't bounce, opaque materials that become translucent with the flip of a switch—the only common denominator in the Tcch Box is surprise. "This is more about serendipity," says Dennis. "Making a lateral connection between slightly odd things that might give us a different perspective."

The Tech Box is our constantly evolving eclectic LegoLand. Everyone aspires to be part of this living show-and-tell. One day I contributed a Pack-A-Cino-a cappuccino with its own heat source. It's a novelty, but Dennis feels it might someday work in redesigning some pice of sports or emergency medical equipment that needs to be highly portable.

The Tech Box straddles process and architecture. It's both a mindset and a physical statement. It's a heck of a lot more valuable to our work and culture than the typical corporate art purchase—some twisted hunk of steel welded by an abstract sculptor. The Tech Box is our own private beach of imagination, a place to let the sand run through our toes and catch a few waves.

Today the Tech Box has been adopted at all our IDEO offices worldwide. Curators continuously seek out new technologies to collect, evaluate, and distribute around the firm. We've now cataloged the twohundred-plus items in an Intranet database that offers some background on each technology, as well as any practical uses we've figured out for it on a project. This Website is accessible from everybody's desk, or from an iMac on top of the Tech Box itself (next to the humanpowered windup radio and the martini glass that never spills).

Dennis's pet project has blossomed into a strategic IDEO advantage. Partners like Steelcase subscribe to the Tech Box (to receive actual copies of the items and updates), and we're considering offering it to select long-term clients. More important, the Tech Box has become a launching point for many projects and helped to improve our creative process. So many workers submitted technologies that Chris Kurjan, one of the Tech Box curators, had a tough time navigating the boxes of rejects engulfing her work space. IDEOers would finish a project with cool samples and materials and be loath to throw them away.

That wealth of great stuff inspired her to create a manufacturing processes display. IDEOers could come to the display to learn how to make things out of plastic and metal—say, the case parts for a remote control, the bezel for a medical instrument pancl, or stunning lithogaphicfinishes. It was't long before Tech Box II, the Manuacturing

Process Story, was playing in IDEO offices across from the original Tech Box.

Dennis Boyle's once underappreciated cardboard boxcs have taken on a life of their own. When space celebrates process and teamwork, you know you've got something you can grow with.

For many companies, space is among the least considered, most overlooked tools in the innovation toolbox. The hundreds of companies I have seen firsthand spend a lot of time on structure and strategy and systems and staff. When it comes to space, however, they think of it as a mere utility. Space is treated as a threshold variable, made just good enough, except for an expensive flourish in the lobby or the main conference room. At IDEO we believe space matters, and I hope you wil too. We all know the legend that innovation starts in a garage, but sooner or later we all grow up and need a place to work. No matter how electronic your business may be, there's a physical element too. Being innovative and successful is more than hiring the right people and buying the best technology. You'e got tocreate aculture whee spacemattrs.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/b9693a9b95a32a6ce0a32c2625260d8c62fcd1259a9b3c644af61564dddec637.jpg)

istory teaches that innovation does not come about by central planning. If it did, Silicon Valley would be nearer to Moscow than to San Francisco. Working on thousands of projects has taught us a simple but critical element that every team or company should come to expect: the unexpected.

Chance offers insights you didn't anticipate. It's a well-accepted truth that inventions and discoveries often result from random accidents or experiments that went awry.

How can you capitalize on that phenomenon? Well, you can start by expecting the unexpected, being open to surprises from sources within and outside your organization.

Try approaching projects with humility and the knowledge that answers may come from places you least suspect. We call this "looking cross-eyed" and "cross-pollination." It's quite liberating and powerful. If you expect to find answers from unusual places, it's far more likely to happen. In nature, we know that cross-pollination leads to superior strains of plants. It's the same with products and services. Launch a project with the assumption that cross-pollination may help you to innovate, and you're more likely to be ready to take the leaps of creativity necessary for innovation.

Now that we're midstream in the innovation process, I think it's appropriate to issue a disclaimer. We don't know it all. Neither does your boss or even the CEO. Accidents happen. Here follow some of my experiences and insights about how it is that serendipity plays a critical role in innovation—and what you can do about this puzzling variable.

Growing up near Akron, I had "Ohio history" classes that included the story of Procter & Gamble's fabulously successful Ivory soap. It began with a blunder: an ordinary worker who went to lunch and accidentally left a mixing machine running with a batch of soap inside. When he came back, the mix had been whipped to a froth, with soap so light that it floated. By chance, the new floating Ivory proved to be both convenient and popular, marketed by P&G as "99 and 44 one-hundredths percent pure." A factory worker stumbled onto a new way of mixing soap, but it was P&G's marketing group that seized the opportunity to create one of the most successful packaged goods of the twentieth century.

Chance has played a role in all kinds of breakthroughs, from science to technology and business. Velcro started when a Swiss mountaineer returned from a hike covered with prickly cockleburs. A little observation under the microscope yielded the reason—nature's tiny hooks, perfect for grabbing a ride in the feathers or fur of passing creatures. Voilà! Soon the French were making Velcro's artificial hooks and loops that stick and easily separate. People had been plagued with prickly burrs from the beginning of recorded history. It took an intellectually curious person with access to the right tools to turn the idea into a breakthrough material. Stop and think for a moment. Are you proactively hiring the intellectually curious? If so, what tools do they need to help them along?

Saccharin was discovered in 1879 when a research fellow at Johns Hopkins University found his bread extra sweet one night and figured that something from the lab must have followed him home. Incredibly, he set about to tasting nearly everything in his lab—and lived to find o-benzoic sulfimide—saccharin by any other name. Chance not only favors the trained mind, but in this case, it favored a man brave enough

Successful companies have the agility to turn small failurcs or anomalies like these into big successes. Unfortunately, the reverse can also occr. Evenater decade achingour ient pouct nd all we occasionally bet on the wrong horsc. Sometimes it seems that guessing which product willfly is a little like picking the classmate most likely to succeed. Tivo that really fooled me were Dynabook and Momenta.

I wasn't the only one who thought the Dynabook laptop computer looked like a winner. It was one of our sleekest portable designs, a black cast-magnesium computer that looked ahead of its time. Dynabook Technologies even had backing from blue-chip venture capitalists at Kleiner Perkins who had a stellar track record of sensing "the right stuff." But there was one thing Dynabook hadn't planned for—Intel coming up with a new chip. Just when Intel 386 chips were becoming available, Dynabook introduced its 286 machine. All the good looks and well-integrated features in the world couldn't carry an old chip around the block.

In the same way, Momenta was one of the handful of IDEO clients I was ready to invest in from the very beginning. Headed by the charismatic Kamran Elahian, the company seemed to have both history and money on its side. Kamran had a great track record and so much sway with venture capitalists that he was turning away money. And the company's marketing campaign was smart. It focused on what Momenta's pen-based computer would do for real people-along the lines of our "verbs not nouns" approach—and had a captivating "Thousand Days to Greatness" program to motivate the internal development team. Momenta spent nearly \$3 million in marketing on the day of the product's unveiling alone to make sure that the world noticed its product launch.

We'd already worked on just about every other hope-springs-eternal pen-based gizmo in the Valley—the GRiDPad, the PalmPad, the Go PenPoint machine and its spin-off, the EO. But Momenta was a powerful computer and—to me—secmed to have an aura of success about it from the very beginning. In retrospect, maybe it was trying to do too much; its large, high-resolution touch screen was expensive; its handwriting recognition wasn't quite there yet. This much I know for certain: Momenta went on to become one of the most glorious flameouts in Silicon Valley history.

## GOOD TIMING DOESN'T HURT

Over the years I've noticed some patterns. Products that become hits seem to enjoy a balance of features, price, and that often elusive element of timing. And lightning can strike for what on the surface seems a humble product. We've designed plenty of computer monitors over the years, for instance, but our most successful monitor is one of our most understated. The "Simply Samsung" SyncMaster monitors have sold in the millions of units, more than any other monitors we've designed. It may not always make headlines, but good design at an affordable price sells.

Some products seem to be blessed with perfect timing and sizing. The Polaroid I-Zone camera was one of our more ambitious projects (we did extensive observations with kids to find out what they found "cool"). Still, I wasn't sure that the postage-stamp-sized photo stickers that had been so successful in Japan would catch on in the United States. Yet in the last few months of 1999, it became the best-selling camera in America. Why? Though there's long been strong demand for Polaroid cameras, the market was partly constrained by the fact that you needed a fairly bulky camera to develop a reasonable-sized print. But since the I-Zone prints tiny pictures, the camera itself can be kidsized. And it turned out that the smaller pictures provided a tremendous opportunity—they're ideal for sticking inside a school locker or on a notebook. In a nutshell, the I-Zone translated the instant gratifcation of the adult-sized original to kids, for whom instant gratification is a way of life.

## NIMBLE RESPONSE

Jus  e c' ei  pu p - was divine what featureor use wll catch the public's maginatin. For that reason, companies need to be in touch with what "quirky" uses consumers have thought up for their products, and be ready to restructure their marketing accordingly. Kleenex, for example, was intenhan cold cream remover. Only when the public started wiping their noses with the paper hankies did the Kimberly-Clark Corporation see the potential and begin to totally reposition its product as a disposable handkerchief.

With the benefit of 20/20 hindsight, it's clear to us what Kleenex is all about. But just how a new product might be used in the marketplace is often nearly impossible to divine. DuPoni originally developed Kevlar to replace the steel wire in radial tires. But it was the aircraft industry that first responded to the exotic fber, recognizing that its lightweight strength could save millions in reduced fuel costs. Police forces discovered that Kevlar vests weighing less than three pounds could stop a .357 Magnum bullet, and the U.S. Army found it a safe and lighter replacement for steel helmets. Canoes, kayaks, and powerboats rode the Kevlar wvave, then came ropes and cables, and soon Kevlar was everywhere, replacing everything from nylon to steel in car and machinery hoses and belts, protecting workers against fire, and spreading to even more exotic uses like lightweight parachutes and aircraft ejection seats.

Eventually, Kevlar tires did take hold, but this amazingly strong and resilient material didn't catch on until people started experimenting with it.

Zelco, the innovative company that brought us the "Itty Bitty Book Light," made a water-resistant clock with a rope on it that you hang in the shower. The product chugged along with unremarkable sales until one day demand shot through the roof. The company had no idea what hit them until someone clued them in that a popular rap band had appeared on MTV wearing Zelco's wall clocks around their necks. The spike in demand may have taken them by surprise, but nimble entrepreneur Noel Zeller was on the phone the same day, ramping up production and revamping his marketing for the product.

Likewise, the venerable Hush Puppies shoe, introduced in 1958, was nearly discontinued for lack of sales in the early nineties. Suddenly, spurred by grassroots consumer interest, Hush Puppies became the darling of the fashion industry, in demand at New York City resale shops, worn at hip-hop dance clubs, and even shown on designer runways. Wolverine Worldwide, who makes the shoes, helped the momentum along with an aggressive new branding program. In 1996 Hush Puppies won the prize for best accessory from the Council of Fashion Designers. Wolverine has since reported record earnings and is now the largest U.S. manufacturer of footwear. A sudden—and unexpected—turnaround for a product on the verge of being discontinued. Being alert to fashion trends helped the company take advantage of its windfall.

## LOOKING CROSS-EYED

Over the years IDEO has learned that ideas for new products and services often come from left field. We've been aided by the fact that we work in diverse fields—high tech, medical, office furniture, e-commerce, transportation, and architecture, to name a few. It's a fact of life. If you see and take part in more innovation in disparate industries, your innovation vocabulary is bound to increase along with the oruyr coss-pollinatiBut ny copay an takevage of this factor. The first step is to learn how to look beyond your own backyard. At IDEO we often begin with questions. Could something in our latest Internet appliances be transferable to one of the medical projects? Might we be able to work some of our new computer interface ideas into a consumer product?

I've been in dozens of exciting brainstorms, and what always amazes me is how many good ideas drift over the fence. Our Nightline shopping cart casters sprang from an office furniture prototype, and the first wheels for the Sand Hill Challenge soapbox race came from high-tech wheelchairs. When IDEOers talk about thinking outside of the box, we're not talking about just going beyond what's typically done in a particular field. We often begin by wearing the glasses of totally different disciplines.

Take beach chairs. Several years back, somebody came to us with the sort of strong observation that often inspires good products.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/bc6cbda32cdbdcedecc244381839198aed485034b7e8897b781053fd0c4d1ddf.jpg)  
The idea for this Sun Tracker Beach Chair came from a tilt-and-swivel computer monitor.

Wouldn't it be nice to have a beach chair where you didn't have to get up and turn the chair every hour or so to follow the sun? So where'd the solution come from? During a brainstorming session, someone brought in a computer monitor with a ball-and-socket mechanism used for tilting and swiveling the display. We adapted that computer concept (itself an adaptation) for the beach. The result was a "sun tracker" beach chair with a rounded base that "scrunches" down into the sand, which acts as the pivot.

Another chair we worked on—this one for the office instead of the beach—had a tilt mechanism underneath with a troublesome metal coil spring. We solved the problem by creating a cylindrical adjustment handle—made by bonding rubber to metal—which could double as the compression spring itself. The safer and more comfortable adjustment mechanism came about partly because two IDEO engineers had experience in the automotive industry, where bonding rubber to metal is commonplace.

Watch people struggle with existing products in new environments, and you'll likely sense an opportunity for cross-pollination. One way to open your mind to this possibility is to ponder how products made for only one use are adapted by clever people or companies for different uses.

There's the milk crate, that delightfully versatile container used for everything from filing to furniture to clothes hampers. Milk companies hated how popular those crates were, and as far as I know, no dairy ever turned the vice into a virtue by selling them on the open market. Every start-up worth its stock options knows that doors make for good cheap desks. Duct tape, originally intended for sealing galvanized heating ducts, has become one of the most versatile items in a handyman's toolbox. The golf cart, of course, is used by Hollywood studios and corporations around the world for quiet, on-site transportation. Then there's the case of Breathe Right nasal strips, a device made to help people stop snoring. San Francisco 49ers receiver Jerry Rice started wearing this Band-Aid-like contraption on his nose to improve his air intake, and sales suddenly soared—for athletes as well as snorers. And of course, there's the personal computer. Highly complex problems

used to be tackled by supercomputers. Then some very smart computer scientists realized you could take ordinary personal computers and channel their computing might through parallel processing. Today you can achieve much the same level of computing power by activating distant machines on the Internet.

Not too many years ago the only mass-produced sports goggles were those made for downhill skiing. Motocross riders, who know something about getting through a rough course, patched up the goggles as best as they could. Since mud would quickly cake up on the lenses, they jury-rigged traditional ski goggles to hold half a dozen clear lenses.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/2d82393f085ef97111a3e9d1a6d986dfb036897087dc1f09583d5e19fc9cb6a8.jpg)  
Smith's clear "Roll-Offs," helped adapt ski goggle designs to the muddy world of motocross.

During a racc thcy'd pcel off thc muddy lenses onc by one to temporarily regain an unobstructed view. Ski goggle leader Smith Sports Optics, knew there had to be a better model. They found inspiration in rolls of 35mm film, and created a clear Mylar roll. Pull a cord on the "Roll-off" cartridge, and you'd get anothcr fresh strip of Mylar, and a clear stripe across your goggles. Roll-Offs held enough clear film to outlast the dirtiest ride.

## EXERCISING YOUR CROSS-POLLINATION MUSCLES

The Tech Box, of course, is our conscious exercise in cross-pollination. Bringing quirky Tech Box items to brainstormers often sparks people to think beyond the norm. In the previous chapter I mentioned a thin copper heat pipe that burns your fingers if you dip one end into boiling water. It has its roots in the aerospace industry, ihere it's used for cooling critical parts. IDEO's brainstorm-fueled idea was to adapt it to cool off those hot microprocessor chips in mobile computers. It's a particularly elegant engineering solution. We've all cxperienced the drawbacks of fans, the most common method of dissipating heat in desktop computers—they're bulky, they suck up power, and they're noisy. But these tiny copper tubes silently carry away the heat.

When Apple came to Dennis Boyle looking for a nonmechanical method to ejcct PC cards from its PowerBook, he wcnt to our Tech Box, a natural starting place, where he found Nitinol, that magical material that returns to its original shape when heated. He worked up a couple of quick prototypcs, and sure enough, figured out that the "shape memory" alloy could bc uscd as a kind of mctal "muscle." A small amount of battery powcr could heat a tiny Nitinol wire, make it contract, pull open a latch, relcasc a spring—and shoot out the card. Light, small, and highly reliable, Nitinol has lots of benefits. I expect it will find its way into othcr consumer products.

Cross-pollination can be surprisingly low-tech as well. Sorbothane, that wonderfully springy elastic material found in better running shoes, is another Tech Box resident. Where did we use it? First, we packed it in the Plus Passport removable disk drive, a device engineered to withstand shock. Later we used tiny amounts of the material to protect delicate components inside portable computers and other mobile devices.

H ow important can a little Sorbothane be? Wel we recently uscd it inside the set-top box for TiVo's personal TV service, a system that digitizes and storcs TV broadcasts so you can pause or evcn rewind the show you're watching. In order to accomplish this magic, the TiVo machine houses two large disk drives that are spinning all the time. Critics found the competitor's product irritatingly noisy, but not TiVo. The difference in our design? We packed the TiVo's drive with a few cents' worth of Sorbothane to dampen vibration and cut the noise.

## SEVEN PLANTING TIPS

What cxactly is cross-pollination? Is it something that strikes you out of the blue? Can you plan for it? The truth is there's a yin and yang to cross-pollination. There's the active, aggressive side: learning about new processcs, methods, and technologies. And there's the passive, accepting side: making time or creating a place for new ideas. The best cross-pollinators cncourage both of these practices. Here are a few of the ways we try to make cross-pollination an integral part of the workplace.

## 1. SUBSCRIBE AND SURF

In your free time, browse magazines (and books) and don't forget to surf the Intcrnet. We call this "idea wading"—so the farther afield the better. We've got subscriptions to more than a hundred magazines, everything from Abitare to Zoon, and we get a kick out of checking out the latest offbeat website. Tom Peters says he routinely picks up as many as fifteen magazines from the airport newsstands and then spends the entire flight tearing out pages from interesting and offbeat articles. Around IDEO café tables, employees routinely flip through magazines, exposing themselves to the latest ideas, shapes, and advertisements. Bike magazines can be particularly inspirational, since bicycle companies constantly experiment with new materials. Browse the aisles at your favorite bookstore and make a practice of looking at sections you've never checked out before. You'd be surprised at how much you can learn about the latest trends by scanning titles and book jackets.

## 2. PLAY DIRECTOR

Take on the perspective of a film director. Break the world down into scenes and become expert at watching people perform even the smallest tasks. Like pumping gas. Flying a kite. Or carrying stuff to the beach. Watching events outside your normal sphere can heighten your observation skills. Take in a cricket match. Watch workers at a loading dock. Visit a factory. Or check out the FedEx man in his truck during the late afternoon crunch.

## 3. HOLD AN OPEN HOUSE

Periodically hold open houses to spread the best disciplines of your company far and wide. Keep it casual, displaying a few crude prototypes or perhaps a quick posterboard describing what you're working on. Photos, sketches, or some simple graphics work well. Combine it with some food to get people talking. Encourage comments and ideas, and you'll be surprised at how much you'll learn talking to visitors from other departments.

## 4. INSPIRE ADVOCATES

People talkabout racial and cultural diversity. Mcntal diversity can be just as inportant. We all need individuals who celebrate diffcrent viwpoints. Ricksn Sun as becoe ure fctree pron, skilld inr pacage ts. JnFulnSur yrs ocreuseatns havmadeet rentativor derlyBrendan Boy' pogus venihs ad i p on kids. We've got half a dozen other advocates. They've become part of our fabric. They make us all a little more conscious of critical constituencies and considerations in every new project.

## 5. HIRE OUTSIDERS

Fresh blood invigorates a company and introduces new ideas. Hire people just slightly off center, and you'll be pleasantly surprised. Dennis Boyle and Tim Billing, two IDEO leaders, came from General Motors, which might seem odd, since at the time we'd done no work for the car industry. And, of course, there's Jim Yurchenco, the first IDEO hire, and one of our most solid technical performers over the years. Yurchenco had no formal training in engineering and spent most of his college and graduate years soldering sculptures out of metal.

## 6. CHANGE HATS

We've already talked about the importance of observations. One of our very first clients was Telesensory Systems, creating reading machines for the blind. Team members were taken to thcir own house, blindfolded, and told to try making a peanut butter and jelly sandwich. Fumbling around without the benefit of sight, some couldn't find the jars, others the bread. Spreading on the peanut butter was tough. They gained a small but tangible appreciation of the challenges of blindness.

The best new products are like shoes someonc else has broken in. Smart folks have already anticipated likely problcms and opportunities. The difficulty most companies have when they try to do this is that they have trouble getting out of their own shoes.

Great products or services tell stories. It's a conscious narrative process. We often work up compelling characters to represent some possible users of ncw products. We might sketch them, give them names and families—background stories, in movie business lingo. The next time you start a ncw project, try dreaming up a few of these archetypes to help you see a little more broadly.

## 7. CROSS-TRAIN

Some athletes improve their performance in an event by training in multiple sports. We, too, can benefit from little drills or processes that we pick up from other businesses. Even the best companies can suffer from some overlooked weak internal business system. When my brother David joined NCR Corporation twenty-five years ago to work on early ATM machines, he found out that the company had a hopelessly disorganized scheme for tracking product drawvings and documentation. But David had learned a great system during his brief tenure at Boeing, so he introduced a similar approach at NCR.

This principle, of course, is exactly why businesses have boards of directors with executives from widely different fields. Sometimes wve focus too much on "the competition." IDEOers spend as much time learning from noncompetitive industries. Simple solutions to many problems are often just down the street—waiting for you to find them.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/668f9bd9192a47b7118565d1abf7f75da4db4f4ab869bf0b991cd2236e66221f.jpg)

t happens on almost every project we work on. You observe, brainstorm, prototype—and still you occasionally stumble.

You're facing a barrier. The barrier might be external—cultural resistance to a new product or technology. Or it might be an ingrained ritual. How people perceive and use products often handicaps innovations. Companies get this wrong more than almost anything else. What people will accept or reject is a fast-moving target. And to make it even harder, you've usually got to predict this a couple of years out in the future. If that's not bad enough, there's the traditional barrier of patents.

The first step is anticipating and recognizing these barriers. The biggest barrier to innovation, of course, is a company's mind-set. We've assisted and watched some of our partners leap what seemed to be pretty high barriers; though in truth often the best path is to sidestep the obstacle. We've learned a lot about the little "handshakes" you can make with the people who use your products. We've learned that success often lies in embracing the spirit of the best of rituals. And we know that clever, coordinated effort—good old evangelism by any other name—can often get you over that hurdle when there's no way around it.

Barriers often stump even the best innovators. They wall in your imagination like a prison yard. Several years back, for example, my brother David figured the latest innovation was asking too much of people. He was being given a personal demonstration of a radically new mobile computing interface. Though impressed, he believed the device would face an uphill battle. People won't take the time to relearn how they use a computer, David argued. Why would someone want to learn a whole new way to write?

Over the years, David has been pitched thousands of inventions and innovations, everything from wearable computers to self-closing toilet seats. If there's one thing he's learned, it's that you should never underestimate the barriers to people adopting a genuinely new product.

So David seemed the ideal person to gauge the success of this particular innovation. He knows his computer interfaces and even teaches a course on the subject at Stanford. IDEO made the first commercial mouse, plenty of trackballs, joysticks, and virtual reality headsets, as well as more than a few radical input devices that never found a home.

But the simple shorthand called Graffiti offered benefits that far outweighed the time and effort it took to learn. And the technology was in the right hands. The man giving David the personal demonstration in January 1995 was none other than Jeff Hawkins, a veteran of GRiD Systems, makers of the original laptop computer. And Hawkins had tasted enough failure in the mobile world to make him battle-ready.

David was right that Hawkins faced some tough hurdles. And not only with his novel interface. Half a dozen major efforts at PDAs (personal digital assistants) had flopped in the previous few years, taking with them a billion dollars of capital. But the real and perceived value of Hawkins's new technology, plus a few smart bridges to make the leap easier (which I'll get to in a minute), made the Palm Pilot a runaway hit.

Of course, Jeff Hawkins and the Cinderella story of the Palm Pilot is the exception to the rule. Why are the barriers to adoption of consumer products so high that seemingly intelligently conceived and well-marketed products stumble? Is there a way to bridge the gap?

Entrepreneurs like Hawkins tend to have an intuitive sense of the barr ttn ut mosu uldulte acinizing and responding to potential obstacles. A good place to start is to look overseas.

## WATCHING THE BORDER

Oceans and cultural divides can be big barriers tochange. Not surprisingly, many high-tech consumer products like cell phones are embraced by diverse cultures, largely because there's no history or tradition attached to them. Meanwhile, products with a long heritage like hand tools or furniture or kitchen utensils can be problematic. Cars and personal stereos have proved to be fairly international. But you can be surprised by what is not. Take vacuum cleaners, for instance.

Our research discovered that Japanese consumers prefer their vacuum cleaners quieter and less powerful with smaller motors than the American vacuums. Japanese apartments and homes are much smaller than American ones and the walls are thinner. Some Japanese shoppers will pay for "fuzzy logic" features that actually turn down their vacuum cleaner's power if they detect that a rug isn't too dirty. Japanese customers also are quite willing to pay extra for insulation to dampen the noise of their vacuums.

Americans, on the other hand, want their vacuums loud. We may not ask for them that way, but we associate the noise with a powerful motor and more suction. "If it ain't loud, it ain't working" seems to be the thinking. Our advertisements tout amperage the way hot rod magazines brag about horsepower. A feature that slows down the motor? Why would I want that? Once you know that the underlying desires of the two consumer groups are so different, it's easy to recognize the difficulty in trying to make a "global" vacuum cleaner.

Adoption curves vary tremendously from culture to culture and from product to product. Think of drink cup holders in cars. Who could argue with the benefits? By quickly integrating cup holders into their cars, Lexus and other automakers adeptly mastered one of those small but telling features that can make such a difference. Yet some European manufacturers were slow to embrace cup holders. Why? Perhaps because Europeans spend far less time commuting, drive shorter distances, and seldom pick up beverages at the drive-through window. But the mind-set of some European sports car makers also played a part. Some assumed their cars were such a pleasure to drive that no one would possibly want to compromise their performance or spoil the experience by taking one hand off the wheel to hold a cup of coffee.

The adoption of technology can be equally puzzling. Who would have predicted twenty years ago that the world's highest concentration of cell phones would be in Finland? What were some of the bridges for this tiny northern European country? First there were the commercial and national incentives of having world-leading cellular technology. Finland also has some of the lowest mobile phone rates in the world as well as the world's first global system for mobile communications. Nor did it hurt that Finland has long had a tradition of communication expertise. Early in the century, it boasted more than eight hundred local phone companies.

Yet there are technologies that inexplicably lie dormant for years before catching fire. Why did several decades pass before the fax machine invented in the early 1900s, took off? Since early faxes were expensive and unreliable, they had few advantages over the telegraph, which was superior at shuttling words. In Japan, however, there were cultural incentives to innovation. The telegraph and other existing textbased systems couldn't easily handle the ideographic Japanese language. So the Japanese government launched a massive research effort and urged manufacturers to adopt a common communications standard. Not surprisingly, Japan became a world leader in digital faxes.

Clearly, myriad factors can influence the spread of innovation. The food industry offers rich examples of how tradition can defeat cange. Take the story of Betty Crocker's rude awakening when it tried to peddle its moist cakes in England. They didn't fly. The English like dry cakes and cookies with their tea, often using their fingers. They didn't want a buttery, melt-in-your-mouth cake. Or condensed soup, for that matter, which also failed to catch on in Britain because consumers felt like they were getting half the amount for twice the price. In Japan thick American-style disposable diapers flopped because diapers there are changed more often and storage space is minimal.

You can'simply skate over cultural differences. We nore tradiion or widely held beliefs, however mistaken we might believe them to be, aour Thee holove efc inhi p c tinues down a straight path, even after reality has turned a corner. Consider the case of cattle guards. To keep the beasts from wandering through a road, many ranchers install expensive steel cattle guards slippery grates that the hoofed animals can't cross. But once the cattle have learned to be wary of the grates, all the ranchers have to do is stripe the road with white paint. The cows keep their distance.

Stuc yo thikiActally, an the meg

Folks who grew up in the fifties or sixties tend to rinse off their dishes and put them in the dishwasher practically clean. That, despite the fact that our clients at KitchenAid tell us this ritual has long been made obsolete by newer, more effective machines. What's the lesson? Often you've got to work especially hard to overcome rituals or superstition.

## STUBBORNLY UNMETRIC

To truly understand the extent to which people or nations can resist change and innovation, we need look no further than our own front door. Incredibly, the United States has become the last major holdout against converting to the undeniably better metric system of measurement; only Liberia and Myanmar keep us company. Americans for Customary Weights and Measures dubs the metric system a "European fad." One senator from North Dakota claimed our nation couldn't afford to convert. Richard Weinstein, NASA's manager of engineering studies, went so far as to tell the New York Times in 1996, "We got pretty well into the space station design using inches and pounds. We'll accommodate the metric system where we can, but we simply cannot start over. The expenses would be prohibitive."

Perhaps if NASA had mustered the courage to leap the imperial hurdle, they could have averted the \$140-million Mars Climate Orbiter disaster. The team at the Jet Propulsion Laboratory in Pasadena used the metric system to compute the force exerted by the probe's thrusters. But the folks at Lockheed were thinking imperial. The result was that the Mars Climate Orbiter entered the planet's harsh atmosphere about eighty kilometers low, and likely broiled to a crisp. Yet the October 1999 debacle didn't stir a debate over the inherent foolishness of a twomeasurement system. The media generally referred to it as a math error.

Orbiter was unmanned, but other metric mishaps could cost lives. In 1983 a Boeing 767 flown by Air Canada ran out of fuel in midair because a manual fuel-level calculation had confused pounds with kilograms. Fortunately, the Air Canada pilot had experience flying glider planes, and, miraculously, no one was killed in the dramatic engineless landing.

Searching for examples of metric/imperial conversion problems, I didn't have to look very far. In the building right next door to me, we were recently designing a simple plastic belt clip for the Handspring

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/5a81badce074ca165d0a307795532591a567d73f233ab0a095fe15d1f5ab3701.jpg)  
A metric mix-up: [left side] What the engineer wanted—a belt clip 12 centimeters high. [right side] What he got—a "belt clip" 12 inches high.

Visor. Sending the design out to amodel shop for fabration, we speeified eentimeters, but through miscommunieation, the vendor was thinking inehes. So what happened? You guessed it. The parts eame baek exaetly 2.54 times too big, looking more like a baekpaek than a belt elp. Il bet this sort of thig happes mor often than most flks would like to admit. Engineers and maehinists routinely say, "Take a mil off that surface."Trouble is, a "mil"ean mean either a milimeter or a thousandth of an inch. No wonder confusion erops up.

Why hasn't the metrie system taken hold in the United States? I think it's a simple lack of motivation. Nobody has provided a compelling reason to make the shift. Contrast that to the tradition-bound Brits, who created the imperial system by royal deeree in 1215. Nearly eight eenturies later, Britain boldly deelared September 30, 1995, "M-day." Most of the nation's businesses were ordered to go metrie, with offending shop owners fined thousands of pounds (in theory, anyway). The British laid elever bridges to relieve the fears of traditionalists, nostalgie exemptions for the beloved pint and mile, what the Department of Trade and Industry ealled "deseriptive measures." Poets eomplained mightily, writing that phrases like the Merehant of Veniee's pound of flesh—454 grams—just wouldn't be the same. But when all was said and done, the meter rose and the pound fell. Dragging our feet on innovation, Ameriea eolleetively assumes the cost of eonverting is too high. Yet someday—soon, I suspeet-we'll deeide that it eosts too mueh not to convert.

## SIDESTEPS

In 1990 GRiD Systems introdueed the first pen-based portable eomputer that featured handwriting recognition. The produet was a little ehunky and sluggish, but reviewers saw GRiDPad's promise, and eustomers pined for a peppy, pint-sized eomputer. And so began the first pen-based computer craze. Venture capitalists poured money into companies like Go Corporation, Apple accelerated development of the Newton, and John Sculley gave the phenomenon a name, the PDA, whcn he started rhapsodizing about his "personal digital assistant."

Jeff Hawkins, who had helped create the GRiDPad, left to create Palm Computing in January 1992, and threw everything into his first effort there, the Zoomer—complete with a minuscule keyboard, drivers for printers and fax machines, and handwriting recognition software. He learned the hard way that trying to be all things to all people for seven hundred dollars was a recipe for a consumer electronics disaster. Both the Newton and the Zoomer faded, followed by other shortlived products from Sharp, Hewlett-Packard, Go, EO, and Toshiba.

Remarkably, Hawkins got another chance. Laying the first bridge to adoption, he asked what had gone awry. Surveys of Zoomer customers gave him valuable insights. Following a long-honored tradition in the computer industry, manufacturers tried to cram all sorts of capabilities into the tiny devices. But most customers were more interested in something considerably less complicated. They were looking for a reasonable complement to their PCs, not necessarily a replacement.

He discovered another popular misconception as well. Hawkins and nearly every other vendor had been pounding their stylus against what seemed a fundamental dilemma. Handwriting recognition software has to recognize your handwriting. And since the softwvare required to accomplish that was incredibly complex, PDAs had to be painfully slow. Or did they? Hawkins's research into cognition and neurobiology helped him sidestep this stumbling block. He'd learned about autoassociative memory, the human brain's ability to fill in pieces of the puzzle. Why couldn't people do part of the work?

Hawkins's radical idea was to get PDA users to do some of the heavy lifting. Slim down the software and hardware by off-loading some of the pattern recognition to humans. Create a language—Graffiti—that is simpler for the computer to understand.

Hawkins believed people would make the leap necessary to adopt Graffiti because his research had taught him that people genuinely thirsted for powerful new tools. But he knew the learning curve couldn't be too high. He practiced endlessly with pen and paper, refining and simplifying his Graffiti alphabet before incorporating it into the Palm. Colleagues and friends were surprised at how easy it was to learn the streamlined, single-stroke letters. Hawkins's other big focus was the size of his PDA, and he found the ideal metaphor—the shirt pocket. Mindful of the Zoomer's excess, Hawkins challenged every feature's right to survival, and week by week the device got simpler. He developed a prototype in just a few months and in April 1996 launched the Palm Pilot. It took off like a rocket, gaining market acceptance at a faster rate than the color TV, the cell phone, and the personal computer.

The story of the Palm Pilot fits neatly into the S-curve theory that every technology or industry has a rate of inception, adoption, and decline. In the classic S-curve model, there's a long, slow developmentand-acceptance period, until the product or service reaches an inflection point and suddenly surges. Since trees never grow to the sky, that growth curve inevitably tapers off into another flat part of the curve. Over the years, IDEO has ridden the S-curve like a roller coaster.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/a15548885e735ba8739a5d911ef8d081248d012362c6c53b8121c73fc9e33370.jpg)

When we first started working in the computer field, machines were fixed to desks. Then, in the space of one explosive decade, the center of our universe leaped from luggable to portable and finally something you could hold in your hand. You could say that nearly all innovation programs are either about pushing a product past the inflection point or jumping to a new S-curve.

Our experience with pen-based computing has taught me that there's often a hidden barrier in the S-curve. Individual companies and entire industries often slow adoption rates by embracing faulty assumptions. To me, the more than half dozen highly funded pen-based efforts that preceded the Palm Pilot failed for essentially the same reason. Each was operating under a PC-replacement theory—the idea that a pen-based computer had to achieve 100 percent handwriting recognition. Since that was an enormously difficult technical hurdle, they all failed.

The real barrier to the initial PDAs wasn't the limits of software and hardware in a handheld box. It was the idea that the machine had to do nearly everything, that people couldn't and wouldn't want to learn. Personally, I find the simple Palm OS products compelling. I carry my Palm V everywhere, get a kick out of using the stylus, enjoy beaming contacts to other Palm users, and have found that I have a stronger connection to the Palm than I've had with any other computing device.

What's the lesson here? When the public is slow to accept a new technology, you need to reexamine your assumptions. Sometimes the wisest course you can take is to start with a modest step forward, like exploiting a niche. Early on, pen-based computing, for instance, made great sense for applications that didn't require lots of handwriting. Indeed, of all the pen-based machines we worked on before Palm, there's one that's still in service. Next time you rent from Hertz, you'll see it. It's the rugged litte Norand PenKey computercarried by almost evey Hertz bus driver.

There's another factor, of course, one that you won't find many engineers discussing. Thresholds of acceptance and rejection exist for every new product and service. For example, while cell phones are starting to permeate American culture, it's still a more complex tale than you'd find among early adopters like Finland, where mobile phones are nearly ubiquitous and fully socially acceptable. In the U.S. there's still some lingering social resistance to cellular technology, even when it's not disrupting your dinner conversation. I frequently use my cell phone when rushing from one IDEO building to another, a span of several blocks. My unscientific study suggests that when I'm walking with my phone, motorists at the intersection are less patient, more likely to cut me off, and much more inclined to give me a glaring look. As I write, cities and states across America are contemplating bans on cell phones in restaurants, bars, and even on roads. Some people find them intrusive nd diepecful, epecially when yo'e pa r expensive ritual, lke an exquisite meal in a fine restaurant. I'm not surprised that many people are in favor of a ban. Cell phones, as I've described before, are "verb" products, and thus move with us and create extraordinary opportunities and obstacles. It's an idea well worth keeping in mind, especially as all kinds of devices become mobile and tied to the Internet.

Social norms are notoriously difficult to predict and at times even tougher to change. There was a time when answering machines were considered rude. Then, in the space of just a few years, it suddenly became rude if you didn't have one. E-mail surveillance has long been feasible for corporations, but I doubt most workers embrace the technology (or for that matter, that it contributes to an innovative culture). People take privacy seriously. You can't overlook the intrusion effect. When we were once asked to do some work on a biometrics access device—the equivalent of a digital handprint—we learned a couple of hard lessons. Technically, you only need the rough outline of a hand. In fact, just the relative length of the fingers and the spacing of the knuckles can separate one hand from thousands. But we also found out that many people thought of handprints in the same way they thought of fingerprints. It's intrusive and makes them feel like criminals. That's why biometric access controls still raise some concerns, though it's a barrier that may be falling soon.

## DEAL KILLERS

Silicon Valley marketing expert Regis McKenna talks about the FUD factor—fear, uncertainty, and doubt. FUD may not be totally rational, but it's very real. When we worked on the EV1 electric car charger, we discovered from our research that Americans are anxious about handling electric currents over 110 volts. The midlevel GM car charger was going to be 440 volts. Conventional wisdom suggested using a meaty three-pronged plug like the one on your electric dryer with metal-to-metal contacts. But getting people to embrace electric cars was already an uphill battle. The last thing GM wanted to do was to scare off potential drivers. So we helped them scrap the fork and design a meaty rubber handle that tapers to a paddlelike charger that neatly docks into the car. The energy is transferred by inductive current. The paddle not only is safe but also looks safe. Sometimes you need both.

It's a scenario we've seen countless times. More often than not with new products or services, you've got to acknowledge potential "deal killers." Reach out to the public, or at least meet them halfway. Think of early e-commerce, when people worried about their credit card numbers being stolen, and pundits declared we wouldn't shop on-line. Vendors responded with a series of strategies to diminish that risk and reluctance, features like the "secure connection" dialog box, displaying only the last few digits of your credit card number. And, of course, they also tossed in free shipping, coupons, and other e-centives.

Many new products flop because something critical is left out. Jake Burton Carpenter, founder and owner of Burton Snowboards, says innovation happens at his entrepreneurial company by looking for what i' there. "We ask," he says, "what do peole want? What'missig?"

A few years back, California inventor Tom Christianson came up with a fog-free shaving mirror for the shower. With high hopes, he showed the ingenious device—the trick is in circulating hot water behind the mirror—to senior management at Sharper Image. But the mirror required a pair of pliers to install, and Sharper Image doesn't sell "do-it-yourself" products. Deal killer? Not quite. Nimble Christianson figured out a way to accompany the mirror with inexpensive pliers. Sharper Image changed its mind, and shipped pliers with each mirror. Richard Thalheimer's ad copy read, "The installation is fast and straightforvard, even if you aren't handy"—and the ShowerTek fogfree mirror was a success.

There are also the countless products that don't compellingly differentiate themselves from what's already on the market. Remember the Susan B. Anthony dollar? It wvas almost exactly the same size as a quarter. It's almost as if the U.S. Mint skipped the consumer research stage. Standardized coin sorters mistook the coins for quarters. Cashiers couldn't distinguish them from twenty-five-cent pieces, and people occasionally overpaid for items. The mint experienced something pretty close to a worst-case product launch scenario: a product that failed miserably and generated bad word of mouth.

So how do you recover from a humiliating disaster? The new head of the U.S. Mint took some key steps to make certain he didn't make the same mistake the second time around. The new Sacagawea coin dollar is substantially bigger and thicker than a quarter, and, just in case there's any confusion, it's tinted gold. It just might work.

## PATENT PENDING

When launching a new product of any kind, what can you do when you're faced with what seems the ultimate barrier, a competitor who seems to have a whole product category locked up through a bevy of patents? Is it possible to develop a product in the same category without getting sued?

It's a challenge IDEO has faced more than a few times. Many years ago Smith Corona came to us with an intriguing project. Remember the Dymo label makers that squeezed letters onto plastic strips that you'd peel off and stick on all your earthly possessions? Japanese manufacturers had created updated versions of the label maker, which they protected with more than forty patents. Smith Corona was interested in entering the market but worried about the formidable patent barrier.

One of the first steps in such a project is the obvious one. Somebody's got to start reading—in this case an intimidating stack of patents and more than six hundred claims. Rickson Sun, one of our most technical and scientifically curious engineers, led the project. He just started trying out all the existing label makers to see how they worked. He wasn't impressed. The patents mainly covered the process of opening a door and taking a cartridge out. Rickson thought the mechanisms were clunky and required too many steps. To replace the tape on one label maker, you had to flip it over on its back, unhook a latch, lift a door, wiggle the cartridge out, put in a new one, wiggle it into place, shut the door, and close the latch. Wouldn't it be easier if you could load the cartridge from the side and not have to flip it over? wondered Rickson.

When you're competing against an existing, established product, sometimes the best thing you can do is start from scratch. Rickson knew there had to be something easier for users. And that's how he came up with a simple push-button ejector. Push it and the spent cartridge would pop out. Indeed, the one-step-out, one-step-in motion worked so effortlessly that it was far easier to change label colors.

And it won a patent.

We've talked about some of the obstacles to better products and services. But there's another, tougher kind of barrier. It's the barrier in your mind, or in your office, and it can sap your strength like a virus.

W Stay blcyu ll

What's the opposie  writer' block? This may sound a bit tucyfeelyu y a it as a stream of energy. We all recognize the euphoric moments when ideas and decisions come effortlessly. It's a kind of magic that loosens us up and lets us perform at our peak. Athletes call it being in "the zone" and psychologists call it "flow." But athletes who overtrain and students who cram too hard often find that zone of peak performance to be elusive. The trick is to find that delicate balance between complete choreoy and pure nspration. Ie el it when I trid to play olAt he driving range, I seemed to momentarily master the mechanics of the game, but then I'd go out on the course and let one bad hole turn me into a shanking machine. And yet, every once in a while, the ball sometimes seemed to magically leap off my club when I was barely trying.

Creativity works much the same way. All the talent and intelligence in the world can hold you back rather than help to foster creativity if you are uptight. No matter what our God-given abilities and finely honed skills, we all can use a little help now and then. I think of it as trying to keep your bag of magical instruments in tune, working and playing at achieving a harmony that wvards off the inevitable dips in creative juices.

In the stream of innovation, many companies make the mistake of building dams instead of doing everything possible to increase the flow. But when a culture is devoted to searching for breakthrough ideas, it's as if the river keeper has opened all the floodgates.

That said, droughts come and go. Creativity requires careful tending. Over the years at IDEO, we've collected a list of the dams, and the floodgates that increase the flow of creativity and breakthrough ideas.

Good pilots never take off before running through a checklist. I think we all benefit by periodically cleaning out our organizational and mental attic, seeing if our thinking and processes need some spring cleaning. Here's my own quick creativity checklist.

## SKILL SETS

Sometimes we shut ourselves off from opportunities because a skill seems too hard to learn. But with the right approach, all sorts of skills are within our reach. Take simple sketching, which we have found invaluable in business communication. If you're meeting with someone over lunch or a drink, there's often nothing more powerful than making a few bold lines on a cocktail napkin. There's long been a certain magic between the cocktail napkin and business plans. For example, CEO Herb Kelleher says that the business plan for Southwest Airlines started with a simple triangle drawn on the back of a cocktail napkin. My brother David once had his Stanford students write the "big ideas" for their class project on napkins, as practice for being future Silicon Valley entrepreneurs. In everyday office meetings too, I often find it easier to drive home a point by scratching out a few circles and arrows on a marker board. But of course, many of us haven't done any serious drawing since, say, the fifth grade. And when we try to draw people, we revert to grade school habits.

If you've got the stick figure blues, David's got a wonderful cure that he picked up from a Stanford professor. Whether you're a kindergartner or a senior executive, he teaches the same way. Make your usual circle for a head, then imagine you're drawing a droopy, bulbous leaf. Resist those crabby old stick arms. Let your pen sweep out like a fourpointed leaf, one for each arm and leg. I think you'll be surprised. In five minutes I've seen David teach the most art-resistant corporate types how to sketch a far more friendly, human-looking person.

## HANDSHAKES

There's a good reason why I haven't talked that much about design. The classic image of the designer as the supreme architect of a product just isn't our style. Oh, we've got some of the best industrial designers in the world, and plenty of talented architects, but they always work in tandem with engineers and human-factors experts.

We work to bring the designer back to earth, and make him or her an integral part of a team that finds its inspiration in reality—how people actually interact with products, places, and ideas. This isn't about pretty colors, playful shapes, or style. To me, everybody can take the essence of good design and bring it to their business or products. Truly good design is about meeting people. It's about opening doors. Designers sometimes call these "affordances." I like to think of them as welcoming handshakes, visual clues that make you feel more comfortable.

We couldn't get through the day without good affordances. They're all around us. Think of red and green stoplights, the solid lines on the r that e say put, theokee that eu e  ps, and the raised dots on the shoulder that just may save your life. A flat plate or a curved handle tells us whether to push or pull the door. A weather vane tells us which way the wind is blowing, and, of course, Netscape's sootng stars etus ko e'e gg e.

Try boiling water in a teakettle that doesn't whistle, and you et some idea of what happens when affordanccs are lacking. The Paris Mtro makes you push a button to open thc door, and I've seen a few passengers whisked away to the next stop while waiting for them to open. Websites, of course, are in a class by themselves. Early ones were notoriously bad at the most basic communication. You were generally in the dark as to whether or not the site had finished loading, when you could click, where, and, what you might find. Nor is this a problem replaced by the gimmickry of bouncing images, streaming video, or rock tunes. Most sites don't have a clue about how to put visitors and customers in the driving seat.

Even the best companies can stray from their customers. We love the iMac, for example, but its first mouse wasn't up to Apple's standards. We've prototyped mice in just about every shape imaginable, including a small square that failed the human factors tests because it wasn't comfortable in an adult's hand and was too symmetrical for effortless orientation. We could have told Apple that a round mouse wouldn't work for the same reasons. The proof that Apple's mouse wasn't meeting people's needs (and lacked a critical affordance) can be seen in the thousands of people who have found that it only works with a third-party mouse holder-—the Mouse Catcher, a plastic, oblong mouse-shaped device that snaps on top.

In fact, the entire computer peripheral business is proof that there's often something missing. From printers to expansion cards, from keyboards to every input device imaginable, manufacturers have raced to make the computing experience more complete. Handspring anticipated this wave with its opcn-architccture Visor, which assumed that even a tiny PDA can be expanded and improved upon. Then there are the thousands of people that turn thcir Palms into mini-laptops with a folding keyboard. Auto parts storcs dedicate several aislcs to products that fix things auto manufacturcrs either didn't bother to do or did badly. Like scat belt adapters for kids or pulldown sun visors for people in the backseat.

Bad affordances surround us. They makc us grouchy, they make us late. They give us the sensation that wc'rc down in a drafty lobby, not knowving when to press the talk or listcn button on thc clunky old intercom. Think of how diffieult word processors make it to perform the simplest task, likc inserting a pagc break. Toys for grade schoolers that practically require an engineering background to asscmble. Voice mail systems that befuddlc us with too many choices. Childproof or tamperproof aspirin bottles that parents can barcly crack open.

Find something lacking in a product, and you're likely staring at a tremendous opportunity. Ever notice how computerized registers at many retail businesses can actually slow down checkout? Couldn't leaf blowers be quieter, or trash bags be made to decompose? Why did e-mail lack spell checkers for so many years?

It took nearly eighty years after the invention of the automobile for manufacturers to start seriously coming to grips with the fact that Americans practically live in their cars and thus require a vast array of nooks and crannies (like the first adjustable drink cup holder) to store things. Backseat VCRs may be just the latest in this continuing evolution. As I write, Ford is experimenting vith adding everything from washing machines to microwaves in its minivans.

Sometimes it's hard to tell whether something was really missing or whether you're just riding the latest craze. For example, at the turn of the millennium we did a translueent Visor for Handspring and a snappy translucent cable modem for 3Com. (Epson came out with a laser printer with a transparent cover that shows the paper's movement.) Some might argue that the wavc of translucent produets is a fbu     oe that often seemed remote.

Look around your desk and you'll likcly sec plenty of evidence of homespun affordances: mouse pads with images, clips to hold paper to read as you type, funky sercen savers to dccorate your monitor. (Interestingly, the pads are not neccssary exccpt as they define your mouse territory; similarly, scrccn savers may animate a dozing computer, but you no longer nced them on many machincs.)

Look at pcoplc or situations with unusual or specific needs, and you'll get a sensc of just how great the opportunity is for strong affordances. Think of how Braille madc reading possible for the blind. Or how shorthand made it possible oravcrage typists to kecp up with the spoken word.

In each case, innovators leaped a hurdle and rcached out to people. Think of the remarkable advanccs in computers, laptops, and handheld devices we've seen over the last twenty years. Mike Nuttall of IDEO made one of those seismic shifts in the early 80s when he decided thc monitor didn't have to rest on your CPU or phone books anymore, and could instead pivot on a ball and socket mechanism. Later, he helped shift the architecture of mobile computers from a flat brick to the hinged clamshell, that is so familiar today. We hardly notice these little things, the way keys have gradually receded on laptops to give our wrists a place to rest, the wvay sounds have become second nature. But would the iBook be the same if it didn't cheerfully chime when it powered up or make those cool little vacuumlike noises when we cut and paste?

Good products or services are like generous hosts. They greet you at the door, offer you refreshments, and make you feel at home. I think it's a good thought to keep in mind, and it's not something you have to limit to the marketplace. Look around your office or consider your latest project. Is there a step you might take to make your ideas a little more approachable?

## RITUALS REWARD

Just as Rome wasn't built in a day, rituals or traditions can somctimes create unusual challengcs and opportunities. Rituals can cause us to hold on to the remnants of old proccsses that still resonate with people. When Bayer started protecting its aspirin tablets with Toleraid microcoating back in the 1980s, the cotton balls packed in the bottle to keep tablets from breaking became superfluous. Yet it wasn't until the late 1990s that the cotton got left behind. Why? Customers wanted it. They said it reminded them of their childhood. There was also an enormous amount of myth wrapped up in that little ball of cotton—the idea that it kept the pills potent and soaked up damaging moisture. (Both notions are false, of course: Cotton actually draws in extra moisture from the outside air.) Pulling out the fluffy stuff had become part of the ritual of opening a bottle, something that told you it was new and safe. So strong was the nostalgia associated with the experience that only recently have Bayer and other manufacturers begun to ship aspirin bottles naked—without the cotton.

Indeed, how much change people will stomach is a moving target. When I was a boy, cake mixes tried to float the new, improved "just add water" versions. Homemakers revolted. It wasn't baking if they couldn't add an egg. So the cake mixes quickly retreated.

Try to replace a beloved ritual, and you'll find the barrier is extra high. Take the venerable wine cork. Everybody knows that fine wine needs a good cork, right? Only cheap wine has a screw top, right? So goes the popular mythology of wine and corks. The only trouble is that corks, while attractive and steeped in tradition, are a problematic way of maintaining the quality of fine wine. Over time, some corks fail. When the cork breaches, a tiny oxygen path opens and the wine starts to turn to vinegar. Sommeliers complain the wine is "corked."

Sean Corcorran at IDEO plumbed the depths of this phenomenon for a major wine maker. The brainstorms were wild. Sean bought, as he put it, "every bottle with a closure known to man," including obscure cosmetic bottles with plastic tops and quirky latches. A local wine store had a framed collage of the steps in the making of a champagne cork, so Sean managed to borrow it for the brainstorm.

The ideas ranged all over the map, including using so much of a non-

Or a combination cork and dispenser system. Oxygen spoils wine, but nitrogen preserves it. Sean skctched up a stopper with a onctime nitrogen charge you could dispensc when you didn't consumc the whole bottle.

After consulting with masters, Sean learned that metal actually makcs the best seal. Utilitarian crown caps—like those used on becr bottles—would be far superior to corks in maintaining the quality of fine wine. The metal cap with a rubberized scal keeps out air far better than cork, and lasts longer. Was this a dead end? No, not necessarily. Sean hatched the clever idea of making beautifully stamped crown caps that would be elegant and collectible.

Of course, you probably won't find any of these ideas at your local wine shop anytime soon. No matter that wine corks sometimes seal wine poorly and that collectors must pay experts to periodically reseal rare wines. Over the years, we've developed an emotional, ritual attachment to every part of the process of opening a bottle of wine, from stripping off the lead wrapping (now aluminum) to yanking out smelling, and sometimes keeping the cork. It's going to take considerable time and effort before wine corks are abandoned for bottle caps or an even better solution takes hold. Thousands of years of wine making have created one of the world's strongest rituais, a deeply ingrained mixture of myth, aesthetics, and habit that presents a formidable barrier to change.

We change our habits and expectations reluctantly, even when they seem to serve us badly. When I was in business school at Berkeley, I did a class project with a start-up company called Shoe Care. They said they wanted to be thc McDonald's of shoe repair and bring professional managemcnt systems to a cottage industry. They pointed out that shoe repair shops were dirty and crammcd from floor to ceiling with shocs that sometimes seemcd impossible to find. So thcy took what seemed like a rational coursc: Thcy broke that ritual with a pristine shop where the shocs were shippcd off-site to be rcpaired, and then returned neatly wrappcd. But whcn they opened thcir first shop in San Francisco's Civic Center, people didn't know what it was. The venture failed because it neglected to build a bridge from the familiar to the new.

A similar fate almost befell Harley-Davidson, which nearly went bankrupt trying to compete with Japanese motorcycle makers in the late 1970s and 1980s. But new management decided to reach out to customers. They took an observation-based approach and rode to rallies to see how bikers were souping up their Harleys. They even hit the open road themselves for inspiration. They learned that Harley enthusiasts were angry about the motorcycle maker straying from its heavy chrome past. And so Harley-Davidson returned to its roots, playing up the outrageous size, fury, and ornamentation of the original Harleys (adding a little modern reliability). By the early 1990s Harley-Davidsons were outselling Hondas, and it had won over some of the most loyal customers in the world. (How many of your customers would tattoo your brand on their arms?) The company reinvented itself by celebrating its tradition and heritage.

Lego, of course, is that rarest of breeds, an old tradition that jumped into the high-tech age with robot-controlled constructions that rival the world's most automated manufacturing plants. Good rituals don't die, they just get reinvented. Take the elaborate seals and stamps that personalized documents decades or even centuries ago. Now people sport elaborate e-mail personas and append their electronic missives with titles, schedules, even witty sayings. Browsing the old library card catalog wasn't the fastest way to search, but it may have allowed more opportunity for serendipitous discovery than the computer. When you consider that rich tradition, I'm not surprised that some of the early, overly digital and logical methods of mining the Net aren't necessarily the most successful. Think of Yahoo, which uses humans to catalog the We,and Ask Jeeves, which cleverly realized that people like asking questions in, God forbid, sentences.

Most baby boomers remember the transition from vinyl LP records to compact discs in the 1980s. CDs ushered in improved sound quality greater durability, and simple storage. By trading tradition oriovation, however, we did have to give up one small part of the music experience. Playing a record for the first time, listeners used to curl up with the oversized album covers, reading the liner notes and admiring the artwork. CD covers are just not the same.

In a similar way, we will go through a transition with hardcover books over the next decade. Everyone loves the feeling of curling up with a good book, and the prevailing opinion is that eBooks will never be the same. That's certainly true, but watch for eBooks to chip away at the ink-on-paper variety. Give us great prices, paperlike resolution, and new levels of interaction with authors, and most of us will switch over eventually.

Apple achieved that deeply textured emotional connection with its PowerBooks and iBooks, and I'd like to think we did it with the Apple mouse and the Palm V. It's what we strove for in our work on the leather-covered SoftBook electronic book. It doesn't happen often, but when it does, consider yourself lucky.

## EVANGELISM WORKS

Putting energy and effort into a product, service, or idea can be the difference betiveen a hit and a promising new product that doesn't quite catch fire. Where would the Macintosh be today without the enthusiasm and energy of Steve Jobs and his team of evangelists? The Macintosh wasn't sold so much as it was emoted, starting with that inspirational 1984 Big Brother advertisement that aired during the Super Bowl. Apple reached out to developers as well as consumers, knowing that without solid applications the machine would likely suffer the fate of its predecessor, the Lisa, which never caught on in the marketplace. Apple understood the importance of grassroots organizing and word of mouth. Macintosh user groups were embraced and made to feel part of a campaign, softening possible negative responses to thc first model, which lackcd, among other things, a hard disk.

We've met enough inventors over the years to realize the distance between a good idea and a succcssful product. Even if it's a true invention with little precedcnt, you'd better be prepared for some missionary selling.

A good idea isn't cnough. Take the nuisance of coins piling up around the house. If you're likc me, they collcct like weeds. Banks don't really want them, and, besidcs, does anybody like putting change into those little paper wrappers? Entrepreneur Jcns Molbak didn't, and he figured he wasn't alone. After earning his MBA at Stanford and interviewing grocery store shoppers in San Francisco, he realized he'd found a gap large enough to be filled by a business: The coins idle in American homes add up to several billion dollars. That's when Molbak came to us with the raw idea for a machine to exchange coins for cash in supermarkets. Nobody believed it could be a business.

We made five working machines that were installed in Safeway stores around the Bay Area. But this wasn't an impulse buy like candy by the checkout counter. The Coinstar only worked if you brought in your jars or sacks of coins. That made it at best a two-step processsee the machine, then bring in your stockpile of coins on the next trip. Slowly but surely, however, people started bringing in their pggy banks. Molbak got investors, a partner in manufacturing, and other stores to sign up.

Molbak had to do an enormous amount of evangelizing. The greencolored machine and its video-poker-likc screen featured images of a jar of coins. "Get cash. It's as easy as 1-2-3," said a big display. But even those incentives weren't enough. Marketing people had to stand by the machine and tell customcrs, "Have you seen our new Coinstar machine?"

Ultimately, with lots of hard work and cheerleading, the Coinstar became a succss. Today Coinstar has some seven thousand selfservice coin machincs in the United States, United Kingdom, and Canada and ranks as one of the nation's fastest-growing companies.

How did Molbak do it? He partnered with the leading grocery stores and created some bright incentives to encourage people to start the cycle—such as a Coins That Count program that funnels funds to nonprofit organizations. In the spring of 200o Coinstar hooked up with the U.S. Mint to promote the new Sacagawvea gold dollar. Not a bad partnership for a company in the coin recycling business.

## RUNNING WITH AL GORE

The importance of effort and clever persistence cannot be overstated. It's often not enough to work hard or follow what seems the logical course of action. Sometimes you've got to try a little ingenuity.

Several years ago, hoping to win an account to design some AT&T phones, David, Mike Nuttll, and I strapped on AT&T pagers. We set up an 800 number for AT&T executives to call, and modified one of their phones so that it had just one big button—a direct line to IDEO.
