We were wiling to answver questions from them day or night. They got the message that we were serious, and appreciated our playful approach. We won the account.

Rich Silverstein of the celebrated advertising firm of Goodby & Silverstein may take the cake for the most audacious effort to win over a potential client when he pitched Isuzu Motors. Silverstein printed up flyers and had them placed in the windshields of Isuzus all over San Francisco, offering to pay fifty dollars to anyone who parked his or her car within a two-block radius of the advertising office at the foot of Telegraph Hill. Who could resist? The visiting Isuzu executives were amazed to enter a neighborhood that looked like a giant Isuzu dealership, and Silverstein got the account.

Persistence pays off. You won't always succeed, but if you keep trying, eventually you're going to get through. Some years back, a few IDEOers caught wvind that Al Gore was coming to Silicon Valley. Dave

Blakely and another IDEO engineer, both serious runners, figured they'd try to join him on his morning jog and invite him over to IDEO. I can imagine a hundred reasons why Blakely's scheme wouldn't fly, one of them being Secret Service agents. But Blakely is used to making things happen.

"You don't think you're going running with the vice president, do you?" a Secret Service agent told Blakely when he arrived at the San Jose Hilton shortly after dawn. No one was allowed within eighty feet of the vice president. But Blakely trotted after Gore and his entourage and a mile later was running and chatting with him. Gore had just been on the Letterman show, so Blakely pitched him ten reasons, a la Letterman, why he should visit IDEO. Gore, as it turned out, was too busy at the time to schedule IDEO. But to me that's not the point. If your staff is regularly dreaming up creative ways to advance your company, you're sure to break through eventually.

When somebody champions an idea, it's amazing what a difference that person can make. I couldn't begin to recount the times we won a job or successfully finished one because someone decided to go to the mat for the project. No matter how cool your company may be, we all get tired and sometimes feel we're lost in the crowd. But match up a little bit of creativity with some persistence, and you might just be the person who changes the whole darn company. A simple idea could transform the way you're perceived. Like getting the American Dental Association to sanction your toothpaste or Leonardo DiCaprio to wear your sunglasses.

You could have that idea.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/e51414891d6a0dcd18051b6be007999d4a6a67610b272bd07fdb51226247de42.jpg)

Innovation is a very goal-oriented process, and hot groups under tight deadlines tend to focus closely on end results. As you step through the innovation process, try thinking verbs, not nouns. What does that mean? It means not focusing too much on the object or artifact: the new product, the big report, the latest ad campaign, the remodeled store. Everybody's in the business of creating experiences, so focus on the verbs, the actions. The goal is not a more beautiful store. It's a better shopping experience. And creating more value for your brand.

Look around and you'll see innovative companies and powerful brands linked with the word "experience." The Lexus experience, the Nordstrom experience, the Webvan experience. What's more, if you have been a customer of those companies, you have a clear idea of what is meant in each case. Lexus makes great cars, but the "experience" is more about pampering and courtesy and not just a smooth, easy ride. Nordstrom sells clothes, but people don't talk about their Nordstrom garments, they talk about the piano music, the friendly, attentive help, and going the extra mile. Webvan seils the ultimate commodity—groceries—but what it's really known for is convenience and timeliness.

How about your company? Are you an innovator in delivering experiences—either to your staff or to your customers? As you go through the innovation process, there are plenty of opportunities to seek out the verbs.

You can turn almost any product or service into a better-designed experience. When Amtrak asked us to help design a train interior for its Acela service between Washington and Boston, we came back with ten unique steps in a journey, everything from planning a trip to arriving at your destination. The key was recognizing the importance of moving beyond the physical train. Helping Amtrak redesign the insidc of thc train car was just part of the Amtrak expcrience.

In The Experience Economy Joseph Pine and James H. Gilmore argue that many businesses and services are coming to resemble the experience of a theme park. It's a concept, they believe, that is spreading rapidly throughout all kinds of industries. The day isn't far off, they say, that we'll actually find ourselves paying for the privilege of going to premier malls.

In explaining the evolution toward experience economies, they use the metaphor of a child's birthday cake. In a commodity-oriented economy, Mom bought flour, sugar, and eggs, adding all the labor herself to bake and ice a cake. The next evolutionary step—a product economy—has people buying Betty Crocker cake mix and a can of icing. In a service economy, the cake is made in a bakery, and they'll even put your child's name on it. But in an experience economy, you take Mikey and his friends to the local Chuck E. Cheese or Discovery Zone, where they are provided with active play, pizza, drinks, and—oh, by the waya birthday cake at the end.

Think about experiences that you might stage for your business. Sales teams, with their focus on results and rewards, have alvays done this on a grand scale. Many companies have some version of a President's Club. When I was at General Electric, the reward event wvas called the Mark Makers. I was amazed at the over-the-top experiences they designed for their salespeople. First, they'd fly three hundred of us and our significant others to Hawai. The theme parties were pretty impressive. One year they converted the entire grounds of a lavish hotel into a MASH unit and dressed us up in fatigues. Another year they flew in Monty Hall of Let's Make a Deal to give away trips to the Bahamas and cars to the top producers. Within days of returning home, professional-quality photos of you and your spouse showed up in the mail. People talked about the event for months.

OK, so how do you create a designed experience for a new product or service? Here's a hypothetical example of the kinds of steps we often go through.

Tak an expeience you'e miliar with. Say r travel. I yo'e flown lately, how would you describe the experience? I'll bet you noticed something that could be improved. When we design an experience, we add up all those small incremental improvements into a better complete experience.

One way to begin is to break the product or service down into its component elements. The DNA of the experience, as it were.

What are the main steps in taking a flght? In simple ters, you plan the trip, deal with the airport, board the plane, fly, and arrive at your destination. Unfortunately, it's hard to get insights from those general steps. You have to go to a much finer level of detail. For instance, here are some of the steps involved in just one part of the journey—the airport experience:

Arriving at the airport by car, taxi, shuttle, bus, or train

Finding the right terminal

3.Checking in and checking luggage at the counter

4Confirming or getting your seat assignment and departure gate

Clearing security

6.Locating and reaching your gate

7.Checking the boarding status and updated flight schedule

8. Making upgrade or standby transactions

9.Deciphering preboarding announcements

.Walking down the jetway

What's the dream scenario? James, your limousine driver, wheels you through a special security gate out onto the tarmac where your private jet is waiting with its engines running. While James loads your luggage, you step onto the plane and the doors close behind.

For a few dozen corporate CEOs and celebrities, life is like that, but it probably doesn't match your recent flying experience.

So what's the more typical scenario? You have probably not been hit

You leave plenty of time to get to the airport, but get bogged down in traffic, misread the exit sign, then can't find your terminal and have to circle the entire airport. Your problcms have only just begun. A ballparksized line snakes nearly to thc terminal entrance door. Though you're not sure what's the right line, out of desperation you jump in, only to learn ten minutes later you're in thc wrong line.

When you finally reach the counter, the agent either disappears into thc back room or ignores you and spends sevcral irritating minutes helping the confused agent beside him with someone else's botched ticket. Next, you wait in line at security for the chance to hoist your carry-on bag onto the conveyor belt, empty your pockets, hold up your arms, and be treated like a criminal.

After some minor confusion with the airport signage, you find your gate—which, of course, is at the end of the most distant spoke of the terminal. You hear on the public address system that a flight is in the final stages of boarding, but you can't tell if it's yours. Although your flight seems ready to leave, there's no one to ask whether your section has boarded. In some versions of this scenario, you actually make your flight. But as you're kept vaiting until the last possible moment for an upgrade, you worry (correctly) that the overhead bins are filling up.

Guess what? You lose. No upgrade, and now you've got to check your laptop.

Let's face it. A fair amount of the airline experience isn't what it should be. I believe that customers will rewvard the airlines that strive to systematically improve this process. Here are some possible ways IDEO might redesign parts of this experience:

Your color-coded ticket features an icon that corresponds with the departure terminal, and the outdoor signage itself is colorful and memorable. Inside, customer scrvice agents and well-placed signs make it foolproof to find the right line. While waiting in line you check in at a rental-car-style mobile kiosk so that when you hit the counter, it only takes seconds.

In security, a floor-level conveyor belt effortlessly sends your bag through the X-ray machine without you having to hoist it up.

A smart ticket envelope (you got at the ticket counter) with a color-coded map directs you to your gate. There, a striking revenuegenerating display (funded by advertising) tells you what's up with your flight. It's on time, and rows 30 to 50 are currently boarding. You can see the number of seats available for upgrade and how many people you're competing with for a seat. Enter your ticket in a mag stripe, and it shoots out your upgrade number for the waiting list.

OK, it's time to board. A large monitor over the gate door tells you instantly which rows are boarding and how many minutes remain before the door shuts. A flashing icon on the display unequivocally announces when late arrivals forfeit their reservations and seats become available.

This is all hypothetical, of course, and if we were working for a clien this would just be  start. Still, I' rgue we've redy maped out some general ideas about how to design not only a smoother, more hassle-free experience but one in which the traveler has more control.

You probably don't work for an airline, but you've seen how you can begin to ask the basic question. How can I redesign my operation, install the latest technology, or retrain my staff to deliver a better experience—without, of course, breaking the bank? Start by following your customer journey, breaking it down into its component elements, and asking yourself how you can deliver a better experience.

It's not all about intensely complex services or products.

We've found that the smallest devices or services can inspire welldesigned, worthwhile experiences. Years ago, for example, Bell & Howell asked us to look at the inventory control systems it sold to auto parts mechanics and auto service centers. We observed that the typical sales process involved the clerk asking the customer if he'd pulled up the right part. Trouble was, the customer had to lean over the counter to glimpse the display, a cumbersome process at best. That awkwardness led us to design a display that could pivot 360 degrees, so the clerk could swing the part description and diagram toward the customer (a capability we evolved a decade later for NEC laptops). It's a small innovation. But it significantly changed the way customers felt about the sales process.

Innovation sometimes starts with a small improvement. But a better-designed experience often comes about when you can transform a niche product to something broader that resonates with your customers.

Merloni, one of the largest appliance vendors in Europe, asked us to design a tiny controller for the home that would help customers manage their power usage and shut off appliances before fuses could blow. Electricity is extremely expensive in Europe, and if Merloni could help customers use its machines more efficiently, and automatically notify its service department when something needed attending to, the company was bound to strengthen its brand.

Early in the project, however, an IDEO team led by Douglas Dayton realized that the device Merloni was considering would require so little interaction that customers would forget about it. Nor would they likely see much added value. We advocated a new approach. Why not use this minor product as part of a home communications hub, providing calendar and time management, shopping lists, food ordering, weather channels, and radio? We hooked up a device we designed to the Internet and showed how Merloni could recruit content providers to offer its customers a variety of services. The resulting Home Smart Monitor still accomplished the original power leveraging and service functions. But the breakthrough was adopting active, vital usage scenarios that would be attractive to families. That didn't just give the device a raison d'être; it also lit the path for new business models for Merloni—that of service provider for some 7 million European customers.

A healthy portion of our work involves exploring new uses for existing technology or services. This is something nearly any company can do—and it can reap rich rewards. More and more of our work seems to reinforce the notion that no matter what the produet or service might be, ultimately it's about some sort of human experience. When we worked with Medtronic on new systcms for implantablc defibrillators several IDEOers were asked to gain some firsthand insight into the cardiac patient's experience. I wore a pager for an entire weekend, and each time it buzzed out of the blue it signaled that I'd just gone into fibrillation, and received a powerful shock to restore normal heart function. It made me sce how easily you could be hurt if you fell to the ground, and how many hard objects surround us. One time my beeper went off when I was alone in my garage operating a table saw, a scary situation for cardiac arrest and the jolt of defibrillation that it would trigger. Other team members carried the beepers for a weekend as well, and the exercise gave them new insights to help them make better products and services.

Consumer technology leaders recognize that on some level they're no longer in the gadget business. Recently, we worked with Nokia and several European universities to explore how mobile technologies could be used to increase rather than reduce face-to-face interpersonal communication. In some markets, coming up with compelling reasons for people to use your product or service is a question of survival. If you don't, you risk becoming as obsolete as the typewriter.

## MAKE EXPERIENCES ENTERTAINING

While the experiential approach I'm talking about is fairly new to Internet devices, cell phones, and computers, it's a tried-and-true concept in the retail and entertainment industries. Major retailers were among the first to recognizc that they were selling themed entertainment as much as the merchandise on their shelves. I think there's a lot to learn from their successes and failures.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/9afb4ac9eb3d6382fdeceb62d97a646de7c636a5d4f48cb71249b2ba5d1ea895.jpg)  
REI's sixty-five foot climbing structure offers an experience on-line retailers can only envy.

NikeTown, Warner Brothers Studio stores, and Disney Storcs sell themed Experienccs with a capital "E." Each NikeTown celebrates sports, with roughly half the retail space devoted to tapcd sporting events, memorabilia, and displays. Disney Stores calls its employees "cast members" and makes thcm part of thc studio-lot experience, while Warncr Brothers shoots for movie-set ambience. At the Levi's store in San Francisco, a futuristic machine scans your body with laser beams to measure for custom-fit jeans; more exhibitionist shoppers can soak in a public tub to shrink their jeans to fit. The REI flagship store in downtown Seattle features a 65-foot-high climbing "mountain," a 470-foot-long hiking trail, thirty pitched tents, a Rain Room to check whether the gear is truly waterproof—even a dirty pool for campers to test water purifiers. Aisles are made to resemble hiking trails, and door handles are made of ice axes. It's a dazzling mix of entertainment and education. Moreover, the staff members are themselves mountain climbers, cyclists, and kayakers, able to dispense expert advice to customers. And with 1.5 million visitors in its first year, the store earned its own designation as a National Park Service information station.

It's easy to understand why mass-market retailers would create such big stages for their products. But what about the hundreds of selling experiences that take place in corporate America every day?

A few years back, Steelcase asked us to put our understanding of designed experiences to work on a big stage—the heart of Manhattan. Steelcase, like the nation's other major office furniture makers, had long had a huge showroom at the International Design Center in New York. But when the spot lost its cachet, the company started looking for a new showroom and focus.

When Steelcasc executives started working with our environments group, the success of NikeTown-stylc expcriences was fresh in their minds. Steclcase was looking for a way to improve sales, of course, but it also wanted to demonstrate its expcrtise and knowlcdge of workplace practices. Contract furniture companies have gencrally taken a traditional approach to selling their wares. They usually had a display showroom out front and a back-of-the-house office where the staff worked. Buyers would be shown the furniture, led into a conference room, handed a refreshment, weighed down with mounds of literature, and sent on their way.

After intensive observations of Steelcase's customers, the team came back with what we called an "experience map," breaking down the range of buyers visiting the showroom and the potential points of contact. Right off the bat, we identified one of the great failings of most showrooms and corporate offices. Nobody likes to be kept waiting in the downstairs lobby only to be sent upstairs to wait once again. At Steelcase's New York Work/Life Center, waiting was eliminated. Buyers were immediately escorted to a comfortable seat in the elegant café and served refreshments, fruit, and even lunch, while enjoying one of the best views in New York, overlooking Central Park. Visitors could then take a trip throughout the space. The team created starter kits for buyers, complete with background documents and postcards that captured the flavor of the furniture. Even the elevator told a story. On each floor was a different element of a vertical time line dating to the 1920s, reinforcing Steelcase's long record of success. Polaroid cameras were provided so that you could photograph a particular piece of furniture or installation to create your own document.

While not a theme park per se, there were ample opportunities for interaction. "Sandboxes" invited buyers to try a chair or desk on for size, with fabrics and materials arranged for easy viewing. "Product pods" showed sample work settings, offering the interplay of infrastructure, technology, and ergonomics. Laserdisc case studies, meanwhile, showed real office scenarios. But the biggest change may be how the Work/Life Center brings the back-of-the-house functions front and center. The workplaces of the salespeople and support staff became an integral part of a visitor's journey, making it possible for buyers to see the furniture in use. And after the tour, the buyer could return to the café and enjoy a snack with the salesperson.

In bottom-line terms the space has worked—sales are up dramatically, and the average buyer spends more time in the Work/Life Center tha  l xc hah a more vital workplace—the Steelcase employees in the building also hn y of the showroom.

## LEARNING FROM VEGAS

You can't desg better expeences by stayin in theoffice. You'e ot to walk designed experiences with your own two feet and see them with your own eyes. There may be no better place to observe how effective a designed experience can be than in modern-day Las Vegas. If you haven't been recently, I highly recommend a trip—for business, of course. I'm dead serious. No matter what your field or industry, whether you sell retail or business-to-business, you can learn a lot from Las Vegas.

The original Vegas casinos made you trudge by hundreds of slot machines and gaming tables to get to your hotel room. It was not happenstance that there was no place for the weary to sit except at a blackjack table or a slot machine. Vegas abolished the outdoors and day-to-day reality. Without windows or clocks, casinos made it almost seem natural for time, and your money, to fly. Search for a water fountain to dilute the alcohol-induced haze, and you'd come up empty. Even the underpriced rooms and budget buffets were all part of the equation, a sucker's bait and switch.

The old Vegas crumbled, of course. Treating people more like cattle than humans usually fails in the end. What Vegas did was to reinvent itself, and today it's the ultimate international virtual reality. In one afternoon you can dine in the Eiffel Tower, float down the canals of Venice, surf six-footers in the South Pacific, and walk the streets of 1930s Manhattan. The old objectives of keeping people awake (and gambling) are now blended with opportunities for shopping and fine dining. Caesar's Forum pioneered the widely copied faux ceiling that mimics the rise and fall of the sun over and over again long into the night, and much like natural light, keeps you going into the wee hours.

The latest creations—such as the Venetian, Paris, or Bellagio—do a good enough job of approximating the experience of visiting European cities that many Americans who don't know or can't afford the real thing consider it a reasonable facsimile. Steve Wynn's Bellagio, modeled after the town on Italy's Lake Como, may be the largest, most elaborately designed luxury environment in the world. Seven celebrity chefs have lent their names (and sometimes their talent) to the Bellagio dining experience. The elegant boutiques feature only the ultrapremium brands, and the art museum includes portraits by Cézanne, van Gogh, and Picasso.

The Vegas experience is still about "going over the top," but it's no longer only about outlandish neon and flashy dinner shows. Real Italian cypress trees and Aleppo pines line the Bellagio's pool. The French may turn their noses up at "Paris," but it's not hard to imagine you are in France when you are there. Most of the casino's four thousand employees speak a little French, and if the baguettes taste authentic, its because the oven was shipped from the homeland. Even the paint used on the half-scale replica of the Eiffel Tower is identical to that used on the original.

## MAKE THE HUMAN CONNECTION

So what can Vegas teach us about other business "experiences"? There's nearly always more opportunity to create excitement than you think, whatever it is that you make or sell. Central Market, the Austin grocery store, for example, has turned shopping into a trip to a "foodie"

amusement park. Central Market's 63,000-square-foot space offers a Vegas-proportioned experience. Customers will find two hundred varieti of olive oil, fivehundred cheeses, and thirty kinds of sausage; the bucalonshal eg  askebal cut k from the monotony of standard aisles, Central Market has boldly created a yellow brick road-like path that winds through the store, creating more shelf space and giving customers a grander view of its bountiful produce, diverse foods, and lavish displays. Every kid gets a balloon, and official "foodies" offer free samples and are given carte blanche to open up any package for sale and offer a taste. Cooking students "Shop the Market," gathering exotic goodies and whipping up gourmet meals on-site. And when you order at the café, you receive a pager that brightens when your meal is ready.

If you're thinking food has nothing to do with your business, you're probably wrong. When was the last time you had lunch catered into a conference room for a client? Remember how their eyes rolled when you offered cellophane-wrapped sandwiches that looked and tasted like they came out of a vending machine? When we order lunches for our staff and clients for brainstorms and other meetings, we try to make it fun and delicious. It can get you off on the right foot with a client.

Bon Appetit, our favorite caterer, does a great job of changing the experience of a box lunch by redesigning the box. For one special event, they packed a "Nordstrom picnic," with each beautifully presented meal in its own silver-and-gold-colored Nordstrom's gift box. Another time our sushi lunch arrived in a flower box suitable for long-stem roses, with a long plastic window to show off the food.

We all crave good experiences. It's one reason "experience gifts" are on the rise, with people giving everything from wine-tasting classes to tennis lessons or a weekend at the spa. Companies have gotten better at giving experience gifts to employees as well, from the sales conference in Hawaii to the off-site at a resort or a team trip to a sports event.

By turning your product or service into an experience, you just might find a market you didn't know existed. The Hank Haney Golf complexes in the Dallas arca used to see rcvenues slump every December. Then they started advertising thcir gift certificates for golf lessons and driving range expericnces on local billboards. Suddenly, that new business climbed to over 50 percent of thcir holiday revenues and put December in the black. Customers havc the chance to give an experience gift, and the golf courses get to remove a dip from their seasonal revenue curve.

Designing new experiences is usually about figuring out a way to connect with people. There was a time, for example, when all the travel industry offered was air travel, hotels, and rcntal cars. Now travel agencies offer adventure travel packages, eco-travel, art travel, and literary travel. If you've got a special interest, there's undoubtedly a travel company that offers a travel "experience" for you in some corner of the world. And the adventuresome companies guiding people through these experiences arc rcaping great rewards. Though tourism plods along with less than 5 perccnt annual growth, nature and adventure travel is up by 30 percent a year. Who is responding to these new experiences? Millions of people seeking authentic or meaningful experiences. Consider how radically eco-tourism has redesigned the scope of travel. Other than joining the Peace Corps, you probably can't get a much more meaningful travel expericnce than eco-tourism, vacations designed to help sustain the environment and local inhabitants.

By adding a little spice to what you produce or sell, you may have people lining up as if they were tourists going whale watching. The Pike Place Fish Market in Seattle is a good example. How can one breathe new life into the traditional experience of buying fish? Well, Pike Place Market did it in spades. You point to the fish you want, and then, as the fishmongers start hollering back and forth, one of them winds up and pitches your shining tcn-pound salmon twventy feet or so to another guy holding a makeshift catcher's mitt fashioned out of wads of newspaper. Thcy ncatly wrap it in an icc pack for your drive or flight home. It works! It's a dazzling pcrformance—there's nothing like flying fish to draw a crowd. On the surface, nothing could be more mundane than selling fish, and yet Pike Place Market has designed it into an experience so compelling that Charthouse Learning actually wrote a book—and best-selling video—about it.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/3ecc7f343597477c656fa0bb1cf4be303109c89a3ac15f2f516e0287064100cd.jpg)  
Even buying fish can be turned into an entertaining event.

Think about the process involved in making or delivering your company's product or service. There's always an element of the experience you can bring to life. People have been making tortillas in Mexican restaurants for ages. But the Fresh Mex rcstaurant chain Chevys realized that if they brought the tortilla wheel—dubbed "El Machino" out of the kitchen, customers would be entertained by watching them being made, all thc whilc reinforcing the fact that they are getting frcsh tortillas.

Can you put a new spin on the "tortillas" of your office? How could you turn your invoice into a fun experience?

At IDEO, our hands-on front lobby displays turn waiting into an entertaining experience.

How important is a good experience? Just look at Kinko's—they gave telecommuters, freelancers, and other mobile businesspeople the resources of a big corporation right in the local mall. All sorts of business functions previously available separately—custom printing, computer services, even meeting rooms and videoconferencing—wvere suddenly brought together under one roof. The timing was impeccable, coinciding with the estimated 40 million Americans who now work out of their homes, the rise of desktop publishing, and the Internet. To the "free agent nation" of entrepreneurs and telecommuters, Kinko's is the satellite office, offering the latest technology and services. Proof of the success of this designed experience is in the pudding. Customers now spend hours computing, meeting informally, or surfing the Net at Kinko's stores—and, of course, making copies too.

## TELL A STORY

The next time you travel, try slipping off the beaten path. I believe in learning from both ends of the spectrum of the designed experiencefrom the Vegas and Disney side of the equation as well as the local dive. There's plenty to glcan from one-of-a-kind spots. Often success comes from taking a decidedly low-kcy approach. We all know and love or loal breakast dive with its iportins, lots  noise, and taketor-leave-it waitresses. We wait outside in the outragcous linc becausc it's a good place to read the paper and soak in the atmosphere. Part of the appeal is that we feel such spots are "authentic"; we'd ncver queue up so long to dine at a chain restaurant.

The success of the Martha Stewart empirc is largely about her savvy understanding of our desire for authentic experiences. Whether she is offering an illusion or not isn't the point. Stewart's hard-to-master ca c t etpift era with its own values and sense of time.

Well-designed experiences strike a nerve with the public. Take the sto  he opuar Restratn Harachai. It bean, good companies do, with someone trying to solve what seemed like a little problem. Founder and CEO Stephen Gordon was trying to open abed-and-breakfast. But he soon found it a challenge to locate the period hardvare and authentic lighting fixtures he needed to restore his Victorian inn. As he gradually cultivated national sources, friends and neighbors began asking how they could enjoy his new pipeline. Gordon wisely scratched the B&B and in its place hatched Restoration Hardware.

Just as Vegas has created a discount trip to pseudo-Europe, Restoration Hardware offers the serendipity of searching through an estate sale without the risk. Entering a typical store's "great" room, you wander from section to section, finding Art Deco-inspired chairs, Victorian glass doorknobs, Le Zig-Zag corkscrews, and even vintage toys. Except that everything is new. The store's natural lighting, architectural detail, and fine cabinetry underscore its focus on quality and craftsmanship. Surprise is key. Gordon loves juxtaposing ten-dollar novelty items with two-thousand-dollar leather chairs; return the following week and you'll likely find a new eclectic treasure. Most of all, Gordon tells personal stories about things. He approvcs cvery one of his store's more than 4,500 items, introducing them with placards carrying his tales of the worthiness of the offerings, often laccd with his personal memories.

Restoration Hardware may be as much about a chance to buy a piece of the traditional good life in the Internet age as it is about wellcrafted hardware and furnishings. But doesn't Starbucks offer the same momentary "good life" refuge with its careful balance of good coffee, music, and wood-grained ambience? All around us the best stores, shops, and restaurants are drawing us in with "stories," whether romance, nostalgia, or mystery.

If you're scratching your head wondering what this has to do with your business, think again. Does your company host events, introduce products or services, and meet clients in public places? Most companies tell visual stories on the flat, one-dimensional level of a white paper or press release. If you've ever been to a giant trade show in Vegas, you know that certain booths and parties are packed, and others are painfully empty. It helps to have something worthwhile to sell, but selling is also about designing an experience for potential clients and customers.

## FIX IT

We're fortunate to have worked with some of the pioneers in high-tech, legends like Apple and Dell Computer.

Along the way, we'e tried to gleana little bit of what these companies do best. Just a few years ago it was incredibly frustrating to piece together a new computer. Even though I travel with a Dell machine, I still believe nobody can match Apple's out-of-the-box experience. Well-designed screen icons and manuals that are beautiful and almost fun to read make it easier to put together an Apple than the average children's toy. When the iMac came out, I realized the Internetfriendly machine was ideal for my computerphobic parents and my new-to-computing toddlers, so I bought two machines almost immediately.

All sorts of companies could learn from Apple's superior out-of-hebox experience. You have to wonder about all the "easy-to-use" software programs that spawn stores full of 500-page, \$35 books to help you use them.

Dell successfully tackled two other key parts of the buying experience by pioneering the introduction of efficient, direct sales and by making the configuration of memory and peripherals nearly as easy as asking the deli counter to pile on the mustard and hold the mayo. A direct, reliable customization experience became Dells strongest selling point, a competitive advantage that helped to make it an industry powerhouse.

Lexus, of course, has fixed all sorts of things that are broken, but being forgetful, I'm most grateful for one of its simplest, redesigned experiences. The experience of locking your key in your car. Leave the key in the ignition of a Lexus and lock the door, and the doors will shut for an instant and then release. If you somehow miss that safety feature, they also give you a credit-card-sized key to keep in your purse or wallet. Lexus knows you're not keen on the experience of locking yourself out.

When we redesigned the shopping cart for Nightline, we began our research by investigating shoppers' experiences with the traditional cart, everything from how people shop to how they navigate the aisles. By fostering "hub shopping"—carts with removable baskets—we tried to change the way shoppers experience the store. Our objective was to fix a broken experience that has barely changed in half a century.

## SAVE A LIFE, TAKE A JOURNEY

In some cases, fixing broken experiences can mean the difference between life or death. That's why we designed a defibrillator for Heartstream to be as easy to use as a fire extinguisher. It's portable, features childlike 1-2-3 screen instructions, and has physical and visual clues to make its use nearly automatic. Since its introduction in 1999 dozens of lives have been saved with the device.

Designing new experiences is about striking a balance. Toss in too many new features, and it's like a movie that's too long or has too many characters. Medtronic's first redesign of its temporary pacemaker boasted impressive advanced features. But to run it properly, you needed a one-hour training seminar and a half-inch-thick manual. Each button had multiple functions in order to pack in lots of options.

Medtronic quickly realized the new pacemaker wasn't selling as well as it might, and asked us to rethink the process. We weren't content with the numerous interviews with doctors that Medtronic had conducted. We observed doctors and nurses at work. We noticed that, 80 percent of the time, the medical staff was using just a few basic functions. So we came up with three dial knobs with strong visual graphics showing default settings. Probably our biggest contribution was a layerd menu approach. Cardiologists could still use deeper layers of the device, but the typical caregiver was no longer confused by too many complex capabilities. The best products and services aspire to the classic design principle "Make simple things simple and complex things possible." Sometimes designing a winning experience is about reining in your wish list and resisting the temptation to do too much.

## RETHINKING SERVICES

Talk about broken experiences. Think of the typical hospital room, not the place most people are eager to check into. Precisely why Wellness

LLC, a Nashville-based company that designs and installs modular hospital patient rooms, is studying ways to make its rooms promote healing, adapt to the latest technological innovations, and increase staff efficiency.

Welln and maintain and have been described as being more "holistic," featuring rounded surfaces and calming colors. The company even claims that the epeienc oinstalling thers take le than hal the tme of conventional construction.

Established industries are ripe for a little redesigned experience. Nothing is too big or too small to benefit from a fresh perspective. Imagine how many service companies could use a new lease on life. The hotel industry is a prime example of how broken an industry can be. It also illustrates how quickly leaders can churn out newly designed experiences. Years ago, for example, the Mirage casino developed an airportlike computer-controlled hotel baggage system (aided by teams of bellboys) that can ferry your bag to your room almost as fast as you can get there yourself (while saving you the time and embarrassment of lugging your baggage through the casino). Some Las Vegas hotels, undoubtedly trying to increase the time you have to recoup your losses, have made it possible to check in with your departing flight before you leave the casino. Ten years ago it might have sounded far-fetched to think you could pay for your hotel bill through the in-room TV. But how far off can the day be when most hotels will give you the option of checking in electronically with a swipe of your credit card at a lobby kiosk and speeding straight to your guest room?

Look at how successful hotels have gone cyber, beefing up rooms and business centers with computers, Internet service, and e-mail. Luxury hotels, of course, live or die on how well they cater their services to the needs and desires of their guests. Consider these whimsical offerings by the Park Hyatt Los Angeles: the "movie turndown," with popcorn, candy, soda, and, of course, choice of movies; the "picnic in the park," with the usual goodies in a basket; and the "bath turndown," complete with bath salts, rubber ducky, and loofah.

## LITTLE EXPERIENCES COUNT

What can we learn from studying badly designed experiences? Look around your own workplace or business for routines that people hate, established ways of doing business that burden your company and/or clients and customers. Little improvements matter. Do people enjoy returning something on a warranty? I sincerely doubt it. That's why when a customer returns a backpack for repair to JanSport, the company mails out a wacky response. "Hi! Warranty Service Camp is really cool. They say they're sending me home soon," reads a typical postcard. "Gotta run . . . we're doing zipper races today!" Silly as these cards may sound, JanSport reports that instead of getting mostly angry customer letters, nearly all the correspondence now consists of cheery thank-you notes.

IDEO may lack a warranty service, but like a lot of companies, we get our share of résumés from folks looking for employment. The old way of dealing with the tide of résumés made little sense. The countless people we didn't have a place for or who didn't fit our requirements were mailed dreary attorney-approved rejection letters about a month after they applied. But the more we thought about this, the more we realized that we were typecasting ourselves as the bearer of bad news, not exactly the best course to create good buzz (or karma, for that matter). Why not change the model and our image? We adopted a policy of promptly mailing colorful postcards, letting people know we'd gotten their résumés and appreciated their thinking of IDEO. The tongue-in-cheek card begins with "Thanks, we received your." and then gives a list of items with checks marked off next to what we'd been sent-letter, résumé, portfolio, or even their parking ticket.

But it was srius too, an it et expectatins or jo seekers. "We review all employment inquiries very closely. If there's an immediate match betwveen your talents and our needs, we will contact you directly within four weeks. (It can take somc timc for a résumé to make the rounds of all the people who necd to see it.)"

We sor  t No y as - ter unnecessary, but we also included some wording to let people down y "If thee s ot at ths te we wil ke y éé on honestly. we will We have several valued employees who applied to work here more than once! Please let us know any significant changes in your experience or qualifications.'

To me, this is a designed experience with a "small d," the sort that anybody (with an open mind) can bring to their group, a division, or company.

"Small d" designed experiences don't have to be about your primary products or services. Take company security. Few would disagree that it's notoriously bad and uninspired at most firms. You sign a book and stick on a badge. I've gotten interesting and occasionally valuable information by seeing who signed in today and who else my client is considering. It's not hard to imagine a scenario in which someone could learn a lot simply by flipping through that sign-in book. In the process of protecting their assets, companies sometimes compromise their secrets.

There's also a glaring contrast between the typical, nicely laminated employee badges that most companies issue, and the cheap paper ones that visitors get—even executives with whom you're hoping to do millions of dollars of business.

None of this struck us as secure or smart business. So we brainstormed a new model, to treat visitors as travelers that need a passport or tourist visa. And so "the IDEO Tour" badge, featuring a tourist wearing a Hawaiian shirt and lci with the Statue of Liberty, Big Ben, and the Golden Gate Bridge in the background, was born. We snapped tiny Polaroids of visitors and glued the head shots inside this fun tourist frame to personalize the badge. On the back we printed a colorful map of the IDEO offices. Visitors wore it like a necklace with a nice metal clip.

OK, I admit it—sometimes we sound like kids that haven't grown up, making up games on a summer afternoon. It's easy to stop yourself and feel a tinge of guilt as you wonder whether you should be having so much fun at work. But try resisting the urge to stop. Who knows? You might just find that spirit of fun will push you toward designing a great new experience.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/7dc67241379d0220d4060a90441472ed8efe8e7512e05cd3a658681cfcaaaaa5.jpg)

peed counts. It gets products to market in time for the holiday shopping season. It leapfrogs established players before they can get of te rond. It iplants your bran in conmehearts and mins before your competitors have left the starting gate.

You need great teams to make innovation second nature in your company. And great space. But even if you've got the right people and place, innovation is anything but automatic. There's still the element of speed. You might boast bountiful brainstormers and make strong prototypes, but if you miss all your deadlines and ship dates, everyhing you've worked toward will be for naught. It's not enough to master the basics. You must master them in prime time, under the stress of competition, under the glare of a client losing patience. We get to practice this every day, but even that's not enough. Though we're very good at working fast, sometimes we need a refresher course. A race to see if we're as good and fast as we'd like to be. It's an essential element to keep a team or a company in tune. Here's the story of one of our races. Perhaps there's a competition that might keep your company "battleready."

Over the last few years, the Sand Hill Challenge has become our Indy 500, Kentucky Derby, and America's Cup rolled into one. It's a soapbox derby put on by Silicon Valley venture capitalists to raise money for a charity called Safe Rides, which offers kids a safe, anonymous ride home if they've been drinking or hanging out with other kids who might not be sober. Dave Lyons, then head of our machine shop, got us excited about the first Sand Hill Challenge, which took place in September 1997. It seemed a no-losc proposition. The cause was a good one, and what better way to challenge our prototyping machine than to build a gravity-powered, aerodynamic race car.

Our backer was the venture capital firm of Mohr Davidow, who figured if we could design laptops and computer mice, why not a soapbox speedster? We've always believed that our experience and innovation methods make us a quick study. But therc was something else here. The racc gavc us a chance to test ourselves against some formidable competition.

## PLAY IMITATING WORK

Lyons pooled the efforts of IDEO volunteers, held some quick brainstormers, and within a couple of weeks our first prototype was barreling down the hill. When you're short on time, it's critical to make your dumb mistakes right away. After cobbling together a couple of threewheelers, we finally got around to reading the rule book and realized we were a wheel short. Our early cars were pretty crude (many were thrown together out of old bike parts) and not terribly safe. Nearly everybody was afraid to test-drive, so Lyons volunteered, but even he got a bit queasy when he topped thirty-five miles an hour in the bike lane and started wobbling toward traffic on not much more than a sheet of plywood with wheels.

This soapbox dcrby was more challenging than most because each team was allowed a couple of pushers. Our lead muscle was former San Francisco 49ers running back Roger Craig. Each Saturday, the team would meet at a parking lot by Sand Hill Road at daybreak. Why so carly? The racccourse ran through a major traffic light, and the chances of a collision were lowest in the early morning. Craig and another ex-football player, Scott Davies, gave the car a good running heave, and Lyons would cross his fingers and hope for the best. Four IDEOers took command of the intersection, stopping traffic for the flying fifty-pound soapbox and its intrepid test pilot. The team would race for hours, making adjustments and checking results. Sometimes the cops would tell them to pack up and go home; somctimes they'd just tell them to be safe and stay in thc bikc lane.

Every element of the car was rigorously prototypcd and tested.

Nothing was as simple as it seemed. Take pushing the car. Because of our car's teardrop shape, there was nowhere to push. So in a back alley behind our shop the team tested all sorts of approaches. They'd brainstorm for half an hour and build the concepts an hour later. They must have made cight or nine pushing mechanisms, with the best solution boasting nearly as much design as the car. The design evolved into a T-shaped frame with Rollerblade wheels that docked to the back of the car but stayed behind as the car sped away.

At last, the race drew near. The entrants arrived the day before the contest like kids at the beach, showing off their muscles. By this time, we had tested cars that steered in the front as wvell as the back, cars that were heavy, cars that were aerodynamic, and cars that were light. We found some great wheels by studying the buggy race, a similar contest held at David's undergraduate alma mater, Carnegie Mellon. A couple of IDEO Carnegie Mellon alums described how they used wheelchair racing wheels, and we tracked down a set from a small catalog company that specializes in sports equipment for athletes with disabilities. But the more cars we built, the more we discovered tradeoffs. The heavy cars, for instance, reached the highest speeds, but they accelerated more slowly.

Another successful venture capitalist had hired a talented engineer and race car builder, known for building antique race cars for the Laguna Seca races. The car arrived on a shiny race car trailer. It was huge, a retrofitted Formula race car, stuffed with stacks of weights. IDEO's entry wasn't nearly so impressive. Ours arrived on a rusty old flatbed trailer, the car little more than a sheet of plywood sheathed in a teardrop-shaped carbon fiber shell. The pre-race trash talking started ramping up. The celebrated car designer ambled over and informed Lyons he'd obviously failed physics. Lyons didn't know what to say.

"You haven't done the numbers," Racing Man lectured. "The heavy car is going to win big," he said. He boasted that his car had clocked sixty-five going down the hill. Lyons swallowed hard. IDEO's car hadn't done more than forty miles an hour. He worried that maybe the race ias over.

The next morning some twenty thousand fans lined the course. It was tense right from the start. The temperature was close to a hundred, and to increase the aerodynamics, we literally taped the bubble over the driver, who lay prone, just inches from the ground. We were afraid our driver—IDEO engineer Amy Han—might pass out with the long wait between heats. So as we waited for the starting gun, we stood over the car with a couple of reflecting space blankets, blocking the sun. And then suddenly it was our turn. Roger Craig and Scott Davies gave the car a mighty heave, the T-bar fell back like a rocket ship's propulsion unit, and IDEO's lightweight plywood and carbon fiber car leaped off the line.

Meanwhile, the converted Formula race car started like a tortoise. Oh, it was pushing sixty at the finish—but IDEO won going away, beating it by a dozen car lengths. Even better, the Sand Hill Challenge raised \$100,000 for the Safe Rides program. That's about as good as it gets. Play and adventure imitating work—all in the name of a good cause.

To me, the Sand Hill Challenge is not just a great story of teamwork but even more a lesson in the value of quick prototyping. Racing Man may have mastered his physics, but he didn't spend enough Saturday mornings testing to learn that when it comes to soapbox derbies, there's more than one way to get to the finish line first.

## ONE-HIT WONDERS

History is full of one-hit wonders, and the next year we knew it wasn't going to be easy to defend our title. NASA, Lockheed, and the designer of the Voyager—the plane that circled the globe without refueling—were building cars. How could we compete against such a stellar group? We started the way we often launch an IDEO project. We don't pretend to know all the answers on day one. We looked for help and expertise.

We knew from the first race that wheels were the biggest factor. We did some research and learned that Michelin had built spccial wheels for solar race cars that greatly reduced resistance. A couple of guys from the Stanford Solar Car team helped us find these tires. The real challenge turned out to be designing and building a rim for them. With a little more detective work we located a document that drew the profile of the rim, and worked with a nearby company that makes cooking woks (honestly) to turn out custom-spun aluminum rims.

Next, we tackled the human equation. Roger Craig and Scott Davies were great football players, but they weren't necessarily the best race car pushers. Who would be among the best in the world?

With a lot of luck and some help from our partners at Mohr Davidow, we met Darrin and Dan Steele, fresh from their experience with the U.S. Olympic bobsled team in Nagano, Japan. The Steele twins, bobsledders by winter, decathletes by summer, were big and strong and excited at the prospect of pushing and helping to design the car.

We were playing with some intriguing new ideas. Certainly, the trash-talking race car designer's idea that a heavy car would go faster was right—but only if you could somehow get off the line fast. Could we combine the best of both designs—a light car at the start and a heavy car after letting go?

Bobsledders were the answer, of course. That's what they excel at, pushing and then hopping on the car. So we built a car to do a controlled experiment. We outfitted the car with an accelerometer and visited a guy who owned a go-cart factory. The project was contagious and ridiculously fun. The go-cart guy started showing up several nights a week, offering ideas and outfitting our car with advanced Grand Prix data-logging instrumentation.

But our test surprised everyone. When the bobsledders leaped into the car, it actually made it slower than our one-person version. The difference? When they hopped aboard, the energy of that last benehpress shove was lost.

So how eould we capture the best of both—the last heave and the added speed of the heavier car? We tried having one of the twins hop on board while the other shoved him down the hill. Unfortunately, the slim car wasn't terribly stable. There was a minor crash and some serious road rash. The U.S. Bobsledding Assoeiation—-and the sledders' girlfriends—advised us to return to our one-driver design.

Small crashes sometimes happen when you're trying to innovate. It's part of the territory. We've had this happen a hundred times with major produets, a feature or design that wasn't quite ready for prime time. We didn't despair. We just kept plowing ahead with our one-driver design. The final race was elose, but in the end, we edged out a team of master solar-car builders from U.C. Berkeley.

In two short years the Sand Hill Challenge had become an IDEO tradition, a matter of pride, and a tremendous creative outlet for many of our top people. But after winning two years running it didn't make sense to build another speedy car. Routine is the enemy of innovation. Do the same thing the same way too many times, and it's bound to become routine. It's why smart companies switeh their sponsorships of athletic events and change the theme of their retreats. So in 1999 we entered a beautiful red Chinese dragon mounted on three engineless motorcycles, and won the "whimsical" eategory.

Not everyone had learned their lessons about the importanee of prototyping. Another prominent Silicon Valley firm built a ear with four wheels all in a line like a giant Rollerblade. They designed the ear on the premise that aerodynamics were eritical. But we knew from our Saturday morning tests that friction drag in the wheel was what eounted. The cars didn't go fast enough for aerodynamies to be a big factor.

Nonetheless, theegfru ingindely aerodynamic car, much as we've seen companies prematurely rush eonsumer products into manufacturing. They spent hundreds of hours on it but apparently didn't leave time for a single test run.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/b92796bc4ab38881b87c08c5ff8d0fb357cb180fae3a4ff57e3dbfee0b92a3dc.jpg)  
off-beat competitions can inspire teams. The team had to rush to get its whimsical red dragon ready for race day.

Come race day, the announcer was trumpeting how this worldfamous firm had built such a sleek, amazing vehicle with a unique inline wheel design. Like a great concept car, it was something to behold. The gun went off, the stylish soapbox looked absolutcly marvelous for a few seconds, then wobbled and rolled onto its side, skidding to a stop.

The announcer deadpanncd that he didn't think he'd ask them to design a car for him anytime soon.

## GETTING THERE FASTER

Over the years we've found that entering the Sand Hill Challenge and other competitions have become a critical part of reinvigorating the IDEO Way.

Innovation doesn't happen in a vacuum. The faster you can come up with improvements or genuinely new products, the more likely you'll succeed in the marketplace. But you don't simply order up speed. And the path to superior innovation is rarely a direct line from A to B.

Look for competitions that you might enter (or create) for your teams that emphasize the playful, speedy side of innovation.

You just might get to the finish line faster.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/e794865a7e107405802ffa6ff3f9f0d652c87ad1b1380015b9106ca2ce8039bb.jpg)

|aking decisions about innovation means taking risks. Every executive and tcam leader faces this reality. For Silicon Graphics CEO Ed McCracken, it was a choice between staying with the status quoor breaking away from the pack. He told us loud and clear that his high-end graphics computers didn't perform like the beige computers made by everyone else. Why should they look like them?

IDEO's designers and Silicon Graphics' engineers realized the challenge was to create a palette of colors that would surprise but not shock. So was born the world's first purple computer. Silicon Graphics went on to grab the public's attention wvith its distinctive "Indigo" machine. Green, red, and blue iterations followed, making Silicon Graphics synonymous with bold color and irreverent playfulness.

Sound a little like the colorfully translucent iMac? When the iMac debuted in "Bondi blue" in 1998 and began appearing in different fruit flavors the followving year, the idea seemed as novel and appropriate as Silicon Graphics first splash of color almost a decade earlier. There's plenty more to the ilac story, of course, but the machine's bold shape and color undeniably helped fuel its success.

Risk takers. How can you know when and where to take the plunge? Sticking with the status quo alvays seems safer, even though it loses in the long run. Risk taking happens on the microlevel throughout a project. It's the big risks that we're unsure of. The ones that define what your product will and won't be.

Think of the countless Web ventures that havc sprouted up over the last scveral years. The best have invariably been cntirely new companies diving into thc unknown. Grab nearly any slicc of the Web, and you'll find a company taking a risk—Hotmail gambling that free e-mail could scll, Amazon trying to sell something as bulky and old-fashioned as books, iWon.com trying to grow rich in the long run by giving away millions of dollars to its customers in the short run. Now that the anxiety about using credit card numbers on the Internet has diminished a little, we may tend to forget that virtually all of the early business-toconsumer e-commerce sites took the risk that fearful customers would stay away in droves.

## FAIL YOUR WAY TO SUCCESS

We've got a saying around IDEO: "Fail often to succeed sooner." Failure is the flip side of risk taking, and if you don't risk, odds are you won't succeed. It's a lesson we've learned the hard way, and it's one we've seen with countless clients.

Good companies embrace a culture of mini-failures. This goes to the heart of our prototyping method. By trying out features or capabilities in rough form as fast as possible, you've got a better chance of honing in on the critical fundamentals when the ship date rolls around. Nobody worth their salt hasn't failed on a big stage. Take enough chances and you risk a few big failures. Charles Schwab, for instance, failed a number of times before hitting on a successful formula for e-Schwab. Indeed, you could argue that the story of his business has been a series of risks, failures, corrections—and hits. Schwab himself claims that his company has made so many mistakes that they have coined the term "noble failures." Here are just a few of his company's screwups.

Pocketerm, a handheld stock-quote device, was overly expensive and bug-infested. Schwabline, a system that would provide paper copies of people's portfolios, had a bad habit of breaking down and could only provide information a day old. And Schwab Quotes struggled to make real-time quotes available for customers with a touchtone trading system but was very complicated. Other Schwab systems or software failed because they were too cumbersome or buggy.

What's the lesson? All these "noble failures" kept Schwab on the cutting edge. By the mid-1990s as the Internet was heating up, the company was ideally positioned to take its biggest and most successful risk, one I'l talk about later in the chapter.

"The noble failures always kept us pushing the barriers," says Schwab. "We kept believing that a mistake may not be a mistake when i ee ally

Failure helps teach risk takers which rules can be broken and which are there to teach you a hard lesson. We've seen our share of failures. In the early 1980s, for instance, we decided to introduce a computer accessory, a clever monitor stand for the Apple II. Our product quality was good, our features strong, the price under forty dollars. Things were looking very good, except for one problem. Just as we went to market, Apple abruptly changed its mind and decided to create its own stripped-down version, which it shipped with every machine—for free. Free is pretty hard to compete with. What did we learn? Never ignore the worst-case scenario, in this case someone creating a business model that undercuts your pricing or renders your product unmarketable.

Neophytes tend to become infatuated with their product or business plans, and we were no exception. One of our biggest early lessons was an Italian love affair, a beautiful design that was a commercial failure of operatic proportions. In the early days of our firm, David got a chance to meet the revered Italian designer Ettore Sottsass. They really hit it off and decided to combine Sottsass's Italian styling with our Silicon Valley engineering in an electronic consumer product. The playful cubist red, black, and yellow Enorme phone created jointly with Sottsass Associati was a runaway aesthetic and cultural success. The phone entered the permanent collection of New York's Museum of Modern Art, appeared in movies, and was featured on the cover of European design magazines.

Sottsass came through with a striking design. How hard could the logistics of basic telephony be? In retrospect, trickier than we thought. Inexperienced at the bargaining table, we signed up an unproven manufacturer in Hong Kong and entered into an exclusive distribution agreement through a tiny, high-margin channel. We discovered that

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/93a7aab7908849821a84ad6760b4159347498e7692b0b6ac774f085034a93e44.jpg)

The Enorme phone was a joyful failure; today it's in the Museum of Modern Art.

the Enorme phone we made in Hong Kong for \$25 would cost \$150 at Nah is. It  ha that same time, Time magazine started giving phones away with every subscription.

The Enorme telephone was one of IDEO's biggest and most joyful failurs. I argue hat evey cmpany  individual needsat leason. Sometimes you've got to take risks you don't understand, and maybe even lose some money if you want to gain buckets of experience.

So how can you survive your joyful failures? I think juggling is the perfect metaphor for intelligently embracing risk taking. Learning to ju can help give you theright mindst. Face it, we'e all oi to drop the ball once in a while. As I learned from my fellow Palo Altan John Cassidy, author of Juggling for the Complete Klutz, the trick to beginning juggling is not to use rocks, which may bruise your feet, or balls, which tend to roll under the couch. Beanbags are ideal for learning to juggle (and Klutz Press ships three with each book) because they put such a small price on failure. If you start with tennis balls, they'll quickly roll away, and you'll spend so much time scampering after your errant juggles that you'll likely quit after your first few tries. Beanbags encourage you to keep juggling until you get good enough at it to move up to balls—or knives, for that matter.

In the busness world, of course, you can "juggle beanbags" by doing lots of prototyping in inexpensive, easily malleable materials, by running ideas and rough presentations by bosses or clients early on in stages. That way when prime time approaches and the real deadline looms, you'll be confident enough to juggle knives.

## GROWN MEN AFRAID OF A LITTLE MOUSE

Sitting on the sidelines, waiting for the "riskless breakthrough" or the "ideal risk" to come along, doesn't work. Those who try to scientifically measure opportunities sometimes miss the boat. For example, in the early 1980s we discovered that the computer mouse no longer really needed its electrical "tail." Pop in some Nicad batteries, add an infrared transmitter, and you've got a cordless "mouse on the go."

We proposed the innovation to a client at a very large corporation. He loved it—at first—but the next day, fear set in. We've seen it before. Everyone fears taking risks, but at bigger companies the penalty for risk taking is often larger. We weren't that surprised when a couple of weeks later the formerly enthusiastic manager turned timid. "If this fails," he told us, "I'll be known for the rest of my career with the company as the guy with that stupid cordless mouse."

Instead, he took the easy way out. He passed on the innovation and settled for a bland product. A couple of years later we got the chance to create a cordless mouse for veteran Silicon Valley risk taker David Liddle and his dramatic Metaphor workstation. Metaphor got a lot of press attention for its innovative new product and proved that the technology could work successfully.

## MAKING NEW RIDES

Thinking about what you might lose—market share, revenue, a title, your status, your job—makes it nearly impossible to take a leap. That's why the big new ideas usually come from small companies, or big ones who somehow manage to act small. Fear doesn't get you down the mountain.

Take the amazing phenomenon of snowboarding. Plenty of journalists and ski resorts at first called it a fad. During the early ramp-up of snowboarding, Time dubbed it America's "Worst New Sport." Ski resorts tried to ban boarders, and mainstream ski manufacturers figured it was a passing fancy.

But loack sh i e heuDil skiing had been stagnant for years and was starting to lose its momentum as rising ticket prices were keeping families and children off the slopes. Once a symbol of youthful rebelliousness (remember Robert Redford in Downhill Racer?), skiing was becoming dated.

![](../../The art of innovation lessons in creativity from IDEO, Americas leading design firm (Kelley, Tom, 1955-, Littman, Jonathan etc.)/images/14b9a7ba36e8af6d453e5b78fd84eefce2449d9d481ded8c1130946e7dcfb441.jpg)  
Snowboards reinvented the ski industry.

Great time for the dominant downhill ski makers to reinvent the sport, right? Not exactly. Rossignol, Salomon, Fischer, and the other ski powerhouses were too busy protecting market share in their mainstream business to ride the first snowboarding wave.

Meanwhile, upstart Jake Burton Carpenter was breaking the ice so to speak-by hacking out boards in his Vermont garage. He went to his local hardware store—and Austria—to get ideas. In the early days, Burton's toll-free customer service line rang in his bedroom at all hours. He built one of the first boards that could handle ice and hardpack, partly because Vermont's conditions were so icy. While he didn't invent the snowboard (being first isn't always critical), he did popularize thc sport through a combination of quality, persistence, and lobbying that would have done Macintosh evangelists proud.

In retrospect, it's easy to see why manufacturers and ski resorts initially fought the tide. Early boarders had a nasty habit of embodying the worst of male adolescence at high speeds. But Burton's campaign to convince ski resorts to open their mountains to boards gradually took hold. Within a few years, 90 percent of U.S. resorts accepted snowboards, and now over 5 million Americans enjoy the sport. Burton is the world's leading manufacturer of snowboards, and even IDEO has hitched a ride on the sport, designing face-hugging goggles for use on the slopes.

It's the same story when it comes to biking down a mountain, an activity we know firsthand, since IDEO is full of cycling enthusiasts that have helped develop helmets, locks, and sports bottles for the mountain bike industry. Who would you think blew an inside track to "own" mountain biking? Schwinn, one of the biggest bike manufacturers, of course.

Believe it or not, there was a time when the company was ideally positioned to dominate mountain bikes.

Schwinn succeeded wildly in the sixties and seventies. In 1963 one of Schwinn's sales managers noticed California kids souping up bikes with banana seats and Texas longhorn handlebars. A product design manager whipped out a prototype Sting-Ray cycle, and soon the chubby-tired bike was thc company's best seller.

At its zenith, it sold a quarter of all U.S. bicycles. When mountain bikes cmerged, however, Schwinn mistakenly wrote them off as a fad. That dcspite the fact that some of the early mountain bikers—just north of us in Marin County, California—had first tackled the rugged mountain paths with souped-up Schwinns, partly because the bikes were so darned sturdy. Success has a tendency to hamstring companies, to cause them to become complacent and neglect the risk taking that got them to the top. Though Schwinn adeptly picked up on the customer-inspired Sting-Ray trend in the sixties, somehow the company managed to miss out on an analogous mountain biking trend in the eighties.

When the dust settled, of course, mountain bikes proved to be much more than a fad. The public flocked to the fat-tired, easy-to-ride bikes for lots of good reasons. Ten-speed road bikes, formerly the most popular style, were actually built for racers, not recreational use. Thin tires may be fast, but they aren't comfortable, convenient, or especially safe. And, of course, there's the beauty and thrill of riding down a mountain. We all know that some of those mountain bike tires never even touch dirt, let alone steep mountain trails, but mountain bikes are far better suited for the average rider than the old low-slung ten-speed.

Specialized Bicycle Components, for whom we helped design biking accessories, didn't have the problem of resting on its laurels. Originally a niche vendor of high-end bike parts, Specialized quickly capitalized on the mountain bike craze, popularizing the sport with races and clever promotions, while the old U.S. giant, Schwinn, slowly became irrelevant. Today an amazing 70 percent of the full-sized cycles sold in the United States are mountain bikes. Based on its success as a mountain bike maverick, Specialized still uses its in-your-face slogan, "Innovate or Die," which is printed in huge white letters on the long black hearse the company takes to biking events. Not a bad motto to take to heart.

## SPIRITED SKIES

As the quickly changing sports equipment landscape emphasizes, risk taking is a moving target. If you don't take some chances, you get left behind. But does that approach work for every business? What if your industry prides itself on tradition and safety? What about a more staid field like the airline business?

During my years as a management consultant I studied many of the world's airlines and wrote dense reports about how they might improve their operations. I always admired Southwest, not only for its humor but also for its spontaneity. Legend has it that Southwest was launched when CEO Herb Kelleher drew up the airline's business plan on a cocktail napkin. You won't find those boring standard safety lectures required by the FAA on Southwest, where the easygoing flight attendants embellish the instructions with little asides like "secure yourself around the nose and mouth and breathe like you've never breathed before." The jokes don't just get chuckles out of passengers, they get them back on Southwest flights.

To me, that's the best kind of rule to break—an established pattern that does nobody any good. Indeed, even the stodgy FAA chuckles at Southwest's jokes, noting that anything that gets passengers to listen to safety briefings is fine by the agency. Southwest's funny and friendly flight attendants have become as much a part of the Southwest experience as the planes. On a long taxi to the gate, now you almost expect a couple of corny jokes over the intercom.

But cracking funny bones at thirty thousand feet is just one of the ways Southwest bypassed the traditional rules of running an airline. Kelleher brought his intuitive style to what used to be considered an impersonal industry. Southwest is renowned for personal touches, like sending birthday cards to every employee. Kelleher says his only rule is making as few rules as possible, an antimanagement style that seems to resonate with workers: Fortune recently named Southwest as the best company to work for in America. The good vibes have removed barriers that used to exist between classes of workers, with the result that Southwest topped the rankings in safety, price, on-time performance, baggage handling, and customer service. Things happen faster at Southwest becauseworkers don'ge stuc inthinkin that they ca only do things that fit their job description. The gap between tasks has narrowed. Everybody works as a team. Flight attendants (and occasionally even pilots) tidy up before deplaning. Ticket agents carry luggage in a pinch. The focus is on solutions, not who might have slipped up. And the good vibes seem to infect passengers too, who seem more tolerant and friendly than those on more "corporate" airlines.

Not surprisingly, this can-do spirit has led Southwest to upend other stuffy airline routines. For instance, by making seating first-come firstserved instead of assigned, people can actually choose whom they sit next to (and the colored boarding card simplifies boarding for gate agents). Southwest is great at short-circuiting convention. The airline abandoned that aisle-clogging drink cart by taking your order before takeoff. Southwest seems to do well on the Web too, with a simplified, "fewer clicks" approach that has resulted in a high "look-to-book" ratio.

## BEING BIG AND ACTING SMALL

Southwvest may have grown into a major airline, but it hasn't lost its responsive, entrepreneurial culture. Thinking and acting small is what counts. This is true regardless of the company. I'm constantly surprised at how villing some of our large clients are to take calculated gambles.

We've worked a lot with Procter & Gamble over the years. The \$38- billion consumer products powerhouse is widely praised for its marketing muscle and R&D.

But that size was likely to get in the way when Internet opportunity came knocking. So a handful of P&Gers flew to Silicon Valley and got a crash course on the new medium. They teamed up with the venture firm IVP and spun out a separate entity, Reflect.com, free of the normal P&G hierarchy. Reflect.com's business plan seemed radical for a brand-conscious, traditional firm like P&G. The goal was to offer phenomenal personalization—letting women mix their own products online. I wouldn't dare guess whether it will be a success, but the effort shows that you've got to experiment with new formulas, even when they appear to run counter to your mainstream business. In the bricksand-mortar world, P&G, for instance, can scll only the top dozen or so shampoos. Reflect.com, meanwhile, will be able to offer tens of thousands of uniquc, individually customized products.

Not only is Procter & Gamble taking a big corporate risk, but, true to Reflect.com's personalization philosophy, the company has also put risk at the individual level. Procter & Gamble managers joining the Reflect.com team must actually quit their P&G positions and work "without a safety net." Each individual's success is tied to Reflect.com's performance.

Why should a company that dominates a market bother with risks? Plenty have preferred to stick to old ways and been caught flat-footed by nimble new competitors or novel technologies. Not Gillette. Having just spent over \$200 million creating its successful two-bladed Sensor razor, the company poured phenomenal resources into its new Mach 3. Seven years in the making, the new razor consumed an unheard-of \$750 million in development and manufacturing costs. Innovations included the extraordinarily thin, stronger-than-steel blade and, of course, the three-bladed system. Within months the Mach 3 was outselling Gillette's previous front-runner, the SensorExcel, by an incredible ten-to-one margin. The company's dollar share of the razor blade market topped 0 pernt, ts highest in nearly forty years. rety - gressive behavior for a company that could have just rested on its laurels for a few years. A skeptic might say that they cannibalized their own market share, but in the process they reestablished themselves as the industry leader and trendsetter.

My brother David hates rules. He hates them because he knows that when you start making rules, you sow the first seeds of bureaucracy.
